THE RED PAGE
Poems by E. J. Rupert Atkinson.
THE ENCOUNTER.
She from the wanton highway of delight,
He from the gloom ascetiG of his cell,
Looked each on each. She smiled; he heeded
veil
The proffered lure, and shuddered from his sight;
Then that his flesh be purged of fear his might
Strove forth to succor ner by prayer and spell;
Jesting she swore to gain him, and to quell
His virgin virtue, laughing in the night.
Upward she reached, he stooping down to meet
Her yearning face, with wistful eyes of care,
And side by side for them one day was sweet.
She rose ... he fell. . . she kissed the cross and he,
Her futile worshipper, in shrill despair
Flung to the skies his reeking blasphemy.
THE DEAD SAINT.
Through hurtling space he fled from stq,r to star
On pinions'of damnation, for there trailed
Upon him frantic shapes which clutching wailed,
A long black mass which mouthed each wound
and scar,
Their dry lips mumbling love with lust to mar
His vigor by their weakness ; and he quailed,
And headlong down the darkness flashed and
failed,
Held by their black mass weltering from afar.
He cried : “ They burden me and drain my blood,
And from their sated vampire-love eschew
Me victim, till they famish when they claim
Me saviour, lord of love !. . . This long-time flood
Reeks—whence they praise my glory and pursue
Me ever— .. . pity, Omy God, my shame ! ”
OBSEQUIES.
One moment may we love, and then comes death.
O lay us side by side ; our bodies steep
With stpck and "roses white. Strew wreath on
wreath,.
And usher in the, night, for here we sleep,
Here to the end of time, or till the earth
Is frozen into silence and despair,
Itself the ghastly tomb of love and mirth,
A shiftless spectre in the spinning air.
One moment’s life. ... One moment we are here,
And then we vanish. Lay us side by side !
To us who live no other end may bear
A surer hope ; for each tossed from the tide,
Where mortal life floods on Eternity,
Lies mute in death, and thus shall always lie.
THE ENIGMA.
Think you that she is holy, she who is
The echo of your love, the mirror fleet,
Reflecting; your own appetite, and meet,
Therefore, to be as vain a thing as this ?
You f*jin would find her sweeter than her kiss —
But all your hope is vain, because you seek
A thing” beyond the power of life to wreak,
For who shall deem her dumb devotion bliss ?.
What can you hope of her whose love and trust
Rely upon your greed and vanity ?
What shall you think of her who, to the gust
Of your desire, bends like a stricken tree ?
Wh”at shall be said of her who feeds your lust?
A thing more futile than yourself is she !
A New Australian Poet.
The appearance of a new poet is always a
troubling portent. How can the Critic,
charmed, perplexed, exasperated, finding so
much and yet finding so much lacking, be
sure that his enthusiasm will survive ? Has
this new star the permanence, the steady
glow, though indubitably there is life here,
chaotic, diffuse, insistent ? Is this flash in
our wide skies only a falling meteor, or the
emergence of a new sun ? For a new poet
makes a new appeal, his light discloses a
different spectrum, his brightness dazzles
with its strangeness. How place it in its
proper magnitude ? How decide whether it
has come to stay ?
Something of this dubiety afflicts the
critic in the consideration of E. J. Rupert
Atkinson (with portrait). Two years ago
Atkinson published a small volume of verse,
entitled The Shrine of Desire , which this
Page found chaotic and tumultuous, yet inter-
esting, a vagueness lit with beautiful lines,
a conglomerate of poetry and words. This
little book was disfigured by an extraordi-
nary preface, which led the critic to say that
if E. J. Rupert Atkinson was not youthful,
it was quite time he was. But the poetry
survived the preface.
After this slight essay came two years of
silence. And now in her sumptuous robe
comes the sumptuous muse of a more
mature Atkinson, By a Midnight Sea:
Poems (George Robertson, Melb.) ; and
from this bulky collection of diffuse verses, a
huge volume of high technical skill and
much thought, it is possible tentatively to
assign to the author his place in Australian
verse.
W
Considered in Jffls technical aspect, Atkin-
son discloses a complete command of metre
and rime. He works equally well in sonnet,
rondeau, rondel, triolet, villanelle, and
blank verse. The only thing he has not
learnt is the necessity for condensation. Yet
even his exuberant fecundity aids us
in estimating him as a writer that
cannot be overlooked. His bulk tells.
When confined by rigid stanza forms he can
be rigid and terse ; it is only in his looser
work that he allows himself to ramble on to
interminable diffuseness. Yet even his
loquacity is redeemed by thought and an
occasional sonorous line. But his appeal
would have been more immediate, his final
place more certain, had he bestowed upon
his poems the labor of the file that he has for
his individual lines. He suffers from the
exuberance of youth ; he has not learnt to
prune from his lush growth the branches
I and leaves that would the more certainly
disclose the blossoms. And thus Atkinson
is not eminently quotable. In nearly all
his verse there are specks of poetry to be
found ; but his work needs crushing to find
payable ore. And his vagueness and care-
lessness of writing bar him from the poetry-
lover who is not ready for painstaking fos-
sicking. Yet the good things in his work
are indubitably gold. And it must be
patiently sought—through 132 poems in a
tall volume of 268 pages.
vl/
Let us select a few specimens of his style :
I welcome either pang or feast,
I am content to stand alone ;
And though the night hang thick, at least
The turgid night is all mine own. . . .
Grief lingered till it seemed that grief was
trust. ...
He kissed her, and her tear-lit eyes
Were sweet again with budding mirth,
And her meek face was wonder-wise,
And clear the colored earth, . . ,
Contemptuous of universes, I
Survey all space devoid of fear or trust
With will defiant fearful to defy,
Awed by the vastness of a speck of dust. • • •
But O to see the morning sun
Burst over long green plains of grass,
To know, although the night is done,
That 1 am what I always was ! . . .
His vision reeled, and he beheld
Time motionless and death’s escape
The turgid source whence he had welled,
A bubble in another shape. . . .
Heap withered poppies on her grave ;
Weep not nor smile. No more I fear
Her frown —no more—though once her slave;
For she is lost in utter death . . .
She cannot hear.
O would that I were Death ! . . .
O
Your life, a note struck once to thought, may
sound
The same hereafter, from strange years unbound
One flash might waken What has been before :
Then you would find again what you have
found. . . .
I Did we not love, then God had no delight. . . ,
Away with all such thought
Of vaunted human wisdom : ’tis despair
And leads toward no end. . . . Love lingers yet;
As when the sky is fraught
With memory of sunset,
Which is the breaking of the dawn elsewhere..
Time goes on, time shall go by ine
Like a storm that leaves its spoil beside the
way . . .
My dream has fled ; the Truth remains—enough !
Nought can be evil: Life is infinite
And time, the willing minister of love,
With each breath brings
Some simple-beauty budding to the light,
And all can tell what every petal sings.
W
Atkinson’s work, as will be seen, is not
lacking in thought. He has, too, the
Wordsworthian endowment of simplicity of
diction. His philosophy is that of youth.
Love to him is nearly always lust, a woman
only the vessel of that lust, and death is an
ever-looming menace.
If God is mindless, and His state
But that of earth, then surely I
Can wake Him up to life, through hate,
By curse and blasphemy !
I And, living, He will understand
How he has made the world to weep,
And, by His might and His command,
Death like a storm shall sweep
Out of existence utterly ;
And we, poor creatures, who were swirled
One time in death, will ever be
The masters of the world !
And, as is to be expected, he delights in
passion. A characteristic example :
Midnight and all is ours. Come closer, sweet,
Closer until my heart can feel yours beat,
And, beating, our two hearts become the same.
Lean, sheltering boughs, above us lest there flame
Some vagrant meteor to illume ou,r love.
The wbrld is silent; darkness sleeps above.
Midnight and all is ours 1 Come closer yet,
Make our full moment ours ere we forget,
Ere time sardonic robs desire. Once more
All echoes back, all we have been before,
And I "who loved have conquered her disdain ;
Immortalised this moment shall remain.
Nought else to dream of save our love, beside
'The far-off murmur of the drowsy tide ;
Beneath, the fragrant turf, the soft warm air
Add sanctity to love. I have no care
Now first in all existence. Nearer yet,
Come closer, love, that we may here forget
The treacheries of time ; and for love’s sake
I pledge the future yours. With you I slake
My thirst of love ; I drug my soul with you.
The will is ours. This love we shall renew
Henceforth at will—but now no more of speech,
Your lips to mine—so let our silence teach
Our souls the way that mortal passion dower 3.
Lie still, O sweet. Midnight, the world is ours.
W
And, also as is to be expected, Woman
obsesses his muse. His view of her may
perhaps most concisely be expressed by these
selected lines, marred—as poetry—by the
concluding couplet:
Her eyes speak all the love he showed, and she,
Until she knows the thrill of his embrace,
The consummation of their love, with face
That strains to his, half deems her love is his,
Half deems her passion sanctified which is
Only the craving to fulfil her end.
Yet mother of man’s life, what may transcend
A higher state than thine? Lost on the surge
Of eddying human life, loud with the dirge
And volume of the medley of desires
Breathed from a myriad throats, which are as fires
Sweltering to death, mankind finds common cause
In one fixed will—more life; for they must pause,
Themselves, in utter death. Creator, she
I Woman, smiles over him, her potency
Holding the farrowing bounds of earth. Her love
But yearns for his, that she may fashion of
Herself a lasting heritage of life.
Look up ! The earth is bloody with her strife. . .
Love consummated vvanes to death straightway,
Consumed within itself ; and they but stay
To hearken to their memory of delight
And drift toward their ends. He to the might
Of ever-changing thoughts and knowledge tied,
Unconscious, works his mission—to abide
Her slave and panderer. And she, the slave
Of earth’s one law, toils onward to the grave,
Perchance a ten-times mother. . . And she dies :
Even in death her body multiplies !
So in this big book he reiterates with
scarce a variation the theme of love, of love’s
satiety, of the woman worshipped and her
greed when won. He will grow out of that.
He has imagination and the audacity to
envisage vast conceptions ; and if his book
is by no means perfect, he has put forth a
volume arresting, troubling, big with the
uncertainty of youth. But he has yet much
loving labor with the file to do.
Gm B. Lancaster
G. p. Lancaster improves. The latest
novel by this Maoriland girl, The Altar
Stairs (per Roycroft Library, Sydney) marks
an increase in technical power, while her
masculine strength remains as astonishing as
in A Spur to Smite. Here is a story of the
Islands, wild, fierce, realistic, packed with
gorgeous incident, and working logically out
the redemption of an Island outcast. Lan-
caster has the Kipling instinct of vivid por-
traiture, the Kipling gift of virile phrase,
the Kipling power of creating an atmosphere.
The plot is well welded, weakening at the end
by a convenient death. As a specimen of
her power of condensation to the essentials,
take this bit of by-play between the white
man and the brown girl:
“Then, through the ruled lines of the
casuarina fingers, Tony Heritage saw her,
and he thrust them apart, catching, her by
the shoulder. Her song snapped with a half
scream. She twisted free, sprang sideways
on a rock shining brown as her skin, and
looked at him aslant in a mocking defiance.
The night wind swept up the heavy hair
from her oval, laughing face and shook the
strings of scarlet berries that fell to her
waist. There was strange scent in her petti-
coat of fresh-pulled leaves, and in her wind-
stirred hair, and the flutter of a bat past her
slim, bronze body was no lighter than her
poise on the stone.
“Tony came three steps nearer, . . ..
“ ‘You no Solomon girl,’ he said quickly.
“ Parate swayed a little, her hands on her
sides; bright eyes and teeth flashing through
the dusk of her hair.
“ ‘ What you t’ink 1 What you t’ink, you
one white man ? ’ she cooed.
“Tony Heritage laughed. Then he put
his hands under her arms and lifted her.
“ ‘ I think this,’ he said, and met her lips
with his,”
Lancaster is at home in a “scrap, and
the yarn is full of wild Island fighting and the
contests of two desperate men. But the
greater fight is a psychological one, between
Rod’s better and worse helf. And each of
the characters is clearly drawn, with a cer-
tain crude strength, careless of nuances. Of
living writers her work most resembles
London and S. E. White. And it is good
work. ■ ■ '
Steele Rudd.
I Steele Rudd is at it again with In Australia
(Pratten Bros., 247 George-street, Sydney,
Is.) This is a story of a South Queensland
dairy farm, readable, picturesque, Australian.
Davies paints with a sure hand the hard con-
ditions of boy-labor on a dairy farm, and if
the book only kept up to the realism of the
opening chapters it would be a noteworthy
work. °Here, for instance, is his picture of
the children of the dairy-farms getting in the
cows by lantern light:
“And on the lightly timbered fiats and
ridges at that same merciless hour, eerily
flashed distant lanterns, like so many will-
o’-tbe wisps, each lantern accounting for a
child missing from its bed—perhaps a girl of
tender years, sleepy, hungry, weary, and
thinly clad ! Had they been tutored m some
form of flashlight telegraphy the mites might,
at least, have spent the first hour of then-
morning’s misery pleasantly enough in com-
municating their sufferings and conveying
messages ©f sympathy to each other across
the dark and silent valleys, which Nature, in
her wisdom, had not yet deemed fit to awaken
from a healthy slumber.
But this touch of truth is soon overlaid
with echoes of the Steele Rudd humor, worn
thin and a conventional plot which does not
omit the orphan child, the bush-trained
racehorse that wins the Cup, the imprison-
ment of the innocent man, and the miss-
will. And even in his description of the
race Rudd uses the cheapest and tntest
journalese.
1“ The hearts of the silent multitude beat
th suppressed excitement. 'She suspense
is awful. Every second seemed an hour.
du could hear a pin drop.”
And the conclusion is the cheapest and
ost unconvincing melodrama. It is a
ty that, with his gifts of realism, Rudd
>es not do better. He has a fatal facility
id a lack of care which spoil his art.
oubtless the public will like it all the
ore.

The Hunter of the Years ( J. D. Aitken,

Audacious Paris has solved the universal
poet-problem. That city has married the
irts by installing a “ Salon of Poetry” in
ane of the r&oms of the Art Salon of the
Champs Elysees. The new stall of Pegasus
was opened with formalities by no less a
personage than the Minister of Public In-
struction. Every poet in I ranee can send
in his poem to the Salon, and all those poets
who are not dead have done so. A hard-
working committee sifts the gold jfom the
dross, and once a wdek a dozen of the best
verses are read to the public by trained
readers, or the blushful authors themselves.
If he only knew French, the part of poem-
reader seems just suited to Arthur Bayldon.
Honors are conferred upon the best poets,
and many of the productions appear in the
daily press as news of the day. The Pegasus
stall has been so successful that the
Independent Salon and the Autumn Salon
have installed stables in their galleries for
that happy moke. The suggestion that the
Society of Artists and the Art Society should
go and do likewise would be received in
Australia with delirious joy.
More for its geography than for its
merit, though that is high, we print
the concluding verse of Mrs. V. A. Litch-
field’s “ Australaise,” which arrived too
late for the competition. The writer states
that in the Northern Territory, where she
lives, she never gets The Bulletin until at
least four weeks after it is published, and
then has to wait for a boat to call at Port
Darwin to take her letters south.
Comrades, though to-morrow’s sun
Find us lying low. 1
And the joy of battles won
We no more may know,
I It will be a glorious dying,
On a couch of crimson lying,
Still to see our banners flying
O’er a beaten foe.
A correspondent asks for information re “ The
Austral Literary Agency, ” 317 Coll ins-street,
Melb. The agency charges £5 ss. for reading and
revising ready for press MS. to the length of
80,000 words, and for forwarding and submitting
to publishers £1 Is. If placed, the agency wants
10 per cent, of price. Short stones are read and
revised up to 1500 words for 55., and for every
additional thousand words 2s. Gd. So far this new
agency reports only one sale of a book.
prices may be compared with The Editor
Literary Bureau,” 150 Nassau-street, New York,
U.S. Criticism of prose, 5000 words Bs. (id. ; novels
to 30,000 words 315., or to 100,000 words £5 ss.
The Editor has a good record of American sales,
and claims to revise and criticise with expert
thoroughness. The Red Page offers no advice on
the subject. '

Debility, Indigestion
and Palpitation.
A COMPLETE CURE.
By perusing the following story, the reader will
be made acquainted with circumstances that pos-
sibly bear a personal resemblance, in which event
gratefulness would be felt towards Mrs. Josephine
Mack, of No. 21 Parsons Street, Balmain, for
placing on record her remarkable experience.
Answering the reporter’s first question Mrs.
Mack said :
“As a sufferer from debility and indigestion, I
ought to be able to speak with authority on the
subject of what is the most effective remedy for
such afflictions, and the knowledge I gained in this
respect came through following the example of a
relative, who had been suffering like myself, and
who was quite restored to health by Clements
Tonic.”
“Had you been ailing long, Mrs. Mack?”
“Yes, too long for my liking, and a weary time
it was, too. I had tried many alleged remedies,
and only wasted my money in doing so. Need-
less to say how despondent I was as a result. I
had no strength to get about, and the easiest work
in the house was often too much for me. Yes,
anybody around here can tell you what I was like
then, for I have been in this house for the past
eleven years and am pretty well known. Ah !
little did I think of the misery that was in store
for me when I was a healthy, young woman,
teaching at a school in Tipperary. But we never
know what is before us. Why, my life became a
positive burden to me. I was subject to reeling
sensations. I would feel myself swaying to and
fro, and it required a big effort to save myself from
falling to the floor. The severest form of head-
aches attacked me, and I was terribly nervous,
too, and when nervousness hinges on to a woman
her unhappiness is complete. The terrifying
thoughts that come to ihe brain, such as strange
sounds being present, making the belief that
somebody is stealthily moving about the room, or
a window being raised in the dead of night, are
enough to distract a person entirely. And in the
fretful state of mind then ruling every little
trouble was magnified into an impassable mountain
of adversity. Very often I would go and lie down,
quite unable to keep up any longer, and I don’t
know what would have happened only for the
Clements Tonic that I fortunately started taking
in time.”
“ Did your illness entail any difference in your
weight?”
“My word, it did; but it was astonishing how
soon it came back once I had got properly going
with Clements Tonic, which seemed to make the re-
moval of my afflictions quite easy. Before this, I
hadn’t any prospect of getting my strength back,
for I could not eat, and a person cannot do much
good without an appetite. But the trouble was
that when I did force myself to eat, I suffered
fearfully from indigestion—heavy sensations in
the chest, such as made the very act of breathing
a difficulty, and pains under my shoulders, across
my back, and aches in my limbs chat were most
wearisome. My heart palpitated dreadfully, and
there were pains in the region of that organ, too.
So you see, I was altogether in a bad way. But,
thank God, there is a' very marked difference in
my physical condition between now and then, and
I have to give all the credit to the remedy I’ve
already mentioned. The derangements of my
nervous system, that had obstinately remained in
existence until Clements Tonic was brought into
requisition, do not trouble me now, neither do any
of the disorders that had proved so wearisome.
By judiciously keeping to my newly-won friend,
Clements Tonic, I succeeded in regaining the
priceless gift of good, robust health, and it is only
fair that I should give to that remedy all the
credit that is due to it. As a nerve tonic, to
brace a woman up, and make her feel invulner-
able against the hardest work and the severest
trials that happen during one’s course through
life; I recommend Clements Tonic. It not only
cured my indigestion, but also supplied in unstint-
ed measure nerve force and strength, which gives
a woman confidence in herself, and to the weak
and sickly I shall always advise the use of that
grand preparation.’’
“ Your views will be duly chronicled.”
“Very well, you are at liberty to publish them
where and how you think best for the benefit of the
whole community.”
STATUTORY DECLARATION.
I, JOSEPHINE MACK, of 21 Parsons Street, Balmain,
in the State of New South Wales, ComnVnwealth of
Australia, do solemnly and sincerely declare that I have
carefully read the annexed document, and that it contains
and is a true and faithful account of my illness and cure
by Clements Tonic, and ajgo contains my full permission
to publish in any way my statements—which I give
voluntarily, without receiving any payment; and I make
this solemn declaration conscientiously believing the same
to be true, and by virtue of the provisions of the Oaths
Act, 1900.
Subscribed and declared at Balmain, this twenty-fourth
day of March, one thousand nine hundred and eight,
before me, _ „

VARIOUS VERSE
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Finis.
High-hearted was he as the lark,
Whose song of triumph spurns the dark.
The scarlet music in his veins
Ran glowing like seraphic strains.
The dawn appeared for him alone.
The day, the world, were all his own.
For him the sun kissed lovingly
His silken-robed Sultana Sea.
The hours to him were golden sands :
He scattered them with both his hands.
The thrill of ecstasy that runs
Through song of lark, through light of suns,
Through grasp of hand in comradeship,
Through kiss of woman—heart on lip—
He knew and loved it passing well.
Then on his soul a Shadow fell—
The Shadow of a Shape of Fear,
That made the golden daylight drear.
Its hollow eyes were dim and dull;
A fool’s cap crowned its grinning skull.
It stood a moment by his side,
He looked, and laughed—and laughing, died.
Y. J. DALEY.
Dolorous Gard.
Not here, not here, but under skies,
Touched with a deeper, tenderer blue,
And changeful as a sea-maid’s eyes,
I built in dreams a home for you.
Far set in lonely lands of thought,
A fairy tower, serenely light
As some tall flower in fields unsought
That trembles to the starry night.
All down the silent galleries
My steps re-echo as I pass.
The sunlight falls in traceries
Of gold and crimson through the glass
Of deep-set windows, richly lined
With many an old armorial shield,
Whose lions waded deep in blood
On some old dim forgotten field.
The heavy tapestries are still:
No hasty air disturbs their folds;
The jasmine twines about the sill,
The nesting sparrow fearless scolds.
No voice rings through the startled halls,
No shadow falls across the door ;
The pallid mirrors on the walls
No sudden image brightens o’er.
And sometimes stealing, half-afraid,
At even, through the closing gloom,
I find, not hitherto essayed,
A little, moonlit, empty room.
Here lies a book, the leaves still shut,
Here languishes, untuned and dumb,
A lute ; some blossoms, long since cut,
Lie dead, or e’er the Queen should come.
And there are gardens, never sere,
Green-alleyed, faint with rose and musk,
That bloom for ever through the year.
The fireflies flicker through the dusk,
Wheeling their mazy dance aloft.
Where one tall poplar rears above
The fountain, singing sweet and soft,
Like music heard in dreams of love.
Sometimes I watch the peacocks strut
Where the old dial, throned in flowers,
Displays his motto, quaintly cut,
“I number only sunny hours.”
Till by the shadows made aware
Another day its hours has spanned,
I seek the winding turret-stair,
And sit, my chin upon my hand,
To watch the summer moon uprise,
Large, red ; even like that fruit of Death
Which laid Troy low for Helen’s eyes,
And fair, false mouth, and amorous breath.
And through the silence, pauseful, clear,
Like one who weepeth all alone
By night in some still house, I hear
The sea’s old mournful monotone.
Till, deep within the cloven skies
A pallor slowly spreads and grows ;
And on the heights the white mists rise,
And all the earth is flushed with rose.
The stars die slowly, one by one,
And, shrill across the dewy lawn,
To herald in the day begun.
Peals to my ear the bird of dawn.
Ah, stately palace ! lovely flowers !
From the wild world so far apart!
A sadness is upon your bowers,
And finds its echo in your heart.
How many months of sun and rain
Must fret the grey and lichened stone
Before my Queen to her Domain
Return at length to claim her own ?
(For far from here, and under skies
Touched with a deeper, tenderer blue
Than dwells within a sea-maid’s eyes,
I built in dreams a home for you.
Ah, come, my sweet ! not all in vain
Let me aWait you here alone,
lake up, my Queen, your rightful reign,
And come at length to claim your own !)

THURSDAY, DECEMBER 24, 1908.
THE NORTHERN TERRITORY-STILL
AUSTRALIA’S DANGER.
Parliament did a comparatively short session in
1908, and the number of Acts it passed in that
session can be counted on the fingers of one
hand. It closed early, because under the ridicu-
lous system of Party Government a Ministry
doesn’t hold office for a fixed period, but is liable,
so long as Parliament is sitting, to be thrown out
over any Bill, or clause, or motion at any moment,
and thereby lose its dignity and income without
notice. Consequently, it pays better to abandon
business and close Parliament up, and get into
the cool, peaceful grotto of recess, where Minis-
tries are safe from being thrown out. Also, it
pays to stay in recess as long as possible, and let
business slide. This arrangement is good for the
Ministry, but very bad for a country where so
many things are waiting to be done. To quote only
one—the Northern Territory question is hung up
again, whenadefinite settlement was apparently in
sight; and how long it may be before it reappears
as a business proposition no man can tell. It is a
desperately urgent matter, but the necessity for
getting into recess is the most urgent matter of
all under the Party Government system, especially
when the Ministry is weak and its following pre-
carious.
. * * * *
There is nothing to which any reasonable per-
son can object in the terms on which the Terri-
tory is offered to the Commonwealth. In the first
place its debt (some £3,390,000) is to be taken
over with it. There are probably £1,500,000
worth of money-earning assets to set against this
debt, and even if they are not earning much just
now they will earn a great deal when the Lost
Province is in the hands of a Government which
can find the funds to complete its railway com-
munication and start settlement on a large scale.
That leaves about £1,800,000 as an equivalent for
the transfer of 523,620 square miles of country
described thus:
(1) 158,009 square miles erood tropical, pastoral and
agricultural country ; rainfall from I9in. to 64in. per
annum ; contains metals and probably coal.
(2) 34,000 square miles of good pastoral country, with
patches of rich volcanic soil ; well grassed and watered ;
with wide plains and rich river flats.
(3) 29,000 square miles, semi-tropical; first-class
pastoral country ; part fit for agriculture ; well-grassed
and with rich soil. Not so well-watered as Nos. 1 and 2,
but subject to intermittent tropical rains.
(4) 148,000 square miles: part good pastoral country ;
part metal-bearing ; portions sand and spinifex ; uncertain
rainfall.
(5) About 154,000 square miles ; part metal-bearing;
patches of inferior pastoral country ; rest scrub and sand ;
uncertain rainfall.
With 221,000 square miles of good and well-
watered land and 302,000 square miles of
sundries going for about £1,800,000, the
Northern Territory is the cheapest province on
earth. No auctioneer in history has ever put a
better lot on the market since the days when the
Pretorians used to sell the Roman Empire by
public competition. And it must be remembered
that South Australia doesn’t ask its neighbors to
shoulder the whole responsibility. As a State
which stands for one-tenth of the Commonwealth’s
population it will still carry one-tenth of the
burden. Out of the debt of £3,300,000 it will
shoulder £330,000, which is at least something.
South Australia, in fact, offers to sell a magnificent
fortune for an old song—or rather, it offers to give
away the fortune, for the Territory is simply to be
handed over with nothing save the Territory’s
own obligations attached. S. Australia does this
because it can’t raise the money to build a line
that will open up the Territory and make the
magmficentfortune realisable. It is in the position
of the impoverished person who knows for certain
of a great deposit of diamonds somewhere on the
head waters of the Amazon, and can’t even raise
the passage-money wherewith to go and collect
them.
» * •
The rest of the conditions which the S. A.
Parliament attaches to the transfer are the pur-
chase, by the Commonwealth, of the unfinished
transcontinental line from Port Darwin to Ood-
nadatta (S, Australia, as the State which repre-
sents one-tenth of the population, finding one-
tenth of the money), and the completion of the
line (S.A. again finding its share of the money).
These terms are also eminently reasonable. A
transcontinental railway must be built to connect
Port Darwin with the settled part of Australia,
else the Territory will remain valueless. At the
same time, it is folly to build and pay for and
maintain and work a long line when a short one
will suffice, and the Port Darwin-Port Augusta
line is the short line par excellence, as the follow-
ing measurements —taken as the crow flies—will
show :
Port Darwin to Melbourne .. 1988 miles.
„ „ Sydney .. 1960 „
~ ~ Newcastle .. 1954 ~
~ ~ Brisbane .. 1784 „
„ ~ Albany .. 1679 ~
~ ~ Adelaide .. 1659 ~
~ ~ Fremantle .. 1568 ~
„ ~ Rockhampton.. 1505 ~
~ ~ Port Augusta .. 1472 ~
Furthermore, (1) the short line is partly built
already, and though it will need improvement to
fit it for carrying express traffic, still a vast
amount of the work is done. (2) On this route
the Commonwealth will own every foot of the
railway from sea to sea, and it is stipulated
that it may arrange to acquire Port Augusta, and
thus own the two termini also. The advantage
of this position as compared with any scheme
whereby the transcontinental railway would be
partly a State and partly a Federal concern is
obvious. (3) This line will cross the whole Terri-
tory from north to south, and open up the
Commonwealth’s 523,620 square miles of property
as much as any single railway can do, whereas
the advocates of rival routes propose a line through
Western Queensland, which will only traverse
the coastal fringe of the Territory and do the
smallest possible amount of opening-up so far as
that country is concerned. (4) In every scheme
of a real transcontinental railway there is the so-
called desert to be crossed, but this scheme
tackles the desert at almost its narrowest part
and makes about the quickest possible time across
it. These are solid and material reasons in favor
of the straight Port Darwin-Port Augusta route.
A political reason is that the terms proposed are
the best that the Price Government was able
to wrest from its State Legislature. It had to
struggle with people who thought the proposed
offer too lavish ; with people who still thought
S.A. could borrow the money wherewith to finish
the railway and open up the Territory, whereby
it might keep the profits of the enterprise for
itself ; with anti-Federalists who hated the idea
of the Commonwealth acquiring this vast pro-
perty on any terms whatever ; with nigger-labor
folk who would rather see the Territory handed
back to England and made an appanage of the
Government at Calcutta, and flooded with our
cheap Brown Brother; and with people who
opposed out of shber cussedness. And if the Aus-
tralian Parliament doesn’t grasp the Territory
with both hands while this eminently favorable
offer is available, it is quite possible that, in the
chapter of accidents, the chance may never come
again.
Why, then, it may be asked, did the new
Government skedaddle into recess without an
attempt to secure the ratification of the agreement
which the Deakin Ministry fixed up with S.A.
over this matter? It is a desperately urgent
matter. When N.S.W. abandoned the Territory
in the early 60’s it lapsed back into the condition
of a British Crown colony. It is one to this day.
There is a pledge that it will be ceded to S.A.
when the transcontinental railway is finished, but
that railway isn’t finished, nor does S.A. alone
seem able to raise the money for the work. There
is also a promise that it will become definitely a
part of Australia when the Commonwealth takes
it over; but the lagging Commonwealth doesn’t
take it over. Therefore it is still a British Crown
colony. The Federal tariff and the White Aus-
tralia policy are only there on sufferance. Any
day—this very evening, perhaps—news may
arrive that the British Government has, by an
Order-in-Council, cancelled the arrangement
whereby the Lost Province is tentatively adminis-
tered from Adelaide; and has resumed possession.
Then, whether it attaches the Territory to the
dominions of the Viceroy at Calcutta or sends out
an autocrat of its own, the unity of Australia is
gone and White Australia has disappeared for
ever. Already Britain’s Japanese ally is reported
to have pointed out to Bull over and over again
that the Aliens Restriction Act doesn’t really
apply to the Territory; that it is Bull’s own
523,000 square miles of country ; that it is really
Bull who is keeping his Japanese and Chinese
and Hindu friends out of this empty and desirable
land ; and that the act is an unfriendly one on
Bull’s part. Similar influencesarealmost certainly
being brought to bear from India; and are certainly
being brought to bear in England by the diseased
philanthropists who love “our cullud brother,”
and send out missionaries to rouse him from his
heathen repose. If Bull one day yields to this
or some similar pressure, what can Australia say
by way of complaint ? Bull doesn’t profess any
special sympathy with our White Australia
ideas, but he is apparently quite willing that we
should enforce them in our own domains. He has
told Australia distinctly that, by a stroke of the
pen, it may legally acquire 523,000 square miles
of country which now belong to Bull, and may
make the White Australia policy law throughout
that great region. If Australia won’t trouble to
take what is offered it, and if one day,-,, perhaps
before Parliament meets again, Bt i. can make
an Asiatic friend or pacify an Asiatic enemy by
admitting his colored subjects freely to the
Territory, why should he not do it? His act
would be perfectly legal, and as Australia
was too tired to definitely extend its White
Australia law to the Territory in the sim-
ple method that was open to it, he will have
no reason to suppose that even Australia will
object very much. A country that takes so little
trouble can’t be supposed to be strong on ideals.
Yet with all these reasons for haste the Fisher
Government (like the ones which preceded it) has
drifted into recess and left this great question
lying about loose.
* * • *
Apparently the reason is that the curse of State
selfishness has broken out again as it did over the
Federal capital. There are mean economists
around who propose that the Commonwealth
should take over the Territory but only assume
part of its debt, and leave South Australia to
carry the balance; also, of course, its share
(about one-tenth) of the proportion taken over.
This is in addition to the fact that South Aus-
tralia must shoulder, in any case, the accumulated
losses on the bit of unfinished transcontinental
line (Port Augusta to Oodnadatta) which lies
within the limits of its own State, and these
losses, by reason of the fact that the line is an
unfinished one which ends nowhere, have been
very heavy. This is a poor reward for the State
which, during 45 weary years, has held the fort
against wholesale Asiatic immigration, and made
stupendous losses in the process, but the proposal is
made in all seriousness. Then a Queensland crowd
has devised a scheme whereby a large section of
the line already built is to be abandoned, and the
transcontinental railway is to diverge at Hergott
Springs, wander through Western Queensland, and
only enter the Territory near the north coast.
Immediately on top of this came a scheme whereby
the transcontinental railway is to start at Port
Darwin, run straight into western Queensland
(connecting with Brisbane), go on through western
N.S.W. (connecting with Sydney), and finish at
Melbourne. This idea, which is a cynical appeal
to the greed and selfishness of the three great
eastern States, would produce these results : (1) It
wouldn’t open up the Territory after all, because
the railway would take the shortest cut out of the
Territory, and the Commonwealth’s new pos-
session would thus be greatly depreciated ; (2) the
line would be vastly longer than that to Port
Augusta; (3) it would be under divided control,
being partly State and partly Federal; (4) there
would be no chance of the Commonwealth owning
the two termini of the great railway ; (5) the
proposed arrangement would make the large
southern section of the line that is already built a
mere bad debt, and an abandoned streak of old
iron ; (6) it would be a reversion to the old nefari-
ous policy of .trying to drag all population to
where population is already thickest; and (7) as
it would be directly contrary to the agreement
already fixed up, the whole negotiation would
have to be begun afresh. And if the new negoti-
ation failed, the Territory might lapse back to
Britain and become a nigger-infested appanage of
India, after all.
* * * *
But with all these conflicting conspiracies in
view, it was quite obvious that if a weak Govern-
ment, with a weak and precarious following
behind it, tried to deal with the Northern Terri-
tory question, it was bound to be wrecked some-
where. An elected Ministry with a fixed tenure
of office might have fought the question ; a mere
Party Government Ministry, liable to be sacked
at a moment’s notice, couldn’t do so unless it had
a strong following, or a large, patriotic reckless-
ness about its job. So the matter is hung up
indefinitely, and by the time Parliament meets
again the Territory may have resumed its old
status as a British Crown colony, and nigger
immigration may have commenced on a large
scale, and White Australia may be a vanished
dream.
THE CURSE OF BENT AND THE MISERY
OF HAYING SWINBURNE ON THE
PREMISES.
The Tory elements in Victorian politics have
come together again. A week or two ago Tommy
Bent was defeated by 37 votes to 25, because even
the Tories whose work he did had tired of his
vulgarity and treachery and incapacity. By try-
ing to please everybody, by throwing overboard
every item of his policy as soon as opposition
appeared on the horizon, by sacrificing his friends
when he thought it would pay and crawling to his
enemies when he fancied it would be remunera-
tive, by promising everything to everybody“who
asked for it, by an open, frank, brutal acknow-
ledgment of his belief in bribery and corruption
(“he voted against me and I made him a member
of the Blankport Harbor Board, too, ” was his per-
petual wail when things went wrong), and
by his horrible comic ditties and his in-
credible song-and-dance proceedings, Tommy of
the Cabbages dissipated, in 17 months, about the
biggest majority that had ever followed a Vic-
torian Premier. But the men who repented have
thought better of it. Instead of resigning, Bent
persuaded a fresh amateur Governor to grant him
a dissolution, and then he projected his vast
waistcoat into the orbit of events. Exactly how
much loam he ate in the process of the negotia-
tion will probably never be known, but at all
events he and the holy gasman, Swinburne, and
A. Peacock of the amazing laugh, and the rest of
the old elements have decided to go to the country
as a solid party once more. When the elections
are over, the party is to meet and decide whether
it can stand Bent any longer, but Bent will
doubtless have greased many wheels by that
time, and much muddy water will have flowed
under the bridge. Therefore the anti-BKNT
elements are now reduced to the Labor party led
by Prendergast, and Murray and his few fol-
lowers. They have decided to sta,nd no more Bent
at any price.
* • • *
And over this result the Melbourne Argus and
the S. M. Herald are jubilant. This was the
Herald’s comment on the situation :
It is wholly satisfactory to find this morning that Mr.
Swinburne is on the right side, and that the Government
goes to the polls with a practically unanimous party.
Doubtless much of the difficulty with the party lies with
the Premier. If he were out of the way friction would
disappear, and the personal attacks which now tend to
obscure the main issue would no longer hamper the
conduct of leading men. But Mr. Bent is very much in
the way. He has an unrivalled knowledge of the game,
and is too old a campaigner to t hrow any of his advan-
tages as a gift to rivals or critics. Mis astuteness in
obtaining a dissolution shows the sort of leader he is, and
for the present a leader he must remain. Mr. Swinburne
has now left the personal question entirely outof account,
lie must take action, as has already been indicated, on
the main issue—the true interests of Victoria ; and, how-
ever objectionable the Premier’s tactics may have been,
it is clear that a Socialist victory will be little short of a
calamity for the southern State. Both Queensland and
Victoria are in the same boat in this respect. Neither
can afford at the present juncture to destroy the confi-
dence which has grown as the result of State administra-
tion and a sound policy ; and to let the extremists to
power would throw both States back a generation.
Capital would quickly disappear, whatever the Socialists
may think, confidence would be lost, employment would
shrink, emigration and not immigration would become
the order of the day ; and before the inevitable period of
reconstruction arrived much misery to individuals and
loss of Stage prestige would be caused.
“ The extremists ” in this case—the men whose ac-
cession to power is to smash things generally —are
personsof theclass to which belong Premier Price of
S. A., J. C. Watson, Fisher (now Prime Minister)
and Kidston of Queensland, who was also a Labor
leader at one time. Even the S. M. Herald
wouldn’t dare to say that these men have gone
around smashing things promiscuously, but it
works off the old gags about “socialists” and
“extremists” and the disappearance of capital
because the ancient lie has become a habit.
* * * •
So Tommy Bent goes to the country as the
representative of “the true interests of Victoria.”
Tommy represents the party which has held the
reins in Victoria for the past 30 years or there-
abouts. He was a land-boomer of the old boom
type, and it was under the regime of his friends
and his party that Victoria made the heroic
smash of the early ’9o’s. That smash arose
through ignorant and bumptious Tory louts
playing at finance after the manner of children
playing with fire. The Commercial Bank of
Australia lost something approaching £4,000,000 —
probably the biggest sum ever lost by a bank
without the institution doing a total disappear-
ance. The Federal Bank vanished. The Mer-
cantile Bank vanished. The Real Estate Bank
vanished. A dozen land banks, founded by
Bent’s friends, vanished. The Standard Bank,
the British Bank of Australasia, the Land
Credit Bank, and Heaven only knows how many
more vanished in the confusion. Bent himself
vanished for a while, and “went on the land.”
Such institutions as the Australian Deposit and
Mortgage Bank and the Land Mortgage Bank of
Victoria still survive, but they have never made
good the awful losses which arose through Bent’s
friends fooling with financial matters of which
they knew nothing. The brewing interest of
Melbourne hasn’t got over the smash to this day.
In the awful period when the old Bent crowd was
running things it was impossible to throw a
half-brick in Collins-street without hitting a
broken building society which had gone to
wreck under the management of some fine old
Tory of the party whereof Bent is now the boss
representative. The assessed value of property
in Melbourne and suburbs dropped by £24,000,000,
and the flight of capital from the State was some-
thing incredible. With these events still un-
forgotten, the assurance of the Tory press that
the only way to prevent capital flying from
Victoria is to keep Bent and his kind in office is
something too magnificent for words.
• • » *
Tommy Bent, as already mentioned, represents
the party which has held office in Victoria for the
last 30 years or more. And Victoria is urged to
return Bent and his crew to office, because, if
they are not returned, people will leave the
country, and “emigration and not immigration
will become the order of the day.” Well, the
following table, taken from the official statistics,
represents the condition of Victoria in this
respect during the period when, not the Laborites,
but the party which Bent represents, has been at
the head of affairs :
Excess of Departures over Arrivals in Victoria.
1892 .. 11,058 1900 .. 7,828
1893 .. 12.484 1901 .. 1,428
1894 .. 12,698 1902 .. 13,716
1895 .. 14,410 1903 .. 16,570
1896 .. 22,134 1904 .. 13,920
1897 .. 13,754 1905 .. 7,164
1898 .. 11,127 1906 ..
1899 .. 8,020
That is a ghastly enough record for a rich young
country which has a population of only 14 persons
to the square mile. And the Bent faction, with
its Conservative land policy and its craving to
preserve the big estates, has been in power all
the time.
« * * 9
As was mentioned in last week’s issue, Victoria
struck one blessing in its lamentable career. It
got Protection, and Protection kept Melbourne
going strongly—Melbourne where the big factories
and the big Trades Unions and the chief
Labor influences are —and because Melbourne was
very much alive the State couldn’t be wholly dead.
It is no credit to Bent and the crowd to which he
belonged—the old Duncan Gillies push—that
this blessing came to pass, for Bent is a Foreign-
trader of the most awful kind, and Duncan
Gillies was a similar iniquity ; but the blessing
came in spite of them. And when Federation
arrived it confirmed and expanded the blessing
of Protection in spite of all that Bent and his
admirers could do. But so far the Australian
Government has not interfered with the land
question, and the state of the Victorian country
districts is a dreadful commentary on the
incapacity of Bent and his friends. There are 37
counties in Victoria, and when the records of the
last four censuses (1871, 1881, 1891 and 1901) are
compared, some very remarkable results make
themselves apparent:—
Present Population of 13 Victorian Counties Compared
with Largest Population.
1871. 1901. Decrease.
Talbot .. .. 84,762 57,398 27,364
Grenville .. 60,917 48,146 12,771
Dalhousie .. 26,471 20,378 6,093
Grant .. .. 73,828 71,034 2,794
Wonnangatta .. 3,972 2,271 1,701
Ripon .. .. 14,010 12,689 1,321
1881.
Gladstone .. 19,995 17,953 2,042
Kara Kara .. 16.515 16,431 84
1891.
Loxvan .. .. 16,820 14,190 2,630
Gunbower .. 8,706 8,167 539
Millewa .. .. 172 112 60
Eollett .. .. 2,749 2,715 34
Rodney.. .. 19,155 19,140 15
It is a record which calls for a lot of deep thought.
The Labor party has never been in power or in
sight of power in Victoria. Almost ever since
the place was a State it has been run by Bent or
Bent’s friends or people of Bent’s way of think-
ing. These people have managed to depopulate,
or partially depopulate, 13 out of 37 counties, all
located in the most fertile part of the State. Yet
they offer their own unsupported assurance that
the only way to prevent the hurried departure of
population is to return Bent and his followers and
the holy gas-man Swinburne to office again.
* * * *
Between 1901 and 1907 the population of Vic-
toria was estimated to have increased by 56,799.
But 47,777 of that increase was in Melbourne,
Bendigo, Geelong, Castlemaine, Warrnambool,
and Maryborough, and was owing to protected
manufactures—and Bent, the Foreign-trader, is
distinctly hostile to protected manufactures. The
agricultural towns and the great agricultural and
pastoral areas, which are cursed by the Tory land
policy of Bent and his friends, showed an increase
of only 9022 people—about 1500 per annum, and


even that was in the small towns ; the purely agri-
cultural areas showed, so far as the evidence goes,
an absolute decline. And even the humble
increase of 9022 people appears to have
been mostly an increase of aged and super-
annuated folk who were left behind in
the rush to get away from the curse of Bent and
his friends and his policy ; for Victoria is in the
amazing position of having 66,000 people qualified
for the old-age pension, while N.S.W., with a
much larger population, can only raise a moderate
47,000. Victoria is a rich State—one of the
richest on earth. But the traditional Bent policy
has been to concentrate the wealth in the hands
of a few ; and that policy is making Victoria a
land of old men, old women, unmarried young
women, children (not too many children), huge
landlords (often away playing the absentee, and
often persons of strange morals and remark-
able records), huge bank balances, and
one huge city. In the matter of men
Victoria had 609,546 in 1901, and in 1904
it had 605.038. It is a sad record—this record of
the results produced by Bent and his friends.
♦ * » #
The policy of the party which, under one de-
signation and another, has held office in Victoria
for very many years, has been an unwholesome
course of bribery. Taking the country districts
first, they have been bribed (1) by non-paying
railways; (2) by absurdly low freights ; (3) by
exemption from any save a nominal land-
tax—so nominal and so badly administered
that the yield from it is steadily de-
creasing ; (4) by being permitted to so
falsify their valuations that £IOOO worth of land
may be valued for rating purposes at £250, and
the alleged shilling rate thus becomes a mere
threepenny rate; (5) by huge grants from the
Treasury in aid of roads and bridges, -in order
to make good the loss caused by the under-
valuation swindle ; (6) by being allowed
to brazenly repudiate their water supply
and irrigation liabilities; (7) by lavish distribu-
tions of loan moneys; (8) by other devices too
numerous to mention. All these things, however,
tended to throw an absurdly huge load of taxation
on the city man, so he was bribed in his turn.
The bribe in his case was an artificial and ridicu-
lous arrangement of the railway system, devised
so as to drag all the foreign trade of Victoria to
Melbourne. It was all disease and bribery and un-
wholesomeness—an unnaturally large city bloated
out of all proportion through the dragging to it of
trade which, owing to the absurd distances, couldn’t
be dragged to it without heavy loss to somebody ;
while the city repaid the kindness thus bestowed
on it by subsidising the big landlords who
were driving population out of the coun-
try and making its richest districts a wilder-
ness. But amid all this bribery the Tory
party never thought of doing anything for
posterity, because posterity isn’t born yet. It
didn’t think of buying the goodwill of the
small farmer, because he hardly exists. Ana it
didn’t dream of cadging for the support of any
seaport town save Melbourne, because, so far, the
others have practically no population. It would
be infinitely better for Victoria as a whole if its
western railways and its western trade concen-
trated at Portland, and a great city sprung up
there, instead of trade being aragged over vast
needless distances to Melbourne; but there are
hardly any people at Portland, and the Tory
policy doesn’t look ahead. It cares nothing for
posterity or for the future of the country. All it
thinks of is getting the vote of the man who is
there now.
• * * *
The Bulletin has no very gorgeous opinion of
the capacity of George Michael Prendergast,
but it urges the Victorian people to vote for
him and his party with all their heart and soul.
Politically Prendergast can’t be as bad as Bent,
for no politician can be as bad as Bent is now. If
Prendergast gets the Premiership and proves a
failure, he can be thrown out, so the experiment
isn’t irrevocable. It is only by remorselessly
throwing out the Premier who has brought utter
disgrace on the community that there is any hope
or prospect of real reform. A State which does
that gets a decent progressive Government sooner
or later, but a State which sticks always to Bent
because Bent says that Prendergast is a dan-
gerous character has Bent lying on its conscience
so long as that person lives, and when he dies
Bent’s minion takes up the job. No experiment
which gets rid of Bent can be described as
hazardous, for Bent has become the last possi-
bility in the way of incapacity and political dis-
honor. This is the fact which Victoria requires to
Easte in its hat if it hopes to become anything
etter than the lamentable circumstance it is
to-day.
PLAIN ENGLISH.
The Folly of Exporting Raw Material.
On Friday last there left Sydney for Rotterdam
(Europe) the ship Hougomont, loaded up with
4000 tons of oil shale from the Wolgan Valley of
N.S.W. This is the largest shipment of oil shale
ever sent from N. S. Wales, and others on a simi-
lar scale are to follow. The German is raking our
shale away as fast as he can get it, although he
has large quantities of his own. Nobody sup-
poses that the German is buying Australian shale
out of love for Australia, and when he buys it in
the face of the fact that he has so much of his own,
he thereby proves conclusively that the Australian
product has special qualifications which make it
worth while to drag it half way round the world.
For that 4000 tons of shale Australia will get in
the gross £BOOO. Some of it contains 160 gallons
of oil to the ton, none of it is below the 120-gallon
mark, so if it is averaged at 130 gallons to the ton
it will be a low estimate. And at the lowest esti-
mate, allowing nothing for the highly-priced bye-
products, that oil is worth in its crude state Is. 6d.
per gallon ; so that shipment of 4000 tons of shale
represents at least £39,000, for which Australia
receives £BOOO. Therefore this country, through
having (by reason of its Freetrade folly) no oil
industry, loses £31,000 on that single shipment,
and as the export of the rich shale is going ahead
with leaps and bounds, the loss threatens to run
into millions in the near future. It might be
worth mentioning, also, that the greater bulk of
that loss is in wages. ' It comes out of the pockets
of the men who are represented by the Labor party
—the party which sinks the fiscal issue ; and which
was, largely at the instigation of J. C. Watson,
responsible for the removal of the duty on
kerosene, a deletion which handed over the Aus-
tralian oil trade to the infamous Standard Oil
Trust of U.S. A. It is a shocking example of the
disease called Commerce, and the Freetrade statis-
tician of the awful Pulsford type will be delighted
over the transaction. He will point with pride
to the fact that our exports of shale are jumping
skyward, and ignore the fact that every ship-
ment of 1000 tons represents a loss, chiefly in
wages, of £7750. And when Australia sends
abroad a few thousand pounds’ worth of raw wool
to be made into tweed elsewhere, and after making
a loss on that also, uses the money it got front the
sale of the shale and the wool to re-import a little of
the manufactured wool products from Germany,
Puls. ■ > will be in a perfect frenzy of delight,
because rite statistics will show that our imports
as well as our exports are soaring up magnificently.
It isn't as though that shipment of shale were a
solitary horrible example—the same principle
applies toalmost everything that Australia exports.
The daily Freetrade papers rave about the urgent
need to encourage immigration. Well, as good a
way as any to encourage immigration is to make
work for people who come here, and if we had an
oil industry here that £31,000 on board the Hougor
mont that will go into Dutch or German pockets
would have stayed in Australian pouches—mostly
those of Australian artisans. The previous week
saw another ship, only a little one, take away
2000 tons of shale; so, in one fortnight, N.S.W.
(and Australia) lost £46,500 through having no
import duty on kerosene, and the loss is at the
rate of £1,209,000 per annum. That sum would
pay wages to quite an army of people. No
wonder the Yankees regard us as fools! A
country that throws away an industry like that
should certainly have its capital called after an
animal with long ears—the Yass.
Lo! The Gentle Indian.
The inadequately tutored savage who toils for a
small wage aboard P. and O. steamers gave the
black labor cause another bad ad. on the 16th
inst. The.Seedee-boysof the Mongolia started “dis-
cussing politics ” with the Punjabis—whom mail-
ship passengers know generically as Lascars. The
Punjabi coolie’s immemorial method of clinching
an argument is to grab a knife, tomahawk, or axe,
and therewith to lay open the rival controver-
sialist. This is what happened on the Mongolia.
Thus the S. M. Herald’s war correspondent the
morning after the fray :
The Punjabis started on the Seedee-boys, and soon a
desperate encounter was in progress. Knives, forks,
hammers, hardwood sticks, shovels, and anything that
came to hand were snatched up, and freely used in the
m@l6e. Although greatly outnumbered, the Africans
gave a good account of themselves. They used fists and
sticks against the knives of the Punjabis. Several of the
combatants on both sides were injured. Blood was freely
flowing from a dozen of the fighters. A Seedee-boy fell
from a knife thrust in the body, and a couple of Indians
were knocked almost senseless by blows on the head.
Both sides yelled and swore, and their shouts, mingling
with the cries of the wounded, created a veritable in-
ferno. The forecastle was slippery with blood from the
combatants.
The angel of peace in the form of a horde of con-
stables descended on the scene after a while, and
haled the more aggressive gore-spillers to the
lockup. The Lascars who weren’t arrested held
converse together, and decided that the liberty of
the subject had been outraged. They couldn’t
understand it. Again in the words of the
Herald—
They resented their companions being taken to gaol.
They demanded their release, and, refusing to obey the
officers, left the ship in a body. Shouting and gesticu-
lating, they made towards Circular Quay. A crowd soon
collected, and the Indians were the centre of a surging
mob. A number of city police and water police were at
hand in case of further disorder. The Punjabis, chiefly
firemen and greasers, were attired in all sorts of dis-
reputable-looking attire. Many were barefooted, some
were clothed in garments made from hessian bags;
several had cuts, bruises and bandages—mute evidence
of the recent affray. All were black with soot and oil,
and presented a wildly dishevelled aspect.
Their objective was the abiding-place of the
P. and O. manager. Fortunately, at this stage a
brace of Machiavelian John Dunns chanced
along, and piloted the band into the Water Police
Court yard (they told the band the specious tale
that the P. and O. manager lived in the yard in
an undefended state), and there left it to cool.
It was providential, from a heap of standpoints,
that the colored brethren embarked in political
controversy while the Mongolia was berthed by the
shores of a well-policed city. Had the vessel been
at sea the handful of white officers and quarter-
masters that ships of her class carry would have
been hard put to it to prevent not only the fore-
castle, but also the spar and hurricane decks
and the saloons and the cabins being made
“slippery with the blood of combatants,”
and also with the blood of non-combatants —folks
whose last wish when they booked their passages
was to find themselves in a confined space on a
waste of waters while Asiatics were running
amok. The chairman of the P. &O. Company
complained last week about the diminution of
passenger traffic of late years on the Australian
run. A possible explanation is to be found in the
fact that rival companies—the Orient, the White
Star, the N.D.L., the Messageries Mari times,
and others—either employ no blacks at all (this
is the case with the two first-named concerns)
or only employ a limited number; and that
below decks where the passengers are not liable
to come across them. The P. & O. nigger is
always in evidence. He is paraded in state, with
his gladdest rags swathed about him, each Sunday
for the edification of voyagers. He pervades every
part of the steamer on week-days, pottering
futilely away morning, noon, and night at his
brass-polishings, deck-scrubbings, rope-coilings,
and the like. His habits are dreadful. He is
physically dirty. It is his unpleasant way to
cast on white women-folk the tender, yearning
regard that maddens the ladies’ relatives when
they observe it. Anglo-Indians who comprehend
his lingo tell fearsome yarns of the remarks he
passes to his brother-nigs. while about his daily
board-ship toil, concerning the points of conforma-
tion, etc., of the travelling Caucasian damsel—
preferably in her presence and hearing. In his
favor it may be owned that he doesn’t demand
big wages, that he is indifferent as to where he
bunks, that he eats little, works long hours, and
in general plays the rfile of slave in a fairly satis-
factory style. But even as a slave there are
weak points discernible in him. He is too un-
disciplined, too easily roused to riot and mutiny,
and too ready when so roused to submit his griev-
ances to the arbitrament of the meat-chopper.
Taken for all in all, he makes one of the jumpiest
and most unsatisfactory fellow-voyagers imagin-
able for a long sea journey.
The Marriage Tie Again.
Three items, curiously connected with each other,
appeared in the Melbourne morning dailies On the
16th. The first item reported the return of Wal-
pole, the lecturer for the Employees’ Federation,
who has been on a health trip abroad. Walpole
is the gentleman who, at one of his meetings, re-
ferred to marriage as a luxury for working men.
The second item was the report of a meeting of
clergymen, of various denominations, to discuss
the unemployed question. When Walpole was
taxed with his marriage-luxury statement, he first
denied it, and then said it was a joke. Finally
his employers indignantly asked if they were to
be held responsible for every word their lecturer
said ? It is therefore very interesting to hear, at
first hand, what the employers think on the ques-
tion of marriage for the working classes. At the
meeting of the clergymen the following remarks,
as reported by the Age, were made
Rev. Mr. Dixon said that complaints were often heard
that men could not be got; but plenty of labor was
available if fair wages were offered. In one instance that
had come under his notice a married man had been
offered £1 per week, and when he (Mr. Dixon) spoke to
the employer of this offer, he was met with the observa-
tion—“ Men are not compelled to marry.”
Rev. T. S. Woodfuld said the ugly aspect of it was that
decent working men were not able to marry because they
could not get decent wages for their labor ; but the out-
come of that position was to bolster up another man, who
could thus keep three wives.
The third item was the report of a meeting in
Gippsland, where loud and shrill complaints were
raised by farmers about the “ shortage of labor.”
One speaker said, fiercely, that if the Govern-
ment did its duty it would import 1000
farm laborers. And yet, except for a few
weeks during harvest, the wage offered is never
more than £1 a week and found, and is often very
much less. The farmer knows well that he
himself cannot do much for his family on £1 a
week; but he never dreams of the possibility of
the employee having a wife and family, or desir-
ing these useful adjuncts. And, when there
is little labor offering on these rascally
terms, he calls on Heaven to witness
his predicament, and curses the Govern-
ment for not relieving him. If the average
farmer had his way he would import, not 1000,
but 1,000,000 farm laborers, so that they would be
tumbling over each other after jobs, and would be
at last content to work for their tucker and the
farmer’s cast-off pants. They wouldn’t want
coats, for they wouldn’t have time to put them
on. And when Tom Tunnkcliffe, the Labor
man who represents a country constituency, says
that he wants a Wages Board for farm-laborers, the
Argus makes a cross-heading of the awful fact,
and almost faints at the announcement. Yet, if
the Argus were accused of having designs on the
marriage tie, it would probably blush to the roots
of its bonnet.
“ Loppy.”
Thus “Loppy,” whom The Bulletin knows for
a good unionist : —
The Federal Arbitration Court award of 1907 in the
sheep-shearing business will come up for revision in 1909.
It. is possible that some other than the shearer will benefit
from-the revision, and this is written to put the case of
the “loppy” and others.
Experience of the past two shearings has brought two
tilings clearly into view. First, never before have there
been so many big tallies reported from every pastoral
district in the Commonwealth ; and, secondly, the posi-
tion of the shearer has so advanced in strength that he
looks down, in the height of his power, upon his fellow-
workers in the shed. The shed bosses, experts, and wool-
pressers are now' classed in his mind and speech with the
rouseabout under the generic term “ Loppy.” Small
wonder, for he is above them all!
It is natural, of course, that when a man receives a 20
per cent, increase in pay, he should consider himself at
least 20 per cent, more important in the scheme of
things. The shearer values himself at more than that,
for he is pretty sure that in the 1909 revision he will
receive a further increase, and a consequently greater
importance. Meanwhile he has shown little desire to help
his less fortunate co-worker, the “ loppy,” in any way.
ltather he increases the burden. As thus :—
Under the Arbitration award, 48 hours constitute a
week’s work. But it works out at a minimum of 54 hours
for all othershed workers, and, to get these 54 hours in,
it is necessary for all “loppies” to attend on the shearer
for a minimum of 60 hours per week, and in the case of
cooks and slushies, for a further 15 or 16 hours. These
extra hours are shearer-made, and are quite avoidable.
A typical day’s work in any shed will illustrate.
The rate of pay does not enter into the question at
all, be it understood. The first whistle blows at
5.30 a.m. All hands but the cooks apd engine-driver—
they are up at 4.30 a.m.—arise from their downy wool-
packs, to snatch a pot of tea and a bite before thewhistle
blows at 6 o’clock, when shearing starts. The breakfast
run lasts for two hours—for the shearer. The “loppy”
has to wait until the last sheep is off the board, ten
minutes perhaps and later, since a shearer-made custom
prevails to reserve the toughest sheep in the catching-pen
for the “ bell sheep.” Generally “loppy” meets many of
the shearers returning from meals before he reaches his
own tea-bucket.
Start again at 9 sharp, and run till 10.20, when
there is a spell for the shearer of 20 minutes ; for
“ loppy,” about 10. Another run of 1 hour 20 minutes,
and then dinner. More tough “ bell ” sheep for “ loppy ”
to wait for. Start at 1 o’clock, and thence on to 5.50,
with two breaks of 20 and 30 minutes respectively. At
6.50 the last whistle blows, and the shed is cleaned up of
all wool, giving “loppy" liberty to hobble down at 6
o’clock or later, to be abused by the cook for being late.
“ Loppy ” is thus on his legs from 5.80 a.m. to 6 p.m. to
get in 8 hours 40 minutes work, the 40 minutes being
added to allow work to finish at 12 on Saturday. On
Saturday “ loppy ” has to be in the shed after dinner to
clean up generally, and, if he is a shed-hand, to wash
down the whole floor, which he does in about half-an-
hour. Total hours for the week, not fewer than 66!
It has often been asked why the tallies have become so
much greater of late years, and to those who have to
wait on the shearer the answer is plain. They are a direct
result of a shearer-made rule of short “ runs,” punctuated
by comparatively long spells, and would not be possible
if shearers worked as navvies, etc., work, in two runs of
4hrs. 20min. each, with an hour’s break for dinner.
In the old and comparatively easy-going days of
“ blade ’’ shearing, our position was immensely better, for
wool-rooms were designed then to handle a certain
amount of wool per day, while very few have been altered
to allow for the greatly accelerated pace of machine-
shearing. Consequently the “ loppy ” has to work at as
high pressure as the shearer on one-fifth of the wage, and
for longer hours.
What we want is more consideration from our incon-
siderate brother, the shearer. We want something better
than increase of pay, although that is good. We want
something better than putting in 12£ hours per day, to
make up 8 hours 40 minutes. Everybody concerned but
the shearer wants it too. Owners, bosses, musterers,
cooks, shed-hands, pressers, experts—all would then have
a little comfort and reasonable hours at the expense of
not more than, say, from 10 to 20 sheep per day per
shearer. In reality, he would not lose that number, for
he would not so often want a spell of a day under the
two-“run” system of steady work, as against the six-
“run” system of rip, slash and tear.

Elective Ministries and Parliamentary
Stagnation.
The Fisher Ministry has fled into recess without
any progress being made with the Defence
question, or with the taking over of the Northern
Territory, which is a part of the Defence question;
and as the Ministry isn’t expected to bolt back
out of recess for about six months these urgent
matters seem likely to have a long, quiet rest.
This restful habit arises quite naturally out of the
present absurd and illogical system of govern-
ment. If Australia had Elective Ministries fixed
for three years, the Cabinet wouldn’t be perpetu-
ally sprinting for the haven of recess and dropping
its luggage as it ran, because it and its billet would
be just as safe while Parliament was sitting as at
any other time. But with the existing method the
only time when a Government’s job and wage are
secure for even a moment is when the Houses are
shut up and the work of the country is wholly
suspended. An Elective Ministry would actually
want to keep Parliament sitting and to Do Things,
for upon his record for Doing Things would de-
pend each Minister’s chance of being re-appointed
to his portfolio. On the other hand, the Ministry
that is formed under the loony system of party
government, wants to avoid Doing Things. Doing
Things is often plain suicide. To bring in an
urgent measure of 100 clauses only means that the
Cabinet deliberately takes 103 chances of losing
its situation—it risks defeat on the first, second,
and third readings, and on each of the 100 clauses
in committee, to say nothing of about 100 more
odd chances of defeat on amendments and motions
for adjournment. Therefore the Ministry, look-
ing at the Defence Bill, asks itself: “Will we
deliberately take 203 separate risks of losing our
pay and dignity and importance, or will we
shut up and go home, and make our positions
safe for six months, and leave Australia unde-
fended ? ” Naturally enough it shuts up and goes
home, and leaves Australia undefended. Of
course, by so doing it incurs the obloquy of
neglecting the public business, but as every
Ministry under the party system neglects the
public business in order to save its skin, no
Cabinet is likely to be ejected on that ground
unless its neglect becomes something stupendous
and extraordinary. The party system is a curse
all round, and in its tendency to cause the inde-
finite hanging up of great and difficult questions,
it is an especially great curse. The establishment
of the system of Elective Ministries has become
the most urgent question in Australian politics,
for it seems as if hardly anything will really be
done until that system is established.
The Importer’s Clerk and the Manufacturer’s
Clerk.
The United Clerks’ Union of N.S.W. has thrown
a carefully-selected brick at Gregory Wade and
at sundry other members of the Assembly, and
there are hopes that that brick has struck where
it will do most good. It took the clerks
years and years of deep thought before they
decided to abandon their exclusive but wretch-
edly ill-paid attitude and to reach out for
a living wage and better conditions in the
only way that any such reach was possible
—by unionism and affiliation with the Trades
Hall. When they had so decided the rest seemed
easy, and the snag which the quill-shoving pro-
fession struck in the Assembly was quite unex-
pected. The Clerks’ Union was thrown out at
Premier Wade’s instigation, and refused the
benefit of the Industrial Disputes Act. Since
then things have been happening. The secretary
of the Clerks’ Union has written to every mem-
ber who voted against Industrial Arbitration in
clerkdom, asking him to explain whether he voted
as he did because he thought clerks were so beastly
well paid that they didn’t require consideration,
or if that wasn’t his reason what was it? Some
didn’t reply. Others said that they voted as they
did in ignorance of the real circumstances of the
case. Now these members couldn’t be ignorant
of the fact that the over-working and under-
payment of clerks is one of the horrible,
unspeakable scandals of our civilisation —
to be unaware of that would qualify a
man for the lunatic asylum; so presumably
the circumstance these members had not dropped
to was that the Clerks’ Union is likely to be a
power, and that united clerkdom is able to throw
at least six Tory members into the political dust-
bin at next election, and put Labor men in their
places. Premier Wade, however, had other ex-
planations to offer—very remarkable explana-
tions. Gregory holds that some clerks should
have the benefit of the Industrial Disputes
Act and some shouldn’t. He reckons that
a clerk in a woollen factory should come
in along with the rest of the weavers, and
the brewery clerk should be lumped in with the
beer-makers, and soon ; but that those clerks who
are not attached to any industry that is already
under the Act should remain as they are. To
quote Wade’s own words :—
Generally speaking, all persons engaged in manual labor
would be within the purview of the statute, as well as
any person connected with the industry working in inti-
mate association with those engaged in manual labor,
although their work may not be of that character.
Mr. Wade understands that those engaged in clerical
work in connection with many industries that are within
the purview of the Act, come within the jurisdiction of
the Court, or the Boards; but to establish an industry
separately for clerks may bring within the law bank
clerks, solicitors’ and barristers' clerks, whose occupations
are of a commercial ciiakacter, and have no association
with the industrial life of the country.
This shows the usual illogicalness of Wadk, for a
clerk is a manual laborer. His labor may not be
so heavy in a mere muscular sense as some, but
The Blessedness of Belonging.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It would be a very bad thing for the country if it 3 man-
hood were compelled to serve as militia. It would mean
interference with the industrial life of the community,
and an enormous reduction of its earning power. The
people of the country did not intend that any such sys-
tem should be introduced. On the other hand, we belong
to a nation that has a stronger fleet than any two nations
in the world .—Premier Ward, of Maoriland.
It is, it is a splendid thing to be a mere belonging;
To trust to other hands than ours the righting of
our wronging;
To dance demurely to a pipe that other lips are
playing,
Admitting they may choose the tune who have to
do the paying.
To hope that eyes across the sea are sedulously
scanning
Our shaping and environment; that distant heads
are planning
The binding of our swaddling-clothes, the bottles
for our feeding,
And whether spank or hushaby is what we most
are needing.
But it is just a sorry thing, beyond our counte-
nancing,
To give our sons some soldiering, here, of our own
financing;
To teach our country’s menfolk that they owe
that country duty;
To tell them it is up to them to shield their
ladies’ beauty.
Most surely we don’t want to fight, by Jingo ! It
is madness
To take us from the counting-house, the farm, for
such-like badness;
For, oh, it is not right to fight!—leastwise while
there are others
Who can be got to deputise by hailing them as
brothers.
Then twang the lyre, upraise the voice, and set
the welkin ringing
Loud echoes to this Anthem of a proud young
Nation’s singing :
“We are a money-earning crowd, a people not
intending
To fritter any time away, our own effects de-
fending,
“ While we can boast the blessedness of being a
belonging,
One more repressed dependency amid the tribe a-
thronging,
A-pushing and a-shoving, and a-begging for a
hearing,
Beside that Car Imperial which Self is always
steering.”
