The Victorian electors have to swallow a
general election and the Christmas holidays at
one gulp, so to speak. To get through this per-
formance with credit and comfort they will also
need to swallow judicious quantities of that sus-
taining inspiration, D.C.L. Whisky. When duly
fortified with D.C.L. Wisdom the heat and
burthen of the fray—-any fray—is as cheerfully
endured as a Christmas panto.*
the man who pushes a pen is as much a worker
with his hands as the man who pushes a plane or
a barrow, and the man who drives a quill is a
manual worker just as is the one who drives
a horse or an engine. Further, the clerk who
shoves a pen in the office of McArthur, im-
porter, is quite as much a worker as his brother
who shoves the same implement in the office
of Vicars, tweed manufacturer, and it is a very
illogical prospective Chief Justice who makes fish
of one and plain beef of the other. One very
probable reason of the anomaly is this: The
Industrial Disputes Act applies to the Australian
manufacturer, but not to the importer. The
Australian manufacturer has to pay decent
wages; the foreign importer is allowed to
pay what he darned well pleases, and if
he imports his goods from low-wage countries,
where the manufacturer also pays what
he darned well pleases, he gets such an
advantage that the benefit which the tariff is sup-
posed to confer on the Australian manufacturer
may be wholly nullified. Possibly Gregory
Wade, as a virulent Foreign-trader, hates to
spoil this beautiful arrangement of things. Pos-
sibly he thinks that it is quite reasonable that the
clerk who makes out Vicars’ invoice for
Australian tweed should get £3 10s. a week,
while the one who makes out McArthur’s
invoice for imported tweed should struggle along
at £1 if his employer likes to pay him at
that figure, thereby giving the importer £l3O a
year start on this matter of one employee alone.
The arrangement may seem very beautiful to
Wade, but clerkdom is likely to make a note of
the fact that it was the Freetiade and Tory
interest which labored to keep its miserable
Wages down, and it will probably put the note
where it can find the thing on the morning of
election day.
♦
“ Old Soldier ” writes :
Sydney Telegraph lately found a text, and built on it
a leader about a “ great volunteer army.” The text was
the cabled report that a U.S. Congress Committee pro-
posed raising a volunteer army 2,000,000 strong to be
ready for emergencies. The absurd paper seized on the
yarn, and held forth with enthusiasm :
“ Excepting Great Britain, the United States is the
only first-class Power in the world that depends upon
voluntary military service.”
Then it talked about the enlisted army of the Union,
60,000 strong, and proclaimed itself a perverter of the
truth in this wise :
“There are, supplementary to these, organised and re-
served militia, the former with a total strength of
125,000, while the reserves—justifying their title with
literal accuracy—consist of uncalled men between the
ages of 18 and - 45 years. ALL THESE are not only under
the same obligation to service in defence as obtains in
every country, WHETHER IT IS EXPRESSED OR
NOT, but THEY ARE LEGALLY LIABLE TO SERVE
IN A NATIONAI. EMERGENCY FOR TWO YEARS,
SO THAT COMPULSION TO SERVICE IS MUCH
MORE EXPLICITLY provided for than it is with us."
There it was in plain words, in the middle of the leader,
showing that the statement in the beginning about how
the U.S. depended on voluntary military service was a
mere product of the Telegraph’s disordered brain. It
still has “conscription” on the organism it thinks it
thinks with. It even tries to supply alleged arguments
for “ Australian conscriptionists,” whoever they may be,
to account for the alleged Yankee eccentricity, on the
ground that the volunteer system—
“ Is preferable because it brings into the service the
men most fitted for the work. It enables a selection to
be made, because there is no necessity to treat the fit
and unfit, the lazy and the active, the clever and the
dull, all the same as inevitable soldiers so long as they
are not physically incapacitated; and that selective
operation is made the easier because where service is
voluntary the majority of those who enrol will naturally
be men who like the work for its own sake, and have the
invaluable stimulus of enthusiasm.”
Yet the Telegraph cannot surely have forgotten the
Boer war, and Australia’s experience with its successive
contingents. The “ invaluable stimulus of enthusiasm,”
combined with higher pay than is received by any
other army on earth, produced a total of 15,000
men or thereabouts, and, after about the first two
contingents, each one that was sent was physically
and morally worse than its predecessor. The ex-
perience of the British army' has been the same. When
times are good the rate of recruiting is quite insufficient
to keep up the numbers, except in certain favored corps
such as the Artillery and Engineers, where men can earn
extra'working pay. As for the expenditure of men
in war, recruiting has never been able to keep up with it.
There was poor old Raglan’s cry from the Crimea:
“ For God’s sake, send no more boy recruits.” It broke
his heart to see them filling the hospitals. It was the
same in India in the Mutiny. At the time of the out-
break in May,’s7, most of the regiments in the country
were full of seasoned old soldiers, and the campaigning
life suited them fairly well. But the hard fighting round
Delhi, Cawnpore, Lucknow, etc., reduced many regi-
ments to skeletons, and when, after the neck of the
rising was broken, the recruits —the men with “the in-
valuable stimulus of enthusiasm ’’—began to arrive, they
sickened and went into hospital wholesale because
they couldn’t stand the long, rapid marches. Enthu-
siasm is useless without stamina.

The imminence of the Vic. State elections draws
attention to the disgraceful anomalies in repre-
sentation in the State that has the awful Bent
adhering to it. According to the latest figures,
there are 263,909 electors entitled to vote for 65
members, and an analysis into metropolitan,
urban and country constituencies gives this
result: —

The scandalous fact that one man speaks for 5248
electors in the metropolis and that another
babbles for only 3454 in the country will be better
appreciated by this small table, showing the

in proportion to the number of electors

Which shows that the country has beaten the
metropolis for six seats, and is thus six seats over-
represented. So that, in addition to loafing on
the city taxpayer for its bridges, roads, etc., the
country has collared from him six members to
which it is not entitled. Under the circumstances,
it seems really strange that it has left him any
representation at all.
Farmer-Senator Russell, of S.A., makes the
community tired with his enthusiasm about the
advantages of a Yassberra capital because of its
allegedly fine soil—though as Russell doesn’t
know yet whereabouts, within a good many miles,
the city will be, even his bucolic raptures may be
premature. Cows and turnips aren’t the only quali-
fications of a city which is to be unique in the
world’s history, inasmuch as it will be the only city
on earth which is the capital of a whole continent.
If the Australian metropolis is to be the success
it should be, it will presently get far beyond the
mere heifer and mangel-wurzel stage. The only
blot on the design of Melbourne are the “Little”
streets —Flinders, Collins, Bourke, Lonsdale, and
the like. They were narrowed down because, in
the view of a person with the same fine instinct
for cattle which distinguishes Senator Russell,
the only possible use for these back ways would be
to admit the settlers’ cows to the settlers’ hinder
premises. It is a mistake to put cow and
turnip people on to the creation of new cities.
Their outlook is too narrow. If Constantine the
Great had been a dairyman instead of a statesman
he wouldn’t have put Constantinople where he
did; he would have looked for some inland site
where the feed was better, and where there was
no Bosphorus for the cows to fall into.

Now that Bent’s land transactions have been
stirred up, the purchase of the Thornbury brick
land begins to look interesting. Bent bought
this land for £IOO per acre from the Commercial
Bank (he is said to have been one of a shindykit
that held it before the Bank acquired it); and
then he spent about £IOOO in buying brickmaking
machinery. He thus bluffed the Brick Combine
into reducing the price of bricks from 40s. to 38s.
per 1000 for nearly two years; but then, as the
State brickworks were never started, the price
went back to the old figure and stays there.
Bent thus certainly advantaged the public by
something like £SOO or possibly even £IOOO.
But he might have done a great deal more, and
made the benefit permanent; for, when Bent
refused to do anything with the land and works,
at least two private individuals offered to lease
the property, pay standard wages, and sell bricks
for 30s. per 1000. Somebody had, however,
apparently breathed something in Bent’s cup-
like ear; wherefore Bent’s only reply was that
he was going to hand the property over to the
Closer Settlement Board. Evidently he didn’t;
anyway, nothing has been done with the land,
which is still idle. Now why did Bent buy that
land for the State ? To make bricks ? Then why
didn’t he make them, or even allow other people
to make them ?
Dr. Richard Wallace appears for the
defence: —
Re a paragraph of your issue of November 26. I
ask vour permission to lay before your readers the
salient features of the much-debated case of Grimson
v. Freeman and Wallace. It has already, I trust, been
made manifest enough that, so far from having treated
Grimson, the plaintiff, I did not even know of his ex-
istence until I received his writ for £IOOO, because in
August, 1906, I severed my connection with the Freeman
and Wallace Institute, and I never afterwards darkened
its doors until this writ was served on me. I held no
communication whatever with Freeman, and was in per-
fect ignorance of anything which took place for over two
years at the Institute. Grimson, it now appears,
was treated by a Dr. Samuel Lane Wallace. In
August, 1906, when I quitted Freeman, I had a
deed prepared dissociating me absolutely from the
Freeman and Wallace Institute, and stipulating that in
all newspapers bearing my name throughout the Com-
monwealth were to be withdrawn in fourteen days from
the date of deed, and after the expiration of three months
from date of agreement the name of Dr. Richard Wallace
was to be deleted from all printed matter connected with
the Freeman and Wallace Institute. My instructions
were not carried out, but a provision was inserted to the
effect that Freeman was at liberty to use the name of Dr.
Richard Wallace “ until his present stock of printed matter
was exhausted.” The presence of this clause was ruinous
to me at the trial, as the fact that my name was signed to
the deed precluded mv counsel from putting me in the
witness box. The Judge asked, “ Why did not Dr. Richard
Wallace sav that he had ceased to be a partner?” Asa
matter of fact, 1 was not a partner, so how could I state
that I had ceased to be one ? Well, a verdict for £450 was
given against me, which, with Costs up to date, including
those of Bankruptcy, into which I have been driven, lets
me in for £BOO or £9OO.
Again, you say “ While he (Dr. Richard Wallace) was
supposed to be curing the suffering young man in ‘ his labo-
ratories ’ in one part of Sydney, he was really trying to lend
the young man £1 on personal security in a frowsy little
office in another part of it.” Please allow me to say
emphatically that I did not lend on personal security, and
that I never lent £1 in any case. The terms upon which
I lent were advertised in all the daily papers. Again, I
was not struggling with a little money-lending business,
as you state. I had nearly £IOOO in what was then
a successful enterprise, and I had considerably more
than another £IOOO to call upon. “As to a
frowsy little office,” the furniture and fittings
of said office cost me £4O, and everybody who transacted
business with me complimented me on the up-to-date look
of my surroundings. It is surely enough that lam
absolutely ruined, as the Official Assignee has disposed of
mv house, my land, and my financial business, leaving
me absolutely penniless. I shall conclude by quoting a
remark made to my wife, by Mr. Moriarty, counsel for
the Official Assignee : “ Mrs. Wallace, we know that your
husband is the victim, and we hope to make the person,
properly responsible for your husband’s trouble, bear the
expense of the verdict and Costs.”
If the Australian capital is ever planted at
Yassberra, one man who will wish he were dead
will be David Hall, member for Werriwee. In
fact, he probably will be dead—politically. With
900 square miles hacked out of Werriwee there
will have to be a re-arrangement, and David
Hall’s constituency will probably be cut up and dis-
sipated, and David is likely to be an unemployed
legislator. Things are not always what they
seem. In a general way : (1) The thicker the
population the smaller the constituency; (2) the
smaller the constituency the more serious the hole
that will be made in it by the excision of a Federal
territory ; (3) the more serious the hole the greater
the probability that the constituency will vanish
and the local member have to go and hustle for a
crust amid new surroundings.

Julius Knight is no whit less effective as
Marcus than he was a decade ago. He possesses
most of the assets that make for success in a part
of this class. He is a master of “ make-up” and
of costuming. He has the rhythmic diction and
the fine, resonant voice that playgoers look for in
a Roman dignitary ; and a brow that fairly
clamors for the Olympian wreath. In the low-
necked suiting of the period he contrives to look
as though he had just stepped off the “heads”
side of a Pompeiian coin. These advantages, in
all likelihood, more than the acting he puts into
the r6le, cause his Marcus to live in the affections
of the populace in the astonishing way it does.
Herbert Leigh plays Nero in a nose grotesquely
arched and palpably false—the sort of
deformity the Jew usurer of melodrama
is wont to plaster on his countenance. And he
represents the erring potentate as an old man.
Wherefore does he do these things ? It is known
that the youthful Enobarbus was a physically un-
afflicted, not to say a comely lad ; and he was
still in the twenties when his Christian-burning
feats were at their height. Miss Beatrice Day
plays Mercia sweetly and convincingly. Harry
Plimmer (Tigellinus), Eardley Turner (Glabrio)
and Leslie Victor (Philodemus) act with distinc-
tion ; and Miss Wilson (Berenis), Miss Gleeson
(Steplianus), Miss Guildford Quinn (Dacia), and
Miss Preston (Ancaria) all do good work.

These nights there is no vast expanse of
boiled shirt and alabaster neck at Her Majesty’s
(Sydney); in the 12th week of the season the
Merry Widow’s Marsovian millions are being
pursued for the special behoof of Bill who is
down from the country, and Bill’s suburban
cousins. ThelatterhaveheldofftheWidowhitherto
out of consideration for Bfll, who would be down
at Christmas and would be sure to want to go.
Besides, Bill would pay. After three months of
them, the few jokes of the Widow don’t seem any
fresher ; in fact, the door-knob which Gouriet's
hen lays after eating the brass filings is nearly due
for re-lacquering If the old fowl could only be fed
on something that would help her to lay a new
joke it would be a blessed relief to the orchestra.
It doesn’t come so hard on the princes and other
polite young persons who show you your seat if
you don’t know it; for they’ve been changed
since the season started. Evidently, after the
67th time of laughing at it (as per contract), the
door-knob choked them.

Maggie Moore and H. R. Roberts remove
that seasoned drama, “Struck Oil,” from the
stage of Sydney Palace, about 11 p.m. on Christ-
mas Eve, some four hours before the cherished in-
fant arises to lay waste the slumbers of the
hoqsehold with the wail of his tin trumpet. A
period of 15 hours will then elapse in the usual
manner, and on Boxing Day, at 2 p.m., Edwin
Geach will present our old beachcombing friend,
“R. Crusoe,” tp the newer generation. Crusoe
will sail back to Juan Fernandez for his evening
meal, and at 8 p.m. or thereabouts the uncom-
fortable assertion that “ The Woman Pays,” will
be made for the first time and with tremendous
emphasis. The Palace, with its double bill,
threatens to be a busy place for some time to
come.
** * *
The Sydney Liedertafel came out bravely
for its Christmas concert last week, and drew an
unusually crowded house. Two big choruses, with
orchestra by Max Bruch, gave the society scope
for real good work, and the performance was quite
exhilarating. “The Romans’ Song of Iriumph
and “The Vintagers and Boatmen” are the
titles. Instead of time-honored “Nazareth,”
two Mendelssohn’s choruses gave a chance
for the melody of “Hark, the Herald Angels
Sing,” to creep out. Ernestine Louat, a pleasant
soprano, and baritone Andrew Black were in
glorious form.

Sydney Tivoli this week loses the musical
Latonas and the electrical Marconis, who fly from
the footlights just when Christmas is due to rush
on and stun humanity with a leaden plum pud-
ding. J. W. Rickaby, who is really a stage
humorist, has unhappily taken to singing a rather
ordinary love ditty at the end of his turn ; where-
fore his numerous admirers are filled with amaze-
ment, and ejaculate “Blimy !” in a disappointed
manner. Rickaby would do better to cut out the
sentimental item and replace the policeman song.
A number of new people are programmed for the
end of this week, and the “Tiv.” will then pro-
ceed to rake in the numerous Christmas shilling
faster than ever.

With both of Adelaide’s chief theatres
closed for the present, there has been a good hear-
ing for the leading amateur people—.the Append-
reenas. They gave a careful rendering—perhaps
rather too careful—of the robust Parisian humors
of “Dr. Bill,” the gentleman who is locked up in
a bathroom with his mother-in-law, the key being
concealed down the back of somebody else who
has fainted. On the Glenelg beach some Pierrots
have built themselves a house. Miss Ruby Edgar
(late of the Merrymakers’ co.) is with them, also
Bert Barton. In the city, all is Moving Picture at
present. The old Cyelorama, afterwards an ice-
rink (the first in Australia), has given up skating
for the winter, and joined the crowd of film-fakirs.
** * *
“Jack and Jill,” the Williamson panto,
that broke out at Her Majesty’s (Melbourne),
proved to be 3| crowded hours of glorious uproar
and splendid riot. Other pantos, have been
doomed to popularity on account of their varied
attractions, and “Mother Goose’’ran all round
the Commonwealth for a year or more ; but none
were so fully charged with eccentricity and scenic
force as this latest production. “Jack and Jill ”
never flags or drags. One could have wished, on
the first night, that the orchestra would moderate
its transports when the accompaniment was
too loud; nevertheless the feast of frivol
and flow of tumult seemed well worth a
headache next morning, and a panto, headache,
anyway, is no worse than the after-effects of a
problem-play, a flickergraph or a political debate.
Portions of plot and fragments of nursery fable
are dotted amidst the confection like bits of white
fat in a Teutonic sausage, but for the most part
the entertainment is a mixture of irrelevancies
ranging from topical ditties to birds and beasts
of the jim-jam species. A giant rooster is a
peculiarly special feature. There are so many
special features that the ordinary ones get lost,
sight of in the crowd, and none of them appeared
superfluousonSaturday, except, perhaps, asongand
chorus concerning a bogey man. With hardly an
exception, the ordinary items have humor in them,
whilst the poetic ballets and the prose processions
of pleasing Australian girl are positively beautiful,
not to say lovely. Even Solomon in all his
Marrick ville tweed suit was not arrayed like some
of these, otherwise he would have caught cold in
the joints. No doubt the color scheme in every
tableau is good, and in some cases better.
Artist W. R. Coleman and his lieutenant, George
Upward, have done their duty by this costly
show, together with Gerard Coventry, the stage-
manager, and Jennie Brenan, the ballet mistress.
They may expect their reward in that glittering
Hereafter where fairies are immortal and bad
language is never heard behind the scenes.
Of course, the panto, owes much of its
success to the leading character performers, as
well as to the ballets, the animal impersonators,
and the gorgeous surroundings. Miss Stella
Gastelle, as the principal boy, is full of brightness
and animation, and it is she who seems to rush
the performance off the mark, and keep it going
for all it is worth. Bert Gilbert is always droll
because he gets no chance to over-elaborate his
jokes, and he has a clever accomplice in J. M.
Campbell, formerly of “ The Merry-Makers,” wli®
plays the matron or the doting widow. Harry
Shine happens occasionally in the disguise of a
scarecrow, whose lungs and liver are made of
straw. Miss Nance Maher distinguishes herself
as a singing fairy ; and Fanny Dango, Victor
Loydall, and Stella Selbourne have more or less to
do in the foreground.

The panto, at Melbourne Royal presents
the aged narrative of “Cinderejla” in a more
shapely and reasonable form than it has previously
taken within the memory of persons who are no
longer as young as they were. Something of the
soul of romance has been left in it by the librettist.
The main facts of the fairy tale are decently
clothed in shreds and patches of their original
human interest, and soft airs of reality fan the
cheek of wild extravaganza as she capers on her
way. Sufficiently gorgeous in the spectacular
appointments and sufficiently pantomimic in
character, this up-to-date revival of “Cinderella”
strikes one as being very well adapted to the
“Hook of Holland” company, which fills ten or
more of the parts. When the dialogue has been
somewhat condensed and the action quickened by
practice, the show at the Royal should fully
explain why it was a pantomime instead of a
musical comedy. Melbourne didn’t ask for pan-

CONCERNING A MONEY-SPINNER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On Saturday at 8 o’clock p.m.
The Sydney Royal “ drop ” moved briskly up
And showed the same old “ Street in Rome.”
The crowd
Paced in its gaudy robes beneath the “lime,”
As it has paced since most of us were young.
And shortly, ’mid a surge of cheers, strode on
Julius, stalwart, stern-browed, beautiful.
And now each honest face “in front” glows
bright
With undiluted joy. Parsons are seen
To right and left, who cheer—-and then crouch back
In shamed wise, fearing the ribald jeer
That aye greets men of God who come within
The purview of those “ gods ” that visit plays.
And here an aghd chapel-goer bangs
His gamp upon the floor, and there a maid
Lets go her fellow Sabbath-scholar’s hand,
And her own palms concusses frenziedly,
Sighing the while, “ The Darling / ” “ Oh! How
sweet! ”
And other terms expressive of Great Love.
The howls of Stephanus do not offend
This audience —in fact it likes them. It
Is there for thrills. The vice of Berenis
Renders it virtuously pitiful.
Glabrio’s vinous pranks appeal to it
As Prohibition object-lessons. All
The horrid features of bad, bad Old Rome—
The nakedness, the anti-Christian vim,
The flowing bowls and so forth—give it cheer.
It thinks of the pure suburb where it lives ;
And, gazing on its Pledge (fresh signed), and
clothes
(Ample, if somewhat stuffy), it feels good.
Which in a measure, possibly, explains
“ The Sign’s ” perennial success. No show
E’er failed that (boomed by clerics as “refined ”)
A feast of moral uplift and bare limbs combined.
N.S.W.
10-2-4.

Australians will note with interest the new
industry that is likely to spring up as a result of the
discovery of a Textile Fibre in Spencer Gulf, South Aus-
tralia, and which bids fair, in union with low-grade wools,
to produce a material which, owing to its non-inflamma-
bility, will compete with the flannelettes that have for so
long been a menace to every household. Also, due to its
non-conductivity, it retains the warmth which cotton
reflects, as proved by the blankets and flannels already
manufactured. It has further properties for stuffing and
upholstery purposes, competing successfully with flock
and kapok. Attention is drawn to the prospectus of the
Textile Fibre Company appearing on page 33.*

* * ■» »
The matter at the Royal is introduced to
a bevy of youngsters by a sweetly-spoken Fairy
godmother (Miss Delaney) clothed in gauzy
material and a yard or two of auburn hair. The
course of true story runs smooth through the first
act, which terminates in a sort of transformation
scene, with an electric-lighted coach and four
ponies in the centre thereof. Cromwell is the im-
pecunious baron of tradition, and Edwin Brett is
his second wife—a plump suburban shemale, who
strikes the happy medium between a haughty
mayoress and a common or garden washerlady.
Her daughters by a former marriage are Miss
Jennie Opie and Miss Orford, and whereas the
heroine’s step-sisters have hitherto been ugly
ducklings, these are alarming only as regards their
toilettes. Tom Payne is the boy-in-buttons at-
tached to the baron’s household; Miss Essie Perrin
hovers around in a dancing capacity; and the
beauteous daughter-of-all-work, who brings a
prince into the family, is Miss Ruth Lincoln.
The contrasts are not too violent. Dandini, the
valet to the visiting royalty, is quite soberly im-
personated by Bottomley, the baritone, whilst a
new lady from America (Miss Meredith Meredro)
makes a divinely tall and elegant Prince. She
has all the necessary qualifications for a principal
boy, including two legs of unimpeachable contour.
A dimpled ingratiating potentate is Miss Mere-
dro, and her singing voice holds its own in this
harmonious company. All things considered,
“ Cinderella ” seems good for many nights and a
lot of matinees. The dresses are effective, Phil
Goatcher, Vaughan and others have put in some
pretty scenery, and the artistic restraint of the
performance is soothing to the jaded feelings of
tha present commentator.

“ When Knights Were Bold,” prologued
by “That Brute Simmons,” is finishing its last
nights at Melbourne Bijou. Frank Thornton
hasn’t been doing terrific business'with the show,
though the simple egregiousness of his de Vere is
entitled to more substantial recognition. De Vere
is just the sort of purposeless and callow character
that bounds with a cranky dan at some of Mel-
bourne’s more popular resorts on any warm Sun-
day, though, to be sure, no local could hope to
emulate the exceeding culture of his bleat or his
subtle aristocratic form. However, to-night
(Thursday) he packs up his tin armor and hoary
sundries, and, resting over the goose of to-
morrow, will resume his farewell at the Princess’s
on Boxing night with yet another revival of
“ Charley’s Aunt.”
** * *
Meanwhile, afc Melbourne Bijou, “The
Unwritten Law,” alleged to be an accurate history
of the Thaw outrage in the U.S.A., is in prepara-
tion, also for Boxing Night. In the original
trouble, the sensation de luxe was a lovely pink
girl in a pie. In the present copy thereof, a lovely
pink girl in a pie will be eagerly looked for. It
would hit Melbourne hard as an entrhe without a
name, and also without address.

Afc Melbourne Olympia and afc Wirth’s
Park next door, the attractions are of the usual
grandeur, and continue insidiously to draw mobs
of people over Princes Bridge between 7 and 18
p.m. At the former, a new series of pictures,
that included “Continental Navy Manoeuvres”
and the “Tsar of Bulgaria,” was flickered upon
the bedding last Saturday night. At the latter,
and on the same evening, Mounierand Franz per-
mitted a motor car containing six persons to mote
over their reclining bodies. And, by the way,
the Town Hall is to be utilised for two Christmas
entertainments of widely divergent interest. On
Christmas Night the Philharmonic Society will per-
form “ The Messiah,” and on Saturday (January 2)
Allan Hamilton is to present the stous’ning of
Johnson by Burns, or of Burns by Johnson, as the
stoush may be, on a cinematograph screen.

At the King’s (Melbourne) the blood-
stained fiction of “The Assassin ” was to conclude
its revival on Wednesday night, after which the
Andersonian company will lapse into a brief
festive season prior to a grand production of
Uncle Tom and his Cabin on Boxing Night. To
be more precise, the venerable nigger and his
wooden domicile will come to light on Boxing
afternoon, and the company will be less
orthodoxly Andersonian than usual. For instance,
Ernest Fitts is to raise his impressive voice in
song and prayer as Uncle Thomas, Baby Watson
will be little Eva, and two or three other principal
members of a small cast will be new to the
theatre. Apart from the principals, an army of
choristers, eakewalkers and “revellers” with
blackened faces will assist in brightening the Ole
Plantation Home.
** * *
After pouring strange names into its menu
for the past two or three weeks, Melbourne Opera
House will, presumably, take a spell, and enable
The Bulletin to recuperate. For longer than
this paper cares to remember, it has been relent-
lessly forced to consider a host of fresh faces, some
not so fresh kilts, dogs, implements and instru-
ments, and some really indisputable clothes.
After much consideration one may cheerfully
admit that Amalia and Leonora, the Macgregors,
the Dogs, and Cristofolo are some compensation
for the recent arrival of two misguided coOn
melodists. Also La Danse de Rubans—a sym-
bolic atonement by Rickards’ ethereal ballerinas
—is still firmly planted in the First Part. Up to
now, The Bulletin has ignored La Danse de R.,
as its exacting gaiety and stern reality seemed to
belong not to Melbourne, but to Geelong.
From the day when N. S. Wales first had
a Forestry Department, down to the end of its
last financial year, all that the State has got out
of its fine forests of cedar and matchless hard-
wood and other timber, is £579,717; and of that
all but £154,796 has gone in expenses. In the
balance against that trifle millions of millions of
feet of timber have been swept away, a little of it
to market but most of it to ashes; last year alone,
for instance, the Department kept track of
650,000,000 super, feet that went to market, and
there was probably as much more that was de-
stroyed. If N. S. Wales doesn’t get down to
husks it will have a lot more luck than the other
young ass mentioned in Luke xv. 11.
AT POVERTY POINT.
“Adelaide”: It is always interesting-to look for evi-
dence of the time and place of first issue in a play ; and
how lovingly performers cling to the moss-grown. “ The
Belle of Mayfair ” was produced when London was half full
of Frenchmen, and “ Entente Cordiale ” holds good as a
rattling chorus ; but when Miss Ruth Lincoln is dis-
covered to be singing an encore verse of “ Bond Street”
in French, the thing has an effect as of last week’s news-
paper. Brett, by the way, says a meaningless thing
about his front door, which you pull to open and push to
shut; “we call it the push and the pull.” (Loud silence.)
Surely it was intended to be “ We call it the Old Pull and
Push.” Does Mr. Brett imagine that we don’t know the
Bull and Bush in Australia ?
“ J.S.” : Edward Reeves, who recited Dickens’ “Christ-
mas Carol ” at the Masonic Hall, Melbourne, on Friday
night last, is by no means entrancing, and is often not
even interesting. True, he has most of the qualities that
pass for talent in elocution, but, in the lengthy work to
which he is addicted, he at times loses his grip, and drifts
into indifferent and almost colorless declamation. Also,
he selected a yarn whereof the humor and pathos, when
poured forth with garrulous unction, seem thin and
puerile. The weighty English gusto with which Dickens
describes the pleasures of a solemn and portentous Christ-
mas feed is imitated by Reeves with devoted sincerity.
Gambolling among the phrases of gastronomic descrip-
tion ; dwelling with tender cooings upon England’s noble
pudding; and almost choked by fat, oleaginous
adjectives, Reeves appears to halt between mild frenzy
and asphyxiation—or else asparagus. Then, indeed, he
has the characteristics of a good showman, but, at other
times, listening to him is something in the nature of a
penance, though due respect must be paid to his fine feat
of memory.
“ J.D.”: Observing Marcus Superbus at Sydney Theatre
Royal last Saturday night what time he decided to offer
himself up publicly as a course at the lions’ dinner party,
it was borne in on me that he recalled someone I ligd
met'recently. Who? Suddenly it flashed upon me.
Of course. Joe Grim, the iron pug from Philadelphia
((J.S. A.). Both theattitudes, and the attitude towards life,
of the Old Roman are those that the modern Roman
would have exploited had he been born a sufficient
number of centuries earlier. Grim has for years pasc been
voluntarily throwing himself to the lions of the only arena
we have these squeamish times. It is no fault of his that
he hasn’t been eaten up. It has been Joseph’s custom
since he was quite a lad to stand in the public eye in a
Superbus-like pose for as long as possible before submit-
ting himself to be chopped ; and while so doing to orate
concerning the coming repast. Those few splendid
moments were all the change worth mentioning that he
ever got out of his place of honor on the menu. Verily, Joe
would have made an ideal convert to early Christianity.
None would have railed against informers, lEmperors,
Coliseum-frequenters and the like more spiritedly than
he ; none would have chaffed the Bestiarii with a finer
air; and, finally, when the dinner gong went, he would
have blunted and ruined the Nubian lion’s teeth in all
likelihood, and thus been enabled to do his act all over
again at the next Games.
“ Sceneshifter ” anent a classic' story : Tree’s recent
production of “Faust” is the third version London has
seen in the last 50 years. Chatterton produced “Faust ”
at Drury Lane in 1866, and Irving at the Lyceum in the
’eighties. It was a money-maker with both managers,
and was frequently revived. Samuel Phelps, then at the
height of his glory, took the rdle of Mephistopheles in the
Chatterton verson, and his son Edmund was Faust. Mar-
guerite was played by Mrs. Hermann Vezin. Valentine
was made a singing r6le—not a baritone, as in Gounod’s
opera, but a tenor. W. H. Harrison, famous in his day in
connection with the Harrison-Pyne Opera Co., and the
original Thaddeus in Balfe’s “ Bohemian Girl,” essayed
this r6le, and he had a fine song in “ The Fairest Girl may
have a Scornful Eye,” borrowed from Weber’s opera
“ Euryanthe.” Bayle Bernard, a London pressman, was
responsible for the “book,” and he made a big feature of
“ The Walpurgis Night on the Brocken,” but he “cut”
the cellar scene and the witch’s kitchen. The latter
scene was incorporated later on in Irving’s version, which
was from the pen of melodramatist W. H. Wills. Irving
had George Alexander as his Faust. Ellen Terry made an
incomparable Margaret. Barrels of money were emptied
in connection with the mounting, and old Nuremberg
seemed to live again on the Lyceum stage. Tree’s edition
(it is by Comyns Carr and poet Stephen Phillips) is in four
acts, and seems to pretty well follow the conventional
story. A departure is in the prologue, programmed as
“Betwixt Earth and Heaven,” wherein visions of Cleo-
patra, Helen of Troy, Messalina, and other naughty his-
torical or unhistorical ladies appear during the Brocken
scene. Marie Lohr, S. W. Anson, and Madge Titheradge
are in the cast.
“ Harrison O.” : Since the advent of Maud Allan, who
clothes herself mostly in a smile and a few heads, the in-
habitants of the other side have been simply trampled
under the twinkling feet of charming bare-footed (and,
the Puritans allege, bare-faced) dancing girls. They
mostly call themselves Salome, and they are all suffi-
ciently “ nood ” to bring deep blushes to a Maoriland
policeman. In London at present Maud Allan and Vitus
St. Denis and Artemis Calonna are all dancing d la
Milo. In America every music hall, from the Alhambra
and Hammerstein’s Roof Garden down to the little third
and fourth-rate variety theatres, has a Salome tart of its
own somewhere about the premises ; no Yankee vaude-
ville show is considered complete without one, any more
than Rickards’ would be without a rag-time melody or
the person who “ will now endeavor, with your kind per-
mission, to give a few imatations of artists I have seen
and heard.” Hilda Caroli, Vera Olcott, La Sylphe, Eva
Tanguay, Lotta Faust, and La Belle Zola are ’only a few
of a great army of damsels who find bare dancing pro-
fitable in Uncle Ham’s land at the present time. In Hing-
land J. Bull starts to make excuses ; he calls it “classical
dancing,” and says that it is “beautiful” and “inspir-
ing,” and Bull’s press devotes columns to criticising its
“feeling,” “chasteness,” “influence,” “suggestion,”
and so forth. If a man enjoys gazing at slightly-clad
girls, let him do so and shut up, but your smug Briton
will never admit that it is the lack of clothing which
attracts him —it is always something higher.
“Tiki”: The Australasian society for the encourage-
ment of the locally-built opera is going to be responsible
for a lot in Maoriland. There was a tendency in that
direction before the society crystallised ; but the symp-
toms grow more alarming, and every tenth person one
meets has in his eye a wild, operatic look. The first local
production of the kind was the Hill-Birch opera, “A
Moorish Maid,” which the Stevenson co. annexed. Then
arrived “ The Lady Typist,” entirely the work of T.
Humphreys, of Auckland ; followed by the society’s first
purchase, “ The Tea Girl,” by the same composer. She
proved to be rather a tame young person, and passed
without sensation into the Pollard repertoire, where she
is reported to be improving. Now a Humphreys-Birch
opera, founded on a Peruvian legend, and called “The
Golden Wedge,” is heralded as a great and marvellous
work. It also goes Pollardwards. There’s also a musical
comedy by T. Humphreys and Miss Peacocke, of Auck-
land, reported to be off in the same direction. It is called
“Sixes and Sevens,” and when staged here last week
proved a rather uninteresting effort, redeemed consider-
ably by bright music and good acting. Finally, we are
promised a Maori grand opera by Alfred Hill and J.
Cowan, of Wellington. A Hill-Oowan combination should
do something worth while in the wide field of Maori
legend. This list, with the Hill-Adams “Hinemoa” and
a Hill-Anderson cantata, “Tura and the Fairies,” about
completes the list of local efforts to date. But the results
of the Australasian society’s competition for this year are
not yet announced, and meanwhile the opera-builder
builds feverishly.

“Topog”: Memories of Musgrove’s sumptuous revival
ol As You Like It at the Sydney Royal in 1903, were
revived by the warblers at the Cri. in the production of
“ The Lily of Killarney.” Two of the fine “ front ” cloths,
painted by Hawes Craven for the pastoral comedy, were
resurrected to illustrate Benedict’s work. The exterior
of Eily s cottage is the Arden Forest scene in which, five
years ago, old Adam evinced a strong desire to lie down
and die, until cheered on by Orlando. The exterior of
Myles’cottage was Scene 2, Act 2 of Mussy’s production,
and shows the home of good old Adam, where “ from
seventeen years till now almost four score,” the old joker
lived, until he went off with Orlando to Arden. A Melb.
daily waxed eloquent the other week over these scenes,
and regretted that the management did not bill the
artist’s name. But it didn’t want to.
The moral slump of the London music-hall continues.
Harry Fragson, the enormously-paid Ariglo-Parisian
comedian, is allowed to sing at the Tivoli a son«- about
taking a girl out to lunch and dinner, to be home at nine
—the final suggestion being to include breakfast and
make it 9 a.m. Meanwhile, the “ Morality Department ’’
in New York has got busy with the imported posters of
the London Gaiety Co., and has pasted strips of paper
over the too-exuberant upper sections of some of the
ladies. Everybody knows that section. Legend has it
that once, on a P. and O. steamer, it broke loose or poured
oyer in the case of an evening dress dame who was cut
distinctly low, and that the officer alongside, with great
presence of mind, pushed the exuberance back into place
with a soup spoon.
“ Kodak ” : I find the stage ancient too decrepit. A cer-
tain amount of feebleness, of coarse, is necessary, but
the average player of old-man parts totters, wheezes,
blinks and squeaks till the character he is sustaining be-
comes a mere tiresome bundle of whiskers and rags. I
hate to see an old man hobble on to the stage and sit
down on the property log in front of the hut. I know
that he’s going to wheeze and giggle and mumble an old
clay pipe ; also that he is going to indulge in a fiendishly
k ,T IOt ; e ° f ' Vh '° h is ’ ‘) A J I ; them was

grammar and his stagger, and I am infuriated by his dia-
bolical slowness. Some night I will rise up and throw a

A Mystery Act has been baffling Londoners of late,
beside which the darkest magic of the Maskelyne and
Devant combine looks, practically speaking, piebald. It
is shown at the Alhambra Theatre by a Mrs. Tomson, of
America. The act is a new version of the time-worn
cabinet puzzle. The carptnter of whatever theatre the
lady shows at builds a cabinet. He puts it together in
full view of the audience, to guard against deception.
Then the structure and Mrs. Tomson are examined care-
fully by a committee of females taken at random from
the audience. When they are all satisfied that the thing
is solid, empty and apertureless, and that Mrs. Tomson of
America has nothing but the ordinary gauds of femininity
about her, the lady climbs inside and is nailed up. She
is released after a while, and emerges with birds, rabbits,
cats and flowers all over her, as it were, and in a com-
pletely different costume from that which she wore before
being coffined. It is the only act of the sort that the
weedy lad in the stalls is not able to resolve on sight for
the benefit of the lady friend at his side. All the leading
“ mystery ” exposers have tried their hands on the
Tomson deception, and retired flummoxed.
“Busk”: Scribner's Magazine, just to hand, has a
striking article on Richard Mansfield. The hardships he
encountered were, to a great extent, due to his over-
powering and Napoleonic confidence in his own future
greatness. He firmly believed in the principle that the
good is the enemy of the best. So he dashed the cup
from his lips because it was not good enough. His mother
was Mrs. Rudersdorff, a well-known operatic prima donna;
his father was an Englishman. He went from America to
London and under-
went incredible ex-
periences. Securing
an engagement with
the German Reeds,
he fainted at the
piano through hung-
er, and was dis-
missed, A year’s
travel as Sir Joseph
Porter, in “H.M.S.
Pinafore,” put him
somewhat on his
legs. He was the
original Major-
General Stanley, in
“The Pirates,” at a
copyright perform-
ance. He invented
the patter song im-
promptu there, and
it was afterwards
embodied in the
score. Yet Gilbert
set his face against
Mansfield, as one
who would not stick
to the prescribed
business. Mansfield
went to America.
More hardships.
Made a hit in Phila-
delphia as the ori-
ginal Lord Chancel-
lor there, in “ lo-
lanthe,” a perform,
ance that was on a
par with Bob
Brough’s. Mansfield
sprained his ankle

to New York, on
pure spec. He just
had a faint memory

were rehearsing “ A

was in 1883. Both Miss
Carey and De Belleville had
been familiar in Australia. Mans-

he would infallibly have been out of it hail
he not made a pretence of agility, in great pain, before
manager Palmer. He was engaged for a little part—
luckily a sitting-down part—and had a chance to get
well. It was a one-man play, the man being Baron de
Chevrial, an old rou6 who fell 'dead at a grand supper
given to a herd of actresses. Stoddart, the best actor,
was cast for it, and quailed before it. He was seized

sa3'ing “Mansfield is the man.” So Mansfield got it.
Then comes the triumph, the most notable burst forth
of a new great actor ever known on the American
stage. One reads of it with a share in the exultation,
as with Irving in “The Bells.” Irving would have
missed that if Bateman could have succeeded in replac-
ing him with George Rignold.
Quite the most popular feature at Melbourne Gaiety is
the weekly trial matinde, where aspirants for music-hall
fame are trotted out. Generally a large crowd turns up
to these events, for they are certainly funnier than the
legitimate show. The trial habit is spreading. Harry
Rickards is now not above sandwiching an unannounced
trial turn into his bill at the Opera House. On one or
two occasions lately, an unpublished turn has been in-
cluded in the show, just to see how the audience would
take it. That is a radical change for the House of
Rickards, which is conservative even to the extent of
hanging on to its ancient and unimposing first part with
all the force of the average English Liberal hanging on to
Cobdenism. Nobody likes Rickards’ first part; but all
put up with it even as they do with dogs, dust, and bad
beer.
“0.K.": The imported English provincial ballad
amounts to a new infliction upon Australian concert
audiences. Songs about England and her Glory are as
permissible, of course, as songs about Ireland, Scotland
and Wales, and their national pride and greatness. But
the modern English county-advertisement ditty is some-
thing that we ought to be spared. We have done nothing
to deserve it. When the Australian in London goes to
one of Chappell and Co.’s concerts (at which expensive
artists receive fat fees for warbling Chappell and Co.’s
latest and most vapid publications) he must be prepared
to suffer “ A Song of Surrey,” “ A Lilt of Lincolnshire,”
“ Adorable Dorset,” or what not in that puerile provincial
way. Such ditties are intended to tickle the ears of the
native groundlings from Surrey, Lincolnshire or Dorset.
But when the Australian in Australia goes to hear Ada
Crossley and Co., he doesn’t bargain for the imported
baritone to sing praises to “Devon, glorious Devon.”
The foreign provincial ballad is most uncalled for.
England has 40 county divisions, every one of which is the
subject of a tuneful panegyric, no doubt. But we don’t
want them, any more than the inhabitants of Gloucester-
shire want Ada Crossley to come back and sing about the
Glories of her dative Gippsland.
“ S.” : One leading lady in Australia has in her train an
unknown sister, who much resembles her, and when a
part grows plain, stale, flat, or wearisome, the popular
actress steps out, and the unknown sister steps in. So
far, no audience has discovered the imposition. The
undistinguished sister does the deathbed scenes, and
when the exigencies of the drama call upon the heroine
to disguise herself temporarily as a snuffy old beldame, or
some other unpleasant character, she often rests in her
dressing-room for that act and lets the sister beldame in
her stead. In other professions the “ ghost ” is not un-
common, but a useful, steady-going, all-round ghost of
this kind is rare.
A BALLADE OF HIPS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Ah, gone the snows of yester-year !
And vanished is last winter’s rain 1
All things are just one moment here ;
The next —and Life itself is slain !
But thus, when all things wax and wane,
Must Women, too, but waste away?
Can’t you your rounded selves remain ?
Where are the Hips of yesterday?
One day at tailor-mades you sneer,
And to the Empire waist attain ;
The next, you tailor-maids appear.
But how those luscious curves restrain ?
And yet they’re gone—that’s very plain !
Though Fashion’s nods you must obey,
Methinks, if stout, such modes must pain !
Where are the Hips of yesterday ?
Once b6b6-ribbons used to peer
From every bosom. I complain
I cannot find them, though quite near
I look for them, in vain, in vain !
But how that slenderness you gain ?
How melt those rounded curves ? O, say !
This problem still defies my brain—
Where are the Hips of yesterday !
L’Envoi :
Mesdames ! your Hips will come again,
Since e’en the new stays do not stay.
Your iron-clad corsets may explain
Where are the Hips of yesterday !
N. S.W.
ARTHUR H. ADAMS.

PENEOLDS
WINES
Melbourne’s City Fathers have again had
the hawker nuisance thrust under their probosces.
Seemingly the ancients do not know how to
treat it, or they postpone action from sheer
force of habit. Then again they may not
desire to be ’arsh, as one or two of them
might put it; but certainly they will have
to do something some day. The barrowmen
who were wrenched off Princes Bridge have
grown itinerant, and now glide calmly into Col-
lins-street, Melbourne’s most precious thorough-
fare. The raucous local person and the lucid coun-
tryman of P. Pilate now pursue Society itself with
24 bananas in a bag for 3d. Moreover, they have
.taken to clustering before the street’s most im-
pressive edifice, and to impeding the traffic before
-the building’s still more impressive entrance.
'Therefore, the representative of the proputty has
.given a vocal recital that has aroused the Munici-
pal Dads some. So the matter is now beneath
rtheir probosces, as already mentioned, and some-
thing drastic will be expected daily up till
U 990, A.n.
A while back a Melbourne lumper stuck
his dog-hook into a case of alleged curios, landed
from a ship just arrived from China, and marked
for transhipment to a foreign country. After the
dog-hook had been withdrawn, a dark brown
“curio” began to ooze out on the wharf; so the
case was examined, and found to contain £4OOO
worth of opium in tins. Having waited patiently
for some six weeks, the bland Customs Depart-
ment now asks why the owner doesn’t come
forward and claim his property. But as the mere
possession of the drug involves a heavy penalty
as well as confiscation, the aforesaid owner, sus-
pected to be one Ah Fat, continues to maintain a
most enthusiastic silence.
<*- * • *
In Adelaide, at the Court of Industrial
Appeals, Justice Gordon has been trying to find
out what “ a living wage ” is ; and when, after a
long hearing, the parties came into court and said
that they had agreed to call it 7s. a day, the chirpy
Judge looked like a man who had just been found
not guilty of 17 awful crimes. Up to then his
Honor had been getting all sorts of advice ; the
last adviser who got into the witness-box before
the agreement was come to assured the Judge
that lie earned £1 a week and was “happy with
his lot.” Certainly his lot consisted only of a
wife and no children. But even so the Judge was
interested in getting him to go into details. This is
how he did it—Rent (two rooms), 3s. 6d.; groceries,
2s. 6d.; bread, Is. 9d.; butter, Is. 4d. ; milk, 10|d.;
meat, 4s. ; fuel, Bd. ; vegetables. Is. fid. ; lighting,
4£d.; tobacco, Is. 3d.; fruit and drink, Is. 6d. ;
boots and clothes, 2s. That looked like 21s. 3d.,
but perhaps there was something wrong about
the calculation. Anyhow, if you cut out the
butter it is 19s. lid. ; and 19s. lid. would leave
as much as a penny, which he no doubt cut up
among theatres, cricket-club subscriptions, news-
papers and literature, trips home to see mother,
doctors and chemists, crockery (the handles will
come off the cups somehow), furniture, seed for
the canary, and stamps. With what is left he
gets his hair cut, and buys “a little thing for the
wife ” at Christmas. It’s marvellous what you
can do with a penny. ,
“Jean Sibi” complains :
We who live in the neighborhood of Port Melbourne
have little excitement. Footscray has lost its punch, and
the old familiar odor of hides and perished trotters is
almost gone. The Port itself is getting clean, and ex-
changing its fabulous but often picturesque grime for a
smug, middle-class polish. The smudged dwellings, the
squalid streets with their predatory tikes and plots of
refuse, are acquiring a false bloom. Fresh paint and
scraps of garden suggest paint on an ancient dandy’s
countenance. People absorb less beer, and are becoming
demoralised with cleanliness, and stunned with raw reli-
gion. The Port is decadent now. Take Bay or Graham
street after 8 p.m. on a Saturday night—preposterously
respectable, mostly women shopping; an occasional yob
with his fangs buried in a mouth organ ; and rarely a
fight-or a reeling stevedore. The Army, with its brazen
cadge, its cheerfulness, its mawkishness and bad hymn
tunes, holds forth on a bit of vacant land, where
once a picturesque hoodlum full of beer used
to curse at large. The river improvements have almost
abolished the Yarra floods, which formerly washed tons
of strange old substances to our doors. Then the sea
around the Port was a marvel, a curiosity-shop, a museum,
an old clo’ emporium—anything odd and wonderful. But
now, what is it I—justl—just water, or, at its best, thin Irish
stew topped with the offal from ships’ galleys 1 Thus we
have little excitement. Even the Powerful, which brought
Mr. Poore, the exquisite Cup deadhead, round our way,
failed to interest us. It certainly fired a salute of about
914 guns nearly every time Mr. Poore went ashore in the
Admiral’s barge to buy a stamp or get his hair cut; but
as it was burning cordite like that most of the time it was
at anchor it became a weariness. No—the Port isn’t
what it was.
■* * * *
Some of the Sydney crowds that recently
laughed at Petruchio’s way of taming a shrew —
by starving her—probably didn’t know that that
is still the method adopted in N. S.
Wales gaols. Since the new “ phil-
anthropic ” system was introduced
they don’t crack the whip to any
great extent —anyway, not to the
extent that the shrew-tamer does
in W. Shakspeare’s comedy—but
starvation is part of the regular
menu of N. S. Wales prisoners in
some classes. The State’s gaol-
reformers have evidently the same
ideas as Mr. Bumble with regard
to sin and victuals; wherefore,
when any young N.S.W. person
now shows that he or she has got a
mental kink somewhere, the
N.S.W. prison idea is to starve it
out. Ninety-nine out of every 100
people will have reckoned that
Shak. was pulling the public’s leg
when he represented Katharine
as being quite cured by this pro-
cess; the odd one who takes it seriously is the
Prison Department. Its methods are just the
same as those of the boss whip-cracking humorist
in the comedy.

“ Snell” : The A.N.A. intends running a
literary branch in connection with its annual
Melbourne exhibition, and Australian authors
and publishers are invited to provide exhibits.
The idea is to bring before the thousands who
visit the exhibition a striking display of books
made in Australia. It is a%ood idea and a worthy
object. Up to now the A.N. A. has been markedly
indifferent to Australian brains in book form.
Libraries are run in connection with A.N.A.
branches, and a visitor must rummage far and
wide to discover in them evidence of the existence
of anything in the shape of Australian literature.
I went through one library in a populous centre
the other da3 r , and the only Australian book it
contained was a tattered copy of a volume of
Ernest Favene’s short stories.
“ Goderich,” Bayswater-road, Sydney, for
40 years the home of Mrs. Charles Smith, who
died there recently, was built in 1838 by Sheriff
M'Quoid, who suicided on the premises. In its
early days it looked down William-street, the
frontage on the western side not being built upon.
The old mansion has not had many tenants.
Bishop Broughton was one of the earliest. The
executors under Captain Smith’s will are having
the ground cut up into building allotments, which
will front Woolcot-street and Bayswater-road.
The fine old home will be allowed a small area
and, like most of the homes of “ our old Australian
nobility,” will become a hash-turret.

“Nulla”: Notwithstanding the spread
of the beer drought in Maoriland, a recently-

on the increase in the pious isles. Which fact
would seem to indicate that Maoriland is putting
down the curse with such a firm hand that it

THE STORMY PETROL.
“W.”: The Bulletin’s suggestion
(19/11/’08) that every house should have a charac-
ter from the health authorities, which owner or
agent must produce to the prospective tenant
before tenancy is accepted, is good and righteous
altogether. There comes under my notice daily
“ two rooms and a kitch,” at 10s. a week, whither
and whence the procession of victimised tenants is
constant. The house looks all right, but you’ve
got to live in it to know it. Then the artistic
dado in dark colors round each room proves mere
daubing to hide the damp stains rising from jerry-
built foundations ; and a spell of wet weather
turns the premises into a suite of oozy caverns.
To induce people of small or any other means to
go to the expense of moving in and out of such a
trap for sickness is mere swindle, robbery, and
everything else of the kind that Eat is addicted
to. Arise, some legislator, get things altered ; and
the poor people will call thee blessed, and the
landlord will curse thy name for ever and a day !
“ Hiphen ” : The law is as uncertain as
horse-racing, and a good deal more expensive.
And sometimes the law has its dead heats. At
Korumburra (Vic.), last week, grazier No. 1 sued
wool-raiser No. 2 for £99 damages over a matter
of trespass of sheep. Mutton-grower No. 2 sued
lamb-rearer No. 1 for similar damages for a similar
crime committed by his woolly goods. The c l® al ’r
headed Law couldn’t separate them, so awarded
£5 damages and Costs to each of the sheep-
nourishers. Thus the sheep-magnates are just
where they were, barring the loss of time and the
flurry and the Costs. The lawyers have got the
Costs.
“ Hassan ” : There
is an idea abroad that,
next to getting married,
getting locked up is the
easiest thing in the
world. In Melbourne at
least, unless you answer
a very young constable
back, you will find it one
of the most difficult. At
about one o’clock the
other night I happened
to have business in one
of the suburban watch-
houses. As I approached
the door, a forlorn soul
with a breath that light-
ened the gloom clutched
me desperately by the
arm, and some of his
breath fell down my
back. “For God’s sake
lock me up,” he pleaded,
“ away from these.” He
hung on to me, and I
dragged him to the pre-
sence of the watchhouse-
keeper. “Ah ! ” ex-
claimed the Breath, as
he caught sight of the
uniform. “You’ll lock
the spiders out, con-
stable.” “He’s been
here four toimes this
night,” explained the
constable in charge. “Oi
don’t know what to do
wid him, at all, at all.”
He turned to the Breath.
“Go out an’ lie in the
street for a whoile —thin
a polaceman will bring
ye in an’ charge ye.”
“I can’t find a police-
man, ” wailed the Breath.
“Nothing but spiders.”
“Take thim away fr’m
here, thin. Take thim
to Sir Thomas Oar-r-
-michael! Take thim to
Hell! Is it me that’s
goin’ to char-rge ye, an’ to come down here in the
mor-rnin’ to give ividence? Here” The
watch house-keeper pulled out sixpence. “Take
ut an’ get a bed at the doss, an’ wrop yure-
silf up well so the spidhers can’t get under-
neath.” The wreck took the coin and faced
the spiders again. And I have in memory
another case. A sergeant was standing on the
edge of the pavement in Latrobe-street when a
gentle stranger approached, politely asking for a
night’s lodging in the cells. The thing couldn’t
be done, the sergeant declared. “I can’t lock
you up unless you have committed an offence.”
“What sort of offence?” inquired the stranger,
mildly. “ Oh, any sort. Bust a window, assault
somebody,” he was told. He hauled off, hit the
sergeant fair in the eye, followed it up with a
right uppercut to the jaw, fell on top of the
officer, and bit his ear. Three constables ran to
the scene, dragged him off, and assisted their
superior to his feet. “I suppose that’ll do,” said
the stranger. “ Put plenty of blankets in my
cell ; I think it’s going to be cold before morning.”

“ Wealthy Willie ” : A something in the
way of records is about to happen in Melbourne’s
semi-starched suburb of haughty commercials and
partially-bloated softgoodsmen —the simple, not
unpleasant village of Malvern. This something
is to be a double wedding, in which a fond mamma
will wed her Percy (or Hercules, or whatever it is),
while her loving daughter will, on the same day
and within the same sacred portals, march up to
the altar and snatch her own devoted William (or
Henry, as the case may be). Malvern is getting
on. these days, and will be quite a gentleman’s
suburb in time, if the local sensations can be kept
down to homely happenings such as this. Mal-
vern’s trying really hard, nowadays, and doing
rather well so far.

“ Petifi ” : Certain Bulletin references to
the unlucky mortals who have to stagger through
life with such names as “Barnum Maffick Smith,”
and “ Sperry Columbia Star-spangled Jones,” re-
call the only time I ever used profane language.
I asked a romantic matron friend of mine what
she was going to call the latest immigrant from
Storkland, and she gushingly replied : “ Oh ! I’ve
got such a beautiful name for the darling pet—
Loris! So poetic, isn’t it? Reminds you some-
how of Edgar Allan Poe, and moonlight on the
mystic Rhine, and the raven that said ‘Never-
more,’ and sweet, appealing things like
that, doesn’t it ? Loris !—so romantic, I think.’
Later, I descended into a dictionary, and then I
swore. For that child was a blue-eyed, golden-
haired cherub, and to name it after a blastiferous
lemur that scratches itself up a tree in Ceylon
was an outrage and ten different kinds of a curse.
EXCITEMENT IN HIGH LIFE.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
On a night of frosty splendor in mid-January, Mayfair,
the sanctmh of London’s creme de la creme of society,
was excited over an event of more than ordinary interest,
even for that charmed circle of fashion and wealth. —
Opening sentence of Sydney Evening News new shocker
15/ ISf 08.
Lo ! ’tis Mayfair !
The “ sanctum ” where
The social “ cream ”
’Mid bliss supreme
Quenches the first
Sharp pangs of thirst
In B. and S. and champagne cup ;
And afterwards sits down to sup,
And, having madly eaten up
All of the food,
Both bad and good,
That erstwhile stood
The board upon,
Mutters “ It’s gone.
Now let us don
Kid gloves and dance,
And caper, prance,
Waltz, polk and lance.”
See ! They’ve begun 1
What gorgeous fun,
Mark how they run
From place to place,
And leap and race.
What wondrous grace
Hark to that yell!
A duchess fell.
A silent spell
Ensues the while they lever her
Into a seat, and disinter
A medico. Experts aver
Her grace’s need
At utmost speed
Is whisky neat.
All hands repeat
The words. They treat
The aged soul
To alcohol
Out of a bowl.
The revelry
Restarts. Hours fly
Right merrily,
And so does meat
And things to eat
Like fowls, pigs’ feet,
Rolls, soup and jam.
Resounds a slam,
Likewise a “Damn,”
And an old peer is seen to bound
High in theair, screaming “you Hound 1"
While—bouncing off his skull—two pound
Of trotters whirl
And smite an earl,
Whose anguished skirl
Rings through the hall
Convulsing all.
* * •
And now night’s pall
Has vanished ;
The sky grows red,
Everywhere dead
Marines are spread.
The food is fled.
Well-wined and fed
The guests are led
To Home and Bed.
N.S.W.

309-311 GEORGE STREET, SYDNEY.
One of the misfortunes of Australia is the
possession of the Kelly family. T. H. Kelly, of
Sydney, alderman and so forth, plays the violin
occasionally, and apparently believes that he is an
authority on the subject of music generally and
organ music in particular. So the said Kelly,
who possibly mistakes the organ for a whistle,
urges Sydney Municipal Council to send to
Europe, England preferred, for “an organist of
repute” at a salary of £3OO per annum. The
salary is absurd, and no organist of repute is going
to cut himself loose from his special environment
for such a miserable salary. If Mr. Kelly’s pro-
posal is adopted it means that Sydney will get a
fourth or fifth-rate man whose chances of rising in
the land of his birth were practically nil, and
will boom him at the expense of Sydney Munici-
pal Council. The position of City Organist
should be one of the prizes of the Australian
musical profession, for there are, in proportion
to population, many organists of repute in
Sydney. When the subject arose of appointing a
successor to organist Mason, the applicants boiled
down to Messrs. Mason, Sykes and W. H. Wale,
and of these three the last was the greatest.
However, Mason was reappointed after a lot of
wrangling, and within a few weeks he resigned to
take up a position in the London office of the
S. M. Herald at £6OO per annum. Then the
position had to be faced again. Instead of appoint-
ing either Sykes or Wale, fresh applications were
called, and 18 were received. The Council decided
that the whole 18 applicants should be allowed a
chance to show what they could do on the great
instrument, and they were given a month each.
The last of the 18, Mr. Tearne, is now officiating,
and when the Council met to decide which
of them had won the position, Mr. Kellv
proposed once more that it should get
an “organist of repute from England,” at
£3OO per annum. Fortunately, however, the
Council demurred. It felt that it ought to
give local musicians a show, especially after get-
ting them to play its organ for 18 months. And
to pass over a man with the qualifications of Wale
m order to buy a pig in a poke in Europe was folly.
Wale is a Bachelor in Music of Oxford, a Fellow
of the Royal College of Organists, and for years a
member of the council of that distinguished
musical body, a man of much experience and a
well-known writer on musical subjects in many
papers, including the Organists’ Quarterly
Journal. He has been examiner in music
at the universities of Adelaide and Mel-
bourne, and was chosen by the great
English organ-makers, Beard, Norman & Co., to
superintend the fitting up of their organs in Aus-
tralia on account of his technical knowledge. Dr.
Turpin, hon. sec. of the Royal College of Organ-
ists and Warden of Trinity College, London,
said, among many other eulogistic things: “It
would be quite impossible to name a better or
more fully qualified organist for the position in
question.” Yet, in the face of all these arguments,
Mr. Kelly, who plays the violin occasionally, pro-
poses to go to Europe “for an organist of repute.”
It is the breaking out of the Importing Disease
agam, and in one of its most horrible forms.

Besides serving sentence after sentence plaintiff had

the respect of the gaol officials.

pressed himself strongly in favor of allowing

noblest in the language. “ She
was a woman of whom any
man would be proud,” per-
sisted John.
SURE OF ONE THING.
HE: "So you think I'm too poor to marry you .
Hare you a heart .?”
SHE: “I don’t know so much about a heart , but
I know I’ve got an appetite!”
The old tale of the man who turns up after
a long absence to find his grass-widow another
man’s wife, was told again at Gympie (Q.) the
other day. The first husband left his wife, and
stayed away for two or three years. His better
fraction concluded that the husband had grass
growing over him, so she married No. 2, to find
out later that No, 1 was still a matrimonial cir-
cumstance to be reckoned with. Then No. 2
turned dog on her, allegedly because his people
objected to the marriage ; and to her request for
a small sum to make baby-clothes, he submitted
an agreement ”by which she was to renounce all
claim on him, forbear to use his name again,
deliver up the marriage certificate, burn all cor-
respondence and promise never to put a spoke in
the wheel of his newer matrimonial ventures The
woman, however, wasn’t having any agreement,
and registered the child as illegitimate, which led
the Law to become inquisitive and charge her with
bigamy. The case was heard before Judge Rutledge,
who fell on No.. 1 and No. 2 with vehemence.
For leaving his wife and children without support
No. 1 (who was a witness) was a rascal arid a
scoundrel; and as for No. 2, the Court simply
cleaned up the premises and the back yard with
him. The jury then found the woman “not
guilty,” and the parties were left to sort them-
selves out with varied feelings.

“Ifcan”: Perishable foodstuffs, liable to
fly and dust infection, are still exposed for sale in
Sydney and suburban shops in a manner
which ought to shock even a New Hebrides
head-hunter. Not long ago a spasm of
virtue seized the City Council, and
the result was a measure of
protection to the public. For
instance, cooked goods are
now wrapped in white paper,
instead of in waste journals,
which may have first reposed
awhile in any old place.
After that the City Council
threatened to compel butch-
ers to wire-gauze their pre-
mises to keep the millions of
flies off the meat; but the
promise still stands dis-
honored. Legions of flies
sport from the gutter flotsam,
and the horse refuse in the
streets, and the sewer mouths,
and the ancient oyster-shells and.
the decadent fish in the open window
of Demetrius Steakopoulos, to the meat
and mutton and pork that repose and
dream hour after hour in a sweltering'
atmosphere, waiting for buyers. There
is no attempt worth mentioning to
yard off the insect enemy of mankind.
Besides that, every rustle in the air
disturbs the foetid dust, and sends
clouds of it swirling into shops to
spread disease and the dead feeling.
Evidently the municipal councils
have not the backbone to stand up
between the people and the common
or buzzing fly, while the Wade
Government is too busy being nasty
to the Commonwealth to attend to the matter.
* * * *
Two lots of people made a start at fish-
trawling in S. Australian waters the other day,
and both of them met wretched luck on the gang-
way, so to speak. The first steamer to go down
the Gulf from Port Adelaide was Captain Kay’s
Argyle. It did moderately well at the mouth of
the Murray, and then put off for the schnapper
grounds, where the first haul panned out 20l>
stingrays up to 6ft. across and a dogfish—good
enough stuff for the Scotch fishermen’s manure
works; only the manure works haven’t material-
ised yet, and meantime there is some prejudice
against stingrays, boiled, fried, or poached.
After that Kay s men had another try, and
the trawling net came to grief so badly that
the expedition had to get back to port.
The Scotch fishermen fared a lot worse. They
had chartered the steamer Ellen, which followed
the Argyle down the Gulf; but they hadn’t
fairly started before the boat was carried on to
the rocks and became a wreck. The accident
threw a large and heavy wet blanket over the
business; but the people interested are mostly
named Mac Something, and it usually takes more
one wet blanket to make people named
Mac Something turn tail and run.
An interesting trade-mark case cropped
up in the Melbourne Practice Court, t’other week,
the parties being rival manufacturers of soft
drinks. Dixon and Co., the plaintiffs, sought an
injunction to restrain the Australian firm of
Schweppes from using a colorable imitation of
their “0.T.” label, representing a red chilli on a
medallion. The defendants’ trade-mark was a
sprig of chillies on a medallion, in illustration of
the word “ Chillio,” and Justice Hodges decided
that both the pictorial design and the word which
emphasised it were plagiaristic. To the mind of
the, learned Judge, the letters “0.T.” in con-
duction with one red chilli conveyed the same idea
as “Chillio” and a cluster of the red-’ot beans,
therefore he granted a perpetual injuction to re-
strain Schweppes from using either the word or
the illustrative device. Or rather, it was the de-
fendants who agreed to an injunction, and the
Judge who formally approved of the settlement.
The increasing elasticity of the legal mind is very
noticeable in these trade-mark cases. Time was
when nothing short of an exact copy of an estab-
lished brand was accepted as a “ colorable imita-
tion.” Income cases the stealing of a well-known
name was permitted. “ Chlorodyne” was an in-
stance in point. Many years ago Dr. Collis
Browne, the originator of the name, applied for
an advt. monopoly of it, and the Judge ruled that
anybody was entitled to sell another brand of
chlorodyne in a bottle similar to that which had
been identified with the original stomachic.
* * * *
The Coronet, the quaint and serious yacht
that failed to meet with Sydney’s approval, hove-
to alongside Port Melbourne pier, where the mail-
boats stay, the other morning. The skipper and
Sand ford, the alleged owner, were a shade less
exclusive than when in the Ma State’s waters.
The sect which hides in the boat—the Holy
Ghost and Us, Us being presumably the crew—
possesses four or five other boats, which constitute
the “Kingdom Fleet,” or Yacht Club, and most
of them are presently expected to pervade Aus-
tralian ports. The sect, or firm, or combine, or
whatever it is, has an idea of “occupying lands”
in all five continents ; what it is going to do when
it occupies the lands it doesn’t explain. Its head-
quarters are at Shiloh, a sort of mixed monastery
with two “ praying turrets,” and every resident of
Shiloh has to take a turn in a turret. In fact,
they pray in shifts, night and day. Unfortu-
nately, this doesn’t seem to leave the necessary
time for cleaning up the back yard and digging
drains; so there have been two or three bad epi-
demics of diphtheria, etc., at Shiloh.
“ Gilrooney ” : Sydney may not have a
Melbourne Cup, or a Tommy Bent, or a Henry
Gainford, but it more than balances those enor-
mous deficiencies by possessing absolutely the best
tram system in the Commonwealth. To properly
appreciate the efficiency of the Sydney service one
has merely to make a martyr of himself on the
Melbourne and Adelaide tram-cars for a few
trips. In Melbourne the wretched snail-like cars
are painted in about 27 \ different colors, and they
never seem to be going anywhere in particular.
Two or three conductors or heroes (for they are
covered with badges) shove the go-carts around.
the curves, and then, with a shepherd’s crook,,
root about in a dark hole in the street for some- ■
thing they appear to have lost. Still, nobody
troubles, and the Melbourne man walks along to-
his office or his work without so much as glanc-
ing back to see if the multi-colored tor-
toise is following. But to go from Mel-
bourne to Adelaide is to go from Badl
to Worse. I went to a comedy show one night;.
Next day I patronised the horse-trams, after
which I felt like going to the first comedy show
and demanding my money back. No circus was
ever to be compared to those cars. To see the
two-storeyed vehicles run off the rails round the
curves, and the cool, Irvine- drivers
steering them back on again, makes a man forget
his sorrows. By the way, Adelaide’s main streets
are now thronged with workmen, who are laying
down the new electric tramways. I wouldn’t like
to be in the Holy City the day those cars start.
Something will happen. Such unprecedented
velocity coming all in one lump will take the
Religious Capital’s breath away. Worse than
that, the new cars will smite Adelaide in the
back, and it will be taken to the hospital to have,
its spine amputated.
TO THE SUMMER
GIRL
[FOR THE UBLLETIN.]
~JT W AVE I been blinded with excess of
§ m light 1
JL JL Have I been charmed with any
wild bird's whistle ?
Have I been routed in a fearsome fight.
Or merely stunned with some most grievous
missile ?
Or mocked by thee, thou sun-burnt butterfly,
Dear Summer Girl, good-bye!
Oh, shady Summer Hat! Oh, Golden Head /
Oh, flimsy frocking, far too faint for flounces !
They weighed you when your owner went to bed,
VIC.
And all you weighed was nearly
seven ounces.
Oh, honey lips and eyes extremely shy !
Dear Summer Girl, good-bye !
Should we two meet in the brown time to
come
(An unkind Fate sometimes such sport
arranges ),
Would you be sorrowful, would I be dumb ?
Sweetheart, the game is off, the order
changes,
A keen, cool wind is battling through the
sky.
Dear Summer Girl, good-bye!
SHAW NEILSON.
HOW MUCH WILL THIS STATE LOSE?
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
How much will this State lose ? Twelve months ago
all the wool on this side of the border was sent to Moree
to truck to Sydney, and the settlers trot their necessary
supplies back the same Way ; but this year some 6000
bales of New South Wales wool was sent over the border
to Goondiwindi and railed and sold in Brisbane, and
people obtained their supplies back the same way. The
same applies to stock. One settler, I hear, has ordered
trucks for 6000 fat sheep to be trucked to Brisbane at
stated intervals, owing to being unable to get trucks sup-
plied by our Railway Department without months of
waiting. We are spending New South Wales money to
benefit Brisbane by giving her our trade.— Letter to the
Sydney Telegraph (18/12/’08).
The bitter cry’s resounding
Throughout this hapless State ;
Intelligence astounding
Is published while you wait.
The Telegraph, unheeding,
Just prints the awful news—
“ To Queensland trade is speeding ”
How much will this State lose?
Prate not to us of barter
That benefits each man ;
Our Ma State is a martyr,
The victim of a plan !
The other States, combining,
Would rob her of her dues—
No wonder we’re repining—
How much will this State lose ?
Twelve months ago, right gladly,.
They trucked wool from Moree j
But now we miss it sadly—
It doesn’t reach the Quay !
A million bales come yearly
To Sydney’s ships and crews.
But not those prized most dearly—
How much will this State lose ?
O, come and raise a shindy,
And elevate old Cain !
Our wool’s in Goondiwindi
And won’t come back again 1—
The Brisbane shops are reaping
Our profits by a ruse—
Great Wade ! Why are you sleeping ?
How much will this State lose ?
O, call upon Carruthers
And let him hear our woe !
Our wicked Queensland brothers
No ruth or mercy show !
They’re grabbing all our money,
They’re sneaking all our ewes,
Our chickens, wheat and honey—
How much will this State lose l
Don’t talk about the Nation—
The Commonwealth you’ve made ;;
How dare our own relation
Compete with us for trade ?
He may be of our kidney,
But we decline to fuse !
Unless he deals in Sydney
How much will this State lose V
N.S.W.

The society has two funds—the assurance fund, which
now amounts to £5,245,103, and the investment fluctua-
tion reserve, from which £4691 has been off dur-
ing the past year, and which now stands at £79,723. The
progress of the society continues rapid. A comparison of
the assurance funds :
National
A.M.P. Mutual.
