Funds, present .. 23,446,497 5,245,102
Increase .. .. £8,966,919 £2,479,531
= 62p.c. = 90 p.c.
Of course the A.M.P. is much older, and consequently
the drain upon the funds on account of claims is propor-
tionately greater ; yet, allowing for that, a society which
does so well in comparison with the A.M.P.—naturally
the standard of insurance comparisons in Australia—must
prima f icie be regarded as having done very well indeed.
Still there are some doubts or at least qualifications.
In the first place, the National Mutual has been pursu-
ing business with very great energy in S. Africa, and it
is not certain yet how that will turn out. In the next
place, the society is understood to be doing a good deal
of without-medical-examination business ; and there are
some doubts about that, too. Just how it will turn out
depends very largely upon the care and thoroughness
with which inquiries are made, apart from the doctor.
The saving in the doctor’s bill is, of course, substantial.
In medical fees and expenses the A.M.P. last year paid
away £l5 3s. per cent, of the amount received in new-
business premiums ; the National Mutual paid only £7 11s.
per cent., or rather less than half as much. If the National
Mutual paid at the same rate as the A.M.P., its doctor’s
bill would be £7850 a vear more ; but, then, its income
might not grow quite' so rapidly. Neither, later on,
might the claims.
The expense rate is rather high, if it is calcu-
lated on the basis of total income, as in the following list:
Expenses Compared with Total Income.

But there are other things to be taken into account.
First, the amount of new business; for obviously an
office which does the most of that ought to have the
highest expense rate. Taking the old business to cost
7 per cent, of the premium income derived from it, and
the new single premium and annuity business to cost 3
per cent., then the rest of the new business has to bear
the balance of the cost; and on that basis the new
business would last year cost 116 per cent, of the
premiums collected—a slightly higher average than
for the previous year. Thus, though there is a shrinkage
in the expense rate calculated total income, the ex-
planation, or a great part of it, lies in the fact that both
actually and in proportion to total income, last year’s new
business was less than that of the previous year, when, on
the basis used above, the new business cost was 114 p.c. of
new premium income. This is by no means the best
rate in Australia. The A.M.P., which, on account of
its long life and high reputation, gets so much business
automatically, worked last year at a new business rate
of 85'6 per cent., calculated in the same way. Other
offices, some of them smaller, but doing Industrial business,
also show a lower Ordinary new-business rate ; and the
National Mutual will yet have to consider whether it will
not be wise to feed its Ordinary department in the same
way. The founder of the society, the late Colonel
Templeton, was understood to dislike the idea of an
Industrial department; but it may have to come..

Interest and rents are shown by this office “ after deduc-
tion of rates and taxes.” This, of course, reduces the
interest earnings, but it also reduces the expense rate.
Including rates and taxes as expenses, the rate moves up
from £l6 14s. 6d. to £l7 Bs. 7d. per cent, of total income ;
and the interest earnings gross are then £4 14s. lid. per
cent, of funds. The weakest feature of the finance is the
large proportion of the funds in mortgages and proper-
ties and the .small proportion in public securities.
The latter represent only 7*4 per cent, of total
assets, as against 31*6 per cent, in the A.M.P. and
587 per cent, in the Citizens’. Mortgages, on the
other hand, represent no less than 52’9 per cent,
and properties (including foreclosures) 157 per cent,
of the assets. The tendency of both the other offices
mentioned has been to increase the proportionate holding
of public securities and reduce that of properties and
mortgages ; so the position of the three offices now is .

The interest earnings are, of course, different —.National

£4 4s. lOd. But there are obvious advantages in a large
holding of public securities ; they mean smaller losses
and a lower expense rate. Apart from the greater security
which they give, it is almost certain that, oyer a range of
years, public securities, wisely purchased, give the office
at least an equal net return. However, the National
Mutual evidently takes a different view of the value of
mortcfaires, with the result that it has put up the propor-
tion of its assets held in that class of security from 49’8
p.c. in 1907 to the 52*9 p.c. shown above for 1908. One
consequence of the policy is that the society lias £118,093
of foreclosed property on its hands, as against £110,639
in 1907. ,

This bank begins to feel the backwash. Wool, copper,
tin, lead, apples—all had remarkably good markets at
the same time, and the bank shared in the prosperity
caused by the high prices of these Tasmanian staples.
Wool is still fetching something better than moderate
prices, but all the other industries are in trouble. The
Tasmanian tin-dredging boom is, in fact, dead and
buried ; it was a bad fizzle, but while it lasted it added a
few pounds to Tasmanian bank profits. Mt. Lyell still
does great things ; but probably Lyell itself isn’t worth
much to the Tasmanian bank, and the search for other
Lyells has eased off. Under the circumstances it wouldn’t
have b.*en at all surprising if the profits had tumbled a
good many steps further down the ladder. This com-
parison certainly suggests that the downward movement
is likely to go further: —
Nov., ’O7. Nov., ’OB.
Notes in circulation .. £66,089 £57,772 Dec. £8,317
Bills, etc 14,908 10,984 Dec. 3,924
Deposits at interest .. 250,382 261,728 Inc. 11,346
Deposits, free .. .. 314,542 253,789 Dec. 60,753
Liquid assets .. .. 155,173 111,639 Dec. 43,534
Advances .. .. 662,281 649,301 Dec. 12,980
Premises, etc. .. .. 28,268 27,214 Dec. 1,054
An increase of the deposits on which interest is called for,
a heavy reduction of free money, and a reduction of
advances point to smaller profits. Moreover, the advances
are even now greater than they ought to be. The bank
owes £584,273 and has only £111,286 of liquid assets—
only 19 per cent., which, so far as The Bulletin can
remember, is the smallest proportion of liquid assets held
by any Australian bank fora long while past. Unless there
are some very special circumstances which don’t lie on the
surface, this is trading on too narrow a margin altogether.
In the final resort, of course, the thing that counts most,
so far as the depositors are concerned, is the proportion
of shareholders’ capital in the business; and here the
position is quite satisfactory. For every £1 of the share-
holders’ money, the bank has less than £3 of the public’s.
Put in another way, the bank owes £584,273, of which it
has £111,639 in liquid assets, leaving £472,634 repre-
sented by non-liquid assets ; and those non-liquid assets
total £676,515. There is plenty of security, therefore ;
the only trouble is that, with so much of the free deposits
locked up in advances, a bank cannot meet any sudden
crisis. Also, when it works on such a narrow margin of
liquid assets inordinary times, it has to pass unusually'
profitably business that may come its way in times of
stress. Naturally, since it makes use of so much of the
current account money, its profits are rather high. On
the basis of capital and reserves, recent results have
been : .

But the profit on this scale is only being' made by taking
risks which the bank is hardly likely to continue.

This co. was the last of the Australasian fire and marine
offices to stand out of the limited liability provisions of
the modern Companies Acts ; but it, too, took advantage
of the law during the past year. Its subscribed capital
is £lOO,OOO ; half is paid, and the shareholders are liable
for the balance (£1 per share). The year has been a very
profitable one. The co. has a good marine connection,
and Australian marine business, turned out to be much
more profitable than fire business during the past 12
months —esnecially to those cos. that do business in
Maoriland and S. Australia (including Broken Hill). The
figures that follow present some comparisons :
Pre- Ex- Loss Expense
miums. Losses, penses. Rate. Rate.
1898.. £20,095 £30,419 £5llB 151‘4 p.c. 25’4 p.c.
1900.. 23,061 13,910 6115 60.3 „ 26'5 „
1905.. 19,795 8,208 6465 41'5 „ 327 „
1906.. 20,485 8,585 6477 41'9 „ 31'6 „
1907.. 21,275 12,835 6379 60'3 „ 29'9 „
1908.. 21,705 7,953 7159 36'6 „ 32'9 „
The loss rate is erratic, and so of course are the profits,
considering them year by year; that, however, follows
almost of necessity from the limited size of the business.
The profit for the past year was, in fact, better than is
shown by the figures in the first table ; but £4OO has
been written off securities and £lO6 has ‘been paid as a
special bonus to officers. So as riot to disturb
the expense-rate comparison, these charges have
not, however, been included in the second
table. Even without them the loss rate has moved
up, probably owing to the keener insurance competition
of the past year. Of the undivided profits (reserves)
£20,000 is now transferred to a contingency fund. The
object is not clear. Recognising that, in a business of
this size, the losses are liable to be much greater one year
than another, it may have been thought wise to establish
a sort of fluctuation fund ; and the plan is a very good
one. In proportion to risks the funds are unusually large.
On the other hand, no less than £86,416, or just upon
two-thirds of all the assets, are locked up in mortgages.
The invested assets of all sorts return an average of about
£4 7s. per cent. Is it really worth while for a fire office
to take mortgage risks in order to get a return of that
size ? Suppose on £50,000 of mortgages an average of
only £125 a year has to be written off, even that brings
the interest return down by £ per cent.

This co., formed in 1892, did a good deal of agency busi-
ness in its earlier years, for Westralia was the land of
absentees. Latterly, however, as things have settled
down and people have had time to die, the trustee busi-
ness appears to have grown, and no doubt will go on
growing if the co. behaves itself. The increase of trust
balances recently has been considerable. A change has,
however, been made in the manner in which the trust
balances are presented. Formerly only the invested
amounts were added in ; now the full total value of the
estates (including unrealised properties) is included. This
accounts for part of the big jump shown in the last line :
April, Oct., April, Oct.,
1901. 1907. 1908. 1908.
Commissions *.. £1,540 £2,939 £2,788 £2,635
Interest, rent, etc... 535 531 403 510
Expenses .. .. 1,321 1,881 1,917 2,038
Expenses p.c. of re-
venue .. .. 63*6 64-2 60 T 64‘8
Profits p.c. of funds.. 10/12/7 15/10/6 12/7/4 10/17/4
Trust balances .. 103,234 501,355 538,644 1,073,392
The expenses are calculated without allowing for the use of
the co.’s own premises ; the latter amount is deducted from
the rent return. In other words, it is shown as a
reduction of income instead of an addition to ex-
penses. The capital is £10,000; the whole of it
was put into office property, which stands in the books
at £12,000. Allowing £3OO a year as rent for the co.’s
own office, the net return on that book value is a shade
over 6 per cent. The co.’s other assets are mainly mort-
gages arid debentures. Its total liabilities are only £22. As
with practically every trustee co. the return on the share-
holders’ funds is very high ; the only fly in the ointment
is the liability of £2 on each share (paid to 10s.). With
ordinary care, however, the risk is not great; for the co.
has no deposits, etc., to safeguard. The only serious
risk, so far as the uncalled capital is concerned, is that
the trusts will be improperly administered ; but it is about
10 years since an Australian trustee co. got into any
trouble publicly on that account. And then it was
simply the result of eminent counsel reading a couple of
lines in a Victorian Act one way while the Privy Council
read it eventually another way.

This co. is in the land-purchase and subdivision business.
Amongst other properties it owns the fine Furraeabad
Estate, near Glen Innes, which it lately offered at auction
without much business resulting. Meantime, however,
it does what the subdivider doesn’t often trouble about —
it works part of the property itself, and lets the balance
in farm areas, helping the farmer with stock, etc., and
helping itself with some of the profit. As one lot of pro-
perty is got rid of -—mostly on terms —another is secured ;
lately the co. bought a slice of country at Dobroyd, along-
side Haberfield—one of the new suburbs of Sydney that
have least reason to be ashamed of themselves as to the
manner they are laid out, and the sort of build-
ings that are being put upon them. No one
balance-sheet can show' how this time-payment land busi-
ness is panning out; almost everything depends on the
amount of the paper profit w'hich the co. sets down as
real profit—that is to say, on the provision which is made
for purchasers’ failure to complete their bargains, sup-
posing bad times happen along. The gross earnings for
the year are set down at £11,853 ; the average assets
amounted to £215,300, so that the gross return was siper
cent. As nearly one-third of the assets are advances,
w’hich no doubt give more than 5£ per cent., it looks as
though some of the other assets give a poor return ;
because included in the earnings are the profits from
sales, and sales ought to have been fairly numerous and
satisfactory during the past year. On-that basis, some of
the assets appear to be still over-valued. Some recent
balance-sheets can be summarised this way
1900. 1905. 1907. 1908.
Capital .. .. £75,176 £87,018 £89,348 £89,862
Deposits .. .. 50,689 37,460 43,341 41,966
Other liabilities 46,559 55,467 48,847 47,056

The liabilities total £89,052 ; so the paid capital exceeds
all the debts, and there are the reserves as an additional
security for the depositors, etc. Put in another wav,
even if the whole of the reserves were w'iped out, being
used to write down the value of the assets, there w'ould
still be more than 20s. of security for every 10s. of debt.
The depositors’ position therefore seems very secure. As
for the shareholders, they probably know' that they are
in a speculative business, and that the published figures,
especially as to any one year’s revenue and profits, are
very littie to go by. All they can hope for is that the
management will faithfully represent the trend of things ;
and, assuming that that work has been done faithfully,
they have every reason to feel good w'ith regard to the
past 10 years’ progress.

yet probably less speculative than it used to be. There
was a time—it is not yet 20 years ago by a good bit—
when the squatter froze only his surplus stock ; he didn t
breed for freezing. Nowadays he does, with the result
that the supply is much more uniform than it used
to be, and in Maoriland it can easily be more
uniform than is possible in droughtier Australia.
But if the supply is steadier, the market still moves in a
mysterious way. From 1897 to 1903, the last year for
which the N.S.W. Statistical Registicr, has a quotation
for N S.W. frozen beef, the price moved between Is. 7d.
and 3s per stone. For mutton the return for 10 years has
ranged between Is. sd. and 3s. 2d. ; and for the last four
years the average top price has been on the down-grade—
which no doubt accounts for the steady fall in profits
since 1905. Liabilities have increased rapidly, yet not so
rapidly as the capital; £1 of capital had to safeguard
about £1 Is. of the public’s money in the business 10
years ao-o ; at present the proportion is £1 of capital to
12s. 6d.°of liabilities. The doubtful feature of the accounts
is-the allowance for depreciation. With works, machin-
ery plant and land lumped at £282,654, a visible total of
£3500 was written off last year—a little over 1£ per cent.
Probably renewals and replacements are paid for out of
revenue * even so, as much of the plant is new, and the
call for ’replacements should at present be light the
allowance for general wear and tear looks very small. If
it is smaller than it should be, then the profits are over-
stated. * # p
There are barrels of trouble brewing over Aus-
tralia’s explosives supply. For years Nobel’s people had
the run of the market, and put up a very good reputa-
tion for selling safe goods at a fair price. Then some
other people came in and badly undersold .Nobels.
Nobel’s said , they couldn’t understand how the other
neoDle did it, until it was found out that the other people
had been adding a trace of mercury, thus defeating the
only then known means of making the highly important
heat test. When the mercury trick was discovered the
fat was in the fire, the other people were biffed out, and
Nobel’s trot the market again. Then they said that,
having lost money through a trick they were en-
titled* to put up their prices. They did so, and
hinted at another rise. Then the big high-ex-
plosives users of Australia, headed by lialf-a-dosien
of the Broken Hill cos., opened: up negotiations with the
De Beer’s people, who have a nigger-labor explosives
factory in S’Africa. The negotiations ended up in these
big users making a five-years’ contract with.the nigger-
labor factory. The Broken Hill cos., it is understood,
have agreed to take 12,000 cases a year ; if they fail, the
De Beer’s people may terminate the contract. The Aus-
tralian Explosives people will need to look hard at those
shipments. The black-labor stuff hasn’t yet stood the
test of shipment, and it is in shipment that these high
explosives mostly go wrong. The S’African stuff is used
in .De Beer’s and other S’African mines. The butcher’s
bill there is tremendous ; but whether the quality of the
explosives is in any way responsible is uncertain. The
fact, however, that the butcher’s bill is big, and that this
is the explosive used, makes i t at least necessary to look hard
at the stuff when it comes off its voyage, and is put into
the hands of some tens of thousands of Australian miners
for use. And this is apart altogether from the black-labor
aspect of the question. Australia ought to be making its
own high explosives ; but if it isn’t making them, then it
is better to give the job to white men in a Glasgow’ factory
than to niggers in an African factory, owned by the crowd
which was mainly responsible for the Boer-war crime.
* ‘ * * *
There has been an extraordinary amount of bit-
terness over the various processes for getting' the zinc out
of the sulphides at Broken Hill and elsewhere. One
inventor, invited to the B. H. Club to talk things over
with a big man whose name w’as attached to another very
similar but later process, replied curtly that w’hen he
wanted to meet No. 2 he would know'where to find him —
eitherin gaol orin Hell. Another bitter quarrel has been
going on between what are generally known as the
Elmore people and the Cattermole people. The two
Elmores are old experimenters, w»ho have been working
at oil concentration for years ; their patents, now owned
by the British Ore Concentration Syndicate, are used by
the Zinc Corporation, while the Cattermole process,
owned by the Minerals Separation Co., is in use by the Sul-
phide Corporation. After a lot of preliminary sparring,
Elmore took Cattermole before Justice Neville, in July
last, for infringement of two patents (1898 and 1901).
Cattermole won. Then Elmore went on to the Court of
Appeal, where the Master of the Bolls sat with two Judges,
one of them being Fletcher Moulton, the big patent-law
map. According to a cable, which isn’t any too clear, El-
more wins this time. Cattermole, this Court thinks, may
use a thin oil (which be is doing), but on account of the
1901 patent it looks as though nobody else but Elmore
may use acid with w’ater, ore, and oil of any kind or in
any quantity. If so, there is trouble ahead for pretty
well all the other zinc-process people, provided, of course,
the Australian Courts think the same way as the Court
of Appeal, or provided again that the House of Lords
doesn’t knock out the Court, of Appeal.
If the lode at the 600 ft. level of the South
Blocks. Broken Hill, fell somewhat short of the manage-
ment’s expectations, it remains a fairly healthy young-
ster, for all that. The promise which the 440 ft. levil
gave of a widening in the south end has been borne out,
and, what is more, the vughy nature of the lode on the
footwall side of the level above has been continued on the
600 ft. level. The quality of the ore has also materially
improved. Pessimists shy at the fact that the lode
averages only 12ft. in width. But at neither end does it
show anv sign of contracting.

The Mine Managers’ Association at Broken
Hill isn’t a happy family just now. The defection of the
British has further soured the milk. Not that the with-
drawal of the British is likely to make any difference to
the carrying out of the wages agreement. In
fact, it may be just as well that a mine that is
not working and does not contemplate doing
so should not sign the same agreement as those which
are working and intend to go on working. But, that
being so, what was the British doing a.t the conference?
There have been some warm things said on this subject;
but other members of the conference at least had the
means of knowing that the British representative was
present merely to watch proceedings and report to his
Board, and was not empowered to commit himself to
anythinsr.
J -* * * *
After nearly two years of struggling, the
Broken Hill Junction North mill has at length recorded
a week’s uninterrupted run of 144 hours. N 6 other mill
on the Barrier has given such persistent trouble; even
now’ its tendency to kick over the traces has not been
entirely rooted out. The electric pumps at the
bottom level have been nearly as bad. It is
doubtful whether a good Cornish lift would not have
served the purpose better. For a long time
past the “electrics” have not properly coped with the
water on the No. 11 level, to the inconvenience of the
men working thereabouts. Thanks to the installation of
a new’ mill engine (the old mill engine had to run the
pumps as well), better conditions now prevail; but an
auxiliary pump has had to be provided.
Broken Hill Junction North seems to drop in
for all the sweet ore contracts. It has just made another
for 12 months with a new buyer on terms which the rest
of them don’t seem able to approach.
* * * *
Development work at the Broken Hill Prop,
continues to tell the same dreary tale of departing
glory. The small bodies of ore located on the 1300 ft.
and'l4ooft. levels have seemingly been followed to
the end, and the end is bitter. When the ore was
almost lost sight of in the north drive on the 1300 ft.
level, it was thought to be coquetting ; so the foot-
wall was broken through to try to discover where
it was hiding. But the crosscut didn’t disclose enough
ore to fill Mr. Knox’s nailcan hat; and similar results
have followed operations southwards.
Block 14, Broken Hill, is going to shut down.
All through 1908 its sulphide mill has been idle ; now it
is going to also leave off rooting out its carbonates, on
the ground that it can’t get a satisfactory quotation for
them. So still another 150 to 170 men are thrown out.

The annual report of Mount David (N.S.W.)
tells that practically all the profit on £11,962 worth of
gold has gone in developing the mine, sinking the new
shaft and improving the battery. The year started with
a credit balance of £666 and wound up with £740, so
Mount David has been steering close to the wind. In all,
4549 tons averaged about £2 a ton, and 3624 tons of tail-
ings nearly 7s. 6d.; but only 730 z. was got from 1/82
tons won from the open cut, and at that rate it will take
a long time to pay for the Huntingdon mill. The last
crushing of 440 tons for 2380 z. was well up to the average
and gave good profit; but the mine requires so muc h
expensive development that dividends will have to be
delayed.

For the present, anyway, S. Australia isn’t to
have a State coal mine, after all: in the Leg. Council the
Bill authorising the purchase of Shedden’s property near
the East Greta and Abermain (N.S.W.) could only com-
mand five votes. It was fired at from all quarters: the
mine was in another State, the coal was too far from sea-
board, it was too deep, the price was too high, the carry-
ing quality W’as doubtful. And Anti-Sosh whooped for
the opposition on general Anti-Sosh principles.
The Electrolytic Refining and Smelting Co. of
Australia had a formal meeting 12 months ago, but the
first of any consequence took place in Sydney a day or
two back, when general manager Magnus reported
that all things were ready for a start with the new’
year. Walter Bussell Hall, the Mt. Morganaire—a man
who can talk easily in hundreds of thousands—was
chairman ; and it looks just that sort of co. Mount Mor-
gan is behind it, also F. H. Snow, who for years has been
Australian representative of the biggest foreign ore-
buyers. The paid share capital of the co. is £75,000 ; and
£lOO,OOO of debentures will be issued, though only
£50,000 has been called for so far. The plant
to the end of November had cost £117,198; total
expenditure has been £132,503. F. 11. Snow’s offices
look after the ore-buying: and already all sorts of gold,
copper, and silver-lead ore are coming in. The works
represent practically a new Australian industry, for
though Oobar has had its own electrolytic refinery for the
separation of gold and copper, there have been no
other works in Australia, and hitherto even Mt.
Morgan and Mt. Lvell have sent their blister copper
to America for treatment. Mt. Morgan didn’t like that:

All philosophic works, and Holy Writ itself,
teem with warning of the uncertainty of life. Deaths by
natural causes have been pushed far down the years of
civilised life, but with the increase of that very civilisa-
tion the liability to accident has enormously increased.
The railway, the ferry, the tram, and the murderous
motor car inevitably cause thousands of people to place
their lives and limbs in the hands of entire strangers, of
whose ability they can have absolutely no knowledge. It
would seem, therefore, to be only common-sense to guard
against the financial consequences of misadventure by
taking out an accident and sickness policy with the South
British Insurance Company Limited ; £5OO Death, £3 per
week Accident and Sickness Compensation for £3 per
annum. Head Office forN.S.W. : 12 Bridge-street, Syd-
ney. Geo. H. Moore, Manager.*
The Callow Revolving Band Screen gives
promise of revolutionising the system of ore classification
in Australia, as it has already done in America, for all
classes and conditions of concentrating ores, with vastly
improved extraction in the mills. At Broken Hill it has
been especially successful. It is an unqualified success as a
fine wet screen, and this is what all the mining world has
been seeking; it is also equally effective on the coarse rafts
from Cornish Rolls. With it there is no excessive wear, no
clogging of the mesh ; it has an enormous capacity and a
small water consumption. The fundamental principle of
this machine is a travelling band or belt of screen cloth
stretched on a drum over which the ore and its carrying
water is spread by means of a distributing apron of
special design. The drum passes over rollers, and the
belt is caused to revolve continuously at a speed adapted
to the nature and quantity of the material to be screened.
The machine is fully protected by patent rights. For
further particulars apply to the Austral Otis Engineering
Co., of Hanna Street, South Melbourne. This firm are the
manufacturers and sole agents for Australasia.* [2

* * * *
The Highland Mary, which turned out a very
fair quantity of gold in the old days of Swamp Oak
(N.S. W.), is to be given another trial after 10 years! idle-
ness. After the metal had been pretty well rooted out
down to 140 ft., the stuff running about 2oz. over the
plates as an average, an English co., the N.S.W. Gold-
fields, Ltd., took the mine in hand. The English co. put
on, as usual, a good deal of top hamper, sank an expensive
main shaft to 250 ft., put in a crosscut, and did a lot of
other work. It got a reef, of which a concern run on
more modest lines might have thought fairly well ; but it
was no good to this co., so other prospecting was
tried. A second reef was thus cut; it was richer, but
as it appeared to be making into the other, and as it was
too small to pay, driving on it was discontinued. The
shaft was then sunk to 355 ft., and some crosscutting was
done, but only a stringer was found. No attempt was
made on this level to get the second reef. The last 10ft.
of sinking is alleged to have been in softer county; the
present resurrection is on the strength of this, and in the
belief that, if the country is softer, the reefs will have a
better chance to live than they had in the intermediate
levels.
■fr * ■* *
For the year ended May 31 Hainault's report,
which has just wandered back to VVestralia from England,
is enough to set shareholders alight. At May, 1907, the
ore reserves were estimated at 240,000 tons of a recoverable
value of 275. During the succeeding year 68,000 tons
were extracted, so that, even if no more ore had been
developed, there should still remain 172,000 tons in sight.
But the reserves at May, 1908, are estimated at only
100,000 tons, with a value of 28s. gross instead of 275.
net, and nothing whatever is said about this (or the
previous) miscalculation, nor is any attempt made to
reconcile the two estimates. Other unsatisfactory
features about the report are the drop of 4s. 3d. per ton
in the gold yield as compared with the previous year’s
average, the entire absence of any reference to the main
shoot pitching south out of the property, and the
depressing fact that the accounts show a debit balance of
£8969. Ore reserves are alleged to have been considerably
swollen by encouraging developments since the year
closed ; but, although this assertion may be true, it is
bound to be largely discounted by reason of what has
already happened. Kalgoorlie mining ’may have a few
things to whoop about, but the past handling of the
Hainault is certainly not one of them.
* * * *
There have been some good developments
lately at Kalg orlie. The Lake View has sunk a winze
18ft. on 4Jft. of £5 10s. ore below 1900 f- The Persever-
ance has met better values at several points. The Horse-
shoe has cut a sdwt. lode west of the main workings at
2000 ft. And the South Kalgurli has opened up 30ft. of pay-
able ore at 1200 ft., and has found high-grade ore at 1600 ft.
Still, if a mine ought to have half of its market value in
sight, and even then have a sound look about the bottom
workings, nearly every one of the big Kalgoorlie mines is
over-valued at present, while most of the smaller ones
stand at highly speculative figures.
Announced that the monthly output of the Sons
of Gwalia (W. A.) is to be gradually increased from £18,500
to £20,500. Some good judges reckon that it ought to be
put up higher still, but 8., M. and Co., who boss the
show, think differently. For some time past the Gwalia
has been the pick of the firm’s western basket, and next
to the Ivanhoe, Boulder, Horseshoe, and perhaps the
Kalgurli, it is, on present showing, the best mine in
Groperland.
* * * *
Adelaide Register gushes over the prospects of
Norseman (W.A.) to a ridiculous extent in a recent article.
It believes that the Mararoa “ will eventually prove as
good as any mine in the West,” that Norseman is the
place to find a permanent supply of rich specimens, that
telluride will be found at Buldania, and that, with
“judicious expenditure and good management, the re-
sults from other mines should prove satisfactory.” This
looks like leg-pulling on the part of some market-rigger.
At the best, Mararoa will never be anything more than a
pocket edition of the Boulder or Ivanhoe ; specimens are
not strikingly plentiful at Norseman ; while the yarn
about telluride at Buldania is mere boom talk, since
there is no more reason to expect telluride at Buldania
than at 50 other places.
Donovan’s Find, near Southern Cross (W.A.),
looks promising. Since its discovery, 15 months ago,
1751 tons have been mined and milled for about £BOOO
worth of bullion. Ore averaging 3oz. for sft. was at the
latest being broken below 200 ft., and at this point the
reef looks permanent.

Princess Royal (W.A.) has a lot to answer for.
AH other excuses for not coming up to expectations
having given out, the cry was recently raised that the
mine would have to close down on account of an insuf-
ficient water supply. Since then the Government has
remedied this failing, yet operations have been suspended
all the same. Ink and paper can’t adequately express the
contempt earned by some of the methods adopted in con-
nection with this pla3 r ed-out gambling counter.

months, the surplus of liquid and semi-liquid assets over
liabilities is £2500 less than it was when the half-year
srarted.J The rest of the money has gone into development,
and other non-liquid assets which mayor may nob earn
more than their cost. In the six months, 10,947
tons of ore were smelted. General-manager Shepherd
reports that “ the irregular shape and rapid fluctuations
in grade of the ore occurrences in this district make it
difficult to give an estimate of ore reserves which is
at once fair and safe. Assuming standard copper at £55
per ton, the visible tonnage of payable ore was 20,150 tons
on Sept. 30.”, Altogether the O.K. has been a remarkable
little mine ; from 1904, when first The Bulletin reported
its discovery, down to the end of last year, it paid £92,799
in dividends and provided about £40,000 for equipment.
But its future is very uncertain: as Richard Shepherd
says, the mine is in a district where the ore bodies are of
“ irregular shape and rapid fluctuation in grade.”
* * * *
Chairman Millican, at the Brilliant Freehold’s
half-yearly meeting iat Charters Towers, told the story of
hard times for 17 years. Working among freehold blocks
that carry mining rights has proved very expensive:
£55,740 out of shareholders’ pockets, £34,521 out of the
mine, and a percentage of tributors’ gold—in all nearly
£lOO,OOO-—has been spent in developing and working the
Brilliant reef. The straight shaft has been sunk 1256 ft.,
but a lot of work has been done on the underlie. The
shaft is now being deepened to open up the Day Dawn,
and the depth at which, the crosscut will be started de-
pends on the strike and dip of the reef. The trend of
the reef that split away a few feet back from the
face of No. 7 main level is north-easterly, and No. 6
hanging-wall reef has also a northerly bearing, so, after
all, some of these sections of the Day Dawn may junction
with the Brilliant. All the talk about the individuality
of the Day Dawn will subside if the levels are taken far
enough to the north to be close to the Brilliant. The
N. B. Freehold has much unexplored country, and pos-
sibly three parallel reefs, and the question of idernity
seems of more importance to the group of mines further
east. The last fortnightly crushing was 367 tons for
£1939, making with the previous lot 822 tons for £4034.
* * * *
Brilliant Extended (Charters Towers) is talking
of bi-monthly dividends of 6d.; but the show'has had a
long life and has only paidseven dividends (£43,750). The
last return w'as 4450 tons for £10,250, and the monthly
profit is estimated at £3374. If bi-monthly dividends are
paid out of returns like this, there will be mighty little to
add to the reserve ; and B. Extended, with deep ground
to prospect, requires a strong reserve just as urgently as
Mills’ United, which puts by so much money. A mine
with less than two years’ supply of ore in sight has to
keep prospecting, and that can’t be done for tuppence.
B. Extended is developing payable stone in the direction
of the Brilliant Deep’s boundary, so, in spite of the broken
ground in No. 2 West, which at first gave such sensa-
tional assays, backers of Deeps have plucked up hope.
The collapse of Deep shares and East Mexicans has
demoralised the Towers market, and the idea that the
Day Dawn is bearing northerly in the direction of the old
Brilliant Freehold workings isn’t comforting. Bonnie
Dundee’s last crushing averaged £5 3s. 6d., but the
quality is likely to vary. All the available stopes are
being worked for all they are worth, and it is expected
that 400 tons a month will be crushed.
» * * *
Mills’ United, Charters Towers, cleaned up a
crushing of 1753 tons, and then swept up the plates, for
£15,982. After paying a Christmas div. of £30,000 there
will be a big cash reserve. Hard cash is a feature with
Mills’ United, and some of the shareholders will begin to
wonder what the cash is saved up for. Probably the
management has a better explanation than it would care
to make just now. There are numerous poor faces where
prospecting is being carried on ; and preparations are
being made to open up No. 14 East.
•* * * *
Cania, 100 miles from Gladstone (Q.), may some
day be a mineral field. Forty years ago good nuggets
were unearthed there, but little reefing has been done.
At Mount Rose a shaft has been sunk 110 ft., but work
was stopped for want of machinery. Rich pockets have
been found on this hill. The De Quiro (silver lead) is
owned by a syndicate from the Barcoo. The owners
have had exemption, but propose to get to work again.
A tunnel has been driven 120 ft., and a winze sunk
another 50ft. Some of the ore at grass is rich ; quantity
unknown. The field has no battery, so gold-getting is a
precarious business. Dry-blowing is carried on by Piele
and Webb on the top of a high hill, and they are averag-
ing half an oz. gold per day from three loads of dirt.
G. C. Wilcocks has a dredge working in the creek.
Gyrnpie and the Towers took the attention from this
field in its boom days, and since then only pockets and
rich leaders have been sought after ; and pig-rooting is
the order of things. The field has the attraction of a
good water supply, and the climate cannot be surpassed.
Eidsvold, about 70 miles away, is another defunct mining
area ; though lately some of the old workings have been
pumped out with a view to inspection.
Mt. Morgan (Q.), at the inquiry before the
special Board which followed the two recent disasters,
owned up to this shocking record during this year and
last:—
First week February, 1907: No. 2 chamber 750 (S.-E.
corner), 800 tons fell.
May, 1907 : No. 1 chamber, 750 level, 6000 tons.
June 27 : No. 1 chamber, 750 level (S.-E. corner), 1000
tons.
July 27, 1908: No. 2 chamber, 750 level (Ryan’s), 2800
tons.
August 2, 1908 : No. 1 chamber, 750 level (Dolan’s), 3000
tons.
September 5 : No. 1 chamber, 850 level, 1100 tons.
October 13: No. 1 chamber, 750 level (Oreagh’s), 800
tons.
October 30 : No. 1 chamber, 750 level (Dolan’s), 80 tons.
November 4 : No. 2 chamber, 750 level, 12,750 tons.
The only cheerful thing about this record is that the
co. not only owns up to it, but actually supplies the list
itself; and that can betaken as an indication that it
wants to do the fair thing, and doesn’t feel itself at all
bound to the system. The management probably sees
the chance of getting some of the way back into the good
graces of the working miners of Queensland. There was
a time when Mt. Morgan was reckoned a first-rate boss,
but gradually it has lost that reputation—and a reputa-
tion like that is a good asset to have ; for the difference
between the work of a good miner and a poor one is so
great that the co. which can command the good miners
has a big pull over the rest.
* * * *
There can be no question that, from a metal-
lurgical point of view, the present management of
Chillagoe (N.Q.) has knocked matters into a shipshape
condition. The ore supply from outside sources, how-
ever, is likely to be discouraged by the treatment men
get from the railway people. As a specific instance : A
miner, who had something over 50 tons of ore at the rail-
way, a few miles away from the smelters, repeatedly
requested trucks, was kept waiting a week, and then,
without notice of the arrival of the trucks, was ordered
to find labor to load the trucks in a few hours—in default,
demurrage. This is one of the reasons why the ore is
being secured by other firms and sent to the coast.
* * * *
H. L. Hancock, who followed liis father as
general manager of Wallaroo and Moonta copper mines
(S.A.), is back from looking at what the world is doing
in the way of big mining and copper-marketing. He
tries to speak hopefully of copper-market prospects, and
tells the familiar tale of probable railway electrification ;
but there were a few other things that he actually saw—
one place, for instance, where in the smelter sheds was a
store of copper concentrates awaiting treatment
estimated to contain 10,000 tons of recoverable copper.
In the Lake Superior copper region he went down the
Tamarack and Red Jacket. The former, a vertical shaft,
is over a mile deep, mostly under sea-level—the greatest
depth attained in the world. The Red Jacket shaft is
deeper—about 8000 ft. —but, as it is partly underlay, its
vertical depth isn’t equal to the Tamarack’s. H. L.
Hancock found it comparatively cool down there, or at
least, not as hot as he had expected ; but, owing to the
unusual atmospheric pressure, his unaccustomed ears
could only distinguish sounds imperfectly.
«' * * *
“ Val,” concerning the West Arm field, Northern
Territory: “ Yeast ”(B. 15/10/’08) is interesting, but not
accurate. Between the months of June and November
the water is not hard to control. The greatest handicap
is “slurry,” a white clay about the consistency of dough.
One shaft, on the Good Hope claim, had to be abandoned
on that account, notwithstanding that the shaft was fully
timbered, and an iron box was put in and screwed down
with jacks. The only way to contend with the
slurry is to work all round the clock, and even
then if you knock off for half an hour the work
of a week is undone. “Yeast’s” remark, “the deeper
the lode the better the tin,” is not founded on fact.
Hang-Gong’s claim, owned by a Chow who got £30,000
worth of tin in three years, did not go deeper than 15ft.
The Good Hope went 30ft. and apparently cut out; the
Comites claim went 20ft,, and the Mona and the
Northern Queen went between 30ft. and 40ft. No mine
on the Arm has gone deeper than 50ft. at the outside,
and the general opinion is that the shallower the
workings the better the tin. (This is not alluvial, but
reef.) The climate of West Arm will compare favorably
with that of any other part of Australia, It misses the
heat of the Riverinaand Darling districts (N.S. W.), the
scorching brickfielders of VVestralia, and the bushfires of
Victoria. A hot night is an exception, and every after-
noon brings a cool sea breeze. Those who complain that
it is not fit fora white man to live in are those with an axe
to grind. The axe is labelled “ John Chinaman.”
* * * *
New Jubilee is of the same type as the Old
Jubilee, Scarsdale (Vic.), though the two shows are in no
way connected. The new has not done so very badly.
Against calls of £7400 is a set-off of £3OOO in divs. In
addition to calls, however, there has been an expenditure
of £10,200. That even £3OOO has been divided is due to
cheap work, 16s. 2£d. per ton covering all outlay. So far,
shallow makes have proved the most profitable. Atsooft.
310 ft. of driving has only disclosed the lode track. Share-
holders are told that sloping has been done “ off No. 1 and
No. 2 rises, and the material has been sent to
the mill.” Not a word to show the value of
tins stone, though it is the key to the claim.
Prospecting is being carried on everywhere. This shows
what scraping is being done to find stone before the exist-
ing short shoots are depleted. The co. treads too close on
the heels of profits, as no sooner is £l5OO in hand than out
pops a div. A little reversal of form, and out will pop a
call. So it is with most Victorian mining outside Bendigo.
* * * *
W. H. Quick is back in Melbourne from London
with a pea-green hat and suitd la mode. His color ought
to be true blue, like that of the Junction Dredge at Brignt,
to which he stands sponsor. That co. greets his return
with a record for the six months of his absence, divs.
having been paid at the rate of 65 per cent, per annum on
paid-up capital. The co. has two dredges, ladling up
about 320,000 cubic yards of dirt each half-year ; and the
average gold contents are 2T2 grains per cubic yard. This
isn’t much to boast about, but the per cent, return is
better than that of any quartz show in the State. The
Junction has a modest little capital of £9625, and
£ll6s. sd. per share has come out as profits. This fact
isn’t always being paraded, as is the case with some of
the Bright dredges.
V • * •
South New Moon, Bendigo, profits by having
built up a reserve during the time it was paying its
£400,000 of divs. The alluring feature of the co. is its
economy. Plugging away at shaft-sinking, and doing a
good footage of driving and rising, its mining costs,
nevertheless, only amount to a shade above 12s. per ton.
Battery work runs out at only 2s. 6d. per ton. This
allows, even on sdwt. dirt, odd Is. divs., and there are
several thousandsin hand to guard against contingencies.
That is the best of Bendigo. Its methods may not meet
with the approval of the swell mining engineer, but, as
old George Lansell always said, “ we get the most out
of the stone at the lowest cost, and what more can you
do?” Satisfactory, too, is the treatment of share-
holders with regard to news. There is a fair start and an
even course. No wonder Bendigo lasts while other fields
languish.
A couple of tin-mining balance-sheets serve to
remind that there is such a field as North-East Tasmania.
The Gladstone report comes to tell of wrecked hopes,
wrecked estimates and a stranded dredge. Dry weather
keeps the barge up theßingarooma River, so a ss. call is
the Christmas box to help it down to the Scotia Flat. If
recollection is not amiss, that is where work began and
where the boom busted. Yet the story now is that good
tin is there. If so, the management becomes' more 'mys-
terious than ever. Why was the ground left before the
run of tin Was exhausted? Still the local view
always was that the Scotia Flat had never been worked
properly. If the ground pans out all right, someone with
a stiff back will have to stand up against any div.-paying
until some money is saved to help on to another patch.
The other show is the South Mount Cameron, where a
fine plant and a big face of wash afe awaiting a flood to
clear the Ringarooma and sweep away the debt built up
during six months of comparative idleness.

When the big Oriental Timber Corporation was
floated in Sydney last year to work some timber con-
cessions in Siberia, and The Bulletin said Siberia was a
long way off, and there was room for many accidents to
happen between Sydney and Vladivostock, some of the
promoters felt quite hurt about it, and wrote to that
effect. Some of the accidents have happened. There
was a lot more trouble about the rights and concessions
than had ever been anticipated, and to get in £75,000
more capital the co. is now offering 10 per cent, cumula-
tive preference, with the right to share in dividends over
10 per cent.

“Nix”: I note with interest Bulletin’s re-
marks re Metallurgist Opie, late of Mitchell River (N.Q.).
That yarn of “no ore” seems to kick with a previous re-
port, issued in the Northern Miner, of September 1,
1908. In that report shareholders were told that he had
obtained 270 tons of ore in driving and sinking, which,
when treated by the McArthur patent process, was going
to yield £2OOO (the exact figures were given in the report
above referred to). Let us even assume that this 270 tons
is the sum total of ore in the mine ; then, seeing that, the
plant is complete, is it not as well to get this £2OOO of
profit as to leave it lying on the dump ? If it were a ques-
tion of erecting machinery, then his attitude could be
explained. If the plant will do what he says it will, then
why not start it? It is of no use commenting on the
mine being of no value. He was sent out to demonstrate
the process, which he has failed to do. The question is,
why ? A few assays of the ore before treatment and
after would soon demonstrate the success or otherwise of
the plant. It should not matter to Mr. Opie whether the
co. had any more ore or not. I now learn that he is
closing up the mine he is managing in South Australia.
While Mr. Chas. Lowe was managing that mine, he freely
expressed the opinion that it had an excellent prospect;
and as he is a man with a reputation to lose, surely some
reliance could be placed on him. It seems that there is
a bright future ahead of Australian mining; but it can
only be attained when Australians appreciate the fact
that English “ experts ” are not necessarily Australian
miners.

“ Dandalup ” : “ Fitzwilliam ” (B.
26/11/’08) asks, “Is there anything in the theory
that a shark will not touch a blackfellow?” In
my opinion the theory is all bosh. I have re-
peatedly seen niggers tackled by sharks. A shark
will tackle anything when hungry. In support of
my statement allow me to produce the one-armed
nigger diver of Colombo, so well-known to tra-
vellers per mail steamer. This youth was assailed
by a solicitor of the deep some years ago, and
just managed to escape with his life. Hence the
absence of the arm. As to tackling Chows and
such like, if I was a shark I would strike anything
in the yellow line right off my menu. I’d be a
White Australian shark, or no shark at all.
“ Gilrooney Red-tape is a conscientious
and illogical piece of drapery at times. For
instance, the Broken Hill municipal council
recently received from the Forestry Department
in Sydney a request to prevent the “ringbarking
of good timber ”in the Barrier district. Imagine
the irony of preventing ringbarking on a desert
where it would be difficult to find a tree large
enough to provide shade for half a rabbit! lam
now anxiously awaiting another circular to the
council asking it to prevent the wanton destruction
of sperm whales.
“O,” his lie -
Queensland is neglected by the papers of Australia—
The Bulletin included.
I have been living at Wooroorooka for some years. It
is on the border of New South Wales, and the city of
Barringun is in that State, just over the fence. It’s
pretty hot here in the summer time, although visitors
from the Barcoo complain of the cold. The hottest time
of the year is a few weeks after Christmas. The Warrego
was never known to boil before 30th December, except in
1895, when it began to boil on 10th December. The
present summer promises to be unusually warm. A boy
rom Sydney was staying here. He went into the
Warrego for a swim the other day, but came out quickly,
and has since died.
A queer old hatter dwells near me. He is very fond of
eggs, and has a great crowd of pet hens. This is how he
prepares his dinner. He puts the bacon in the frying
pan, and places the pan in the shade of a tree to prevent
the bacon from being burnt. Then he calls out. “Any
of you girls about?” At the sound of his voice all the
hens that are ready to lay rush up to him cackling. He
selects two and says, “Lizzie and Kitty, it’s your
turn. Come along.” Lizzie and Kitty come up and
egg, and walk away pleased. The others slink away dis-
appointed. He breaks the eggs and puts them into the
pan, and in about half a minute they’re cooked. He
spoke to his girls one day as I was passing, but none of
them came forward. Then he called them by name, and
scolded them. Not one of them had an egg in her
possession. They looked shame-faced and crestfallen, and
blushed about the beak. Presently, however, two of
them brightened up, looked as if they had a new idea,
and cleared out towards the pub. In a few minutes they
returned triumphantly, accompanied by an old strange
speckled hen with feathers on her legs, and in less than
a minute the hatter was eating his egg.
Rabbits are fairly numerous in this neighborhood,
although travellers tell me that if I want to see real
rabbits I should go to Eulo. It’s very dangerous walking
about here, for you forget about the rabbits, and when
you step on one it wriggles, and you’re liable to fall and
meet with an accident. Five men had their legs broken
in this way during the last three weeks. I was walking
with my cattle-dog along a rabbit-proof fence about sft.
m height, on Tuesday last. Multitudes of rabbits
were grazing around. Something frightened them and
there was a panic. The fence stopped them and they
began to accumulate in layers. In less than five minutes
I found myself standing with a seething mass of rabbits
around me, up to my neck. If the fence had been a foot
higher I would have been suffocated. When the stratum
got level with the fence, the sight of the rabbits rushing
over reminded me of the water rushing over Niagara. I
lost my dog. He was choked by rabbits, and his dead
body was heaved up by rabbits underneath until the rush
of rabbits carried it over the fence. I found the dead but
faithful animal two miles away in N.S.W. It had been
washed all that way by the wave of rabbits.
If Wooroorooka was in N.S.W. and within a reasonable
distance of Sydney it would be an ideal site for the
Federal capital.

“Stony”: As to the imported aboriginalie,
admired for its simplicity by “ Hassan ” (3/12/’08),
a lie of this class has come my way in one of the
Filchit grablications, which I found in our fowl-
yard. Although not stated as such, the
aboriginalie is, of course, imported, since it
appears in a Filch it snippet-rag. The anecdote
lies thus : “ A very wealthy Society woman, who
gives both herself and her means largely to the
poor, was recently waited upon by her butler with
a rude package and a letter ‘ from a poor little
girl.’ The letter read : * Dere lady—i love you
so I worked and earned 2 pence to get you a eg
for a cristmus gift for you. It is the nisest thing
Ino to ete. It is a good eg.—Frum MARY.’
There was the ‘ good eg ’ well tied in brown
paper ! The woman burst into a flood of tears ”
—and fell on the eg’s neck and kissed it, prob’ly.
This ought to please “Hassan”—it’s a simple,
plain lie, all right!

“ Edyson ” : In my collection of bush
cranks, Jock Hughes occupies a conspicuous posi-
tion. Jock, an old Sydneyside native, was a
successful sheep-breeder in a medium way of busi-
ness, and ran a model farm, but in one particular
he seemed to be as mad as they’re made. He had
a fairly-large dam in the cow paddock adjoining
his homestead, and on it he ran a cranky boat
patiently fabricated from the burnt trunk of a
hollow tree. With his absurd craft, that looked
like a pig trough of larger growth, went a small
sail and a large rudder. Running into the dam
was a pier about the size of an ordinary bush
stool, and built on the same lines. At the end of
it was a broom-handle flag pole, with a miniature
Union Jack. Hughes had built himself a stone
lighthouse, and had piled about it in the
water a cartload of rocks, which he called “ The
breakers.” The whole dam did not cover two-
thirds of an acre, but Jock always spoke
of it in the respectful language an ordinary
man applies to the Atlantic. At one time
Hughes must have dissipated in marine fiction,
and over-indulgence had set up salt water on the
brain. He always donned a sort of sailor suit
before going out on the heaving deep, and spoke
gravely of the lee side and the windward side, and
of dirty weather, and of the wind being “ sou’-
west” or “nor’-east,” and his crazy tub had
fo’castle and poop and bulwarks,.also a wheel and
an anchor and a jib-boom and mainmast, and
was a rakish craft of 5001 b. burthen. On fine
evenings Hughes would sit on the dam bank with
a telescope, watching for strange sails, or waiting
to see the sun sink in the billows. At other times he
would put off in his boat and employ long, desolate
hours fishing for yabbies with a bit of mutton on a
string. He never walked round the dam, and
more than once was dashed on “the breakers.”
One morning, after a wild night, Jock’s daughter
found that her father’s bed had not been slept in,
and on a search being made the old man was
found clinging to his overturned boat in the
middle of the dam. The anchor had fallen out
and tethered the punt, and Hughes had clung
there all night. He might easily have walked
ashore, but that would have outraged the tra-
ditions. This was Jock’s last voyage. He died a
month later of rheumatic fever, brought about by
exposure. In his will he directed that his body
was to be sewn in a sail and buried in mid-ocean,
with a round shot at its heels; but as mid-ocean
was much too shallow a burying place, Jock was
duly interred in the Pibrock cemetery.
* » * *
“Bill Outback” : Be the London Health’s
scientific information re a simple method of test-
ing suspected water, my mates and I have a
better plan. We take a hundred-gallon sample in
a galvanised iron tank, carried specially for the
purpose by each of us in turn. Into this we pub
4'7 bushels of wood-ash (gidyea or mulga pre-
ferred). I forgot to say that the testing tank has
three observation glass windows. The tank,
during the experiment, is protected from the
direct rays of the sun by a huge umbrella. A
handy fire keeps the temperature of the water ab
about 112° Centigrade for 10 hours. Within 60
hours organic matter and piano-particles are de-
tected by the appearance of curious globules of
opalescent hues. These are pricked with a
jewelled hat-pin. If the globes give off a smell
like that exuded by the Stinking Fish party,
the water is further dosed with ash and heated.
If the water is really bad ib bulges the western
side of the tank within a fortnight. Such water
is only fit for washing Afghans, and must not be
drunk by white men who want to live long.
During the testing period we do a perish, of
course; but we never drink bad water.
“Moorabinda” : Away back in the early
’eighties a traveller lost his way in the great
scrubland beyond the Dunlop Range (N.S.W.).
He could find no water ; he could find no station.
Perhaps he wandered round and round in a
circle, ever coming back with the weird
fatality of lost travellers to the same
spot—returning to his fate, like a
murderer to the scene of his
crime. Far and wide
stretched a stricken land.
The glare of a hard and
pitiless sky overhead,
the infinite vista of
saltbush, brigalow.
stay -a - while, and

that w»re

of stone, and

To keep star-
vation back a
day, and to

his dying horse and
drank its blood. That
night, as the sun was
setting over the grey
bastions of mallee scrub,
a bound ary-rider found
him. But it was too
late; now he was cold,
never again to be warm
in a land of a thousand
furnaces. In his pockets
were ss. The boundary-rider took them, and at
the township to which he was going he
gave the ss. to a sundowner, for bush-
men are apt to be superstitious, and he
would not keep the money or spend it. The
sundowner was found on the plains, months after,
dead, in a clump of yellow wattles, and on him
were ss. The two stock-riders that found him
came to Grath, passed through it on foot, and
struck off to a station where they were to work.
They, too, were found—one hanging to an iron-
bark tree, the other dead beneath. The hanging
man had ss. on him. The man who found them
was a rouseabout for Lukes, the then king of
Grath. He became possessed of the ss. Lukes
advised him to throw the money into the river,
but he was a new hand with no superstitions, and
he went off next morning to carry a message to
the back-station, 20 miles away. But before he
got there the floods came, and he was never seen
again, nor any trace of him. He might be lost for
generations in a country where one man leased a
thousand square miles of land, and had but one
sheep to 10 acres. Where are those five
SHILLINGS ?
■* * * *
“Werrimee”: The rabbit is everywhere
and always. He came out with the first fleet. An
inventory of live stock, taken at Port Jackson on
May 1, 1788, included five rabbits, three of which
belonged to Governor Phillip, and two to Lieu-
tenant-Governor Ross. More were brought from
Capetown in 1791 by King, in the Gorgon. In
August, 1858, a case was tried in the Court of
Bequests of Parramatta, in which plaintiff claimed
damages for the destruction of 50 rabbits by two
dogs belonging to defendant. His warren was
situated near Ryde, and was partly enclosed by a
high fence and partly by a stone wall. The dogs
had got over the wall at night. A verdict was
entered for plaintiff, damages £5O. A quarter of
a century later anyone found harboring bunny
was heavily fined ; and before another quarter of

a century had passed squatters and settlers were
being prosecuted for not pursuing and killing the
pest continuously. Despite the ceaseless war,
despite a big export trade and fur market, he is a
lucky grazier to-day whose inventory does not
include five billion rabbits. He would be glad if
somebody’s dog would get over the fence and
destroy the lot.
A SONG OF CHRISTMAS IN TOWN.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Yo-Ho there, my hearties !
Christmas-time on the lee bow !
Christmas-time !
The time of joy and jollity and jubilation and jamborees—
The time of the early uprising- of the sun and of the late
retreat to bed of the sated citizen—
The time when all (insect) things hum or buzz, and the
voice of the cicada is loud in the land —
The time of the visitation of bronzed and hairy persons
from far places—individuals primed with good reso-
lutions and an abiding faith in their own perspi-
cacity—
The time of the heaving-up-on-the-horizon of affable and
wealthy Uncles from Fiji and Guam, and of the
efflorescence of fortunate finders of immensely valu-
able gold rings—
The time when, by the sweet influence of the Pleiades
and the hilarity of the season, the Fortunate Finders
part with the aforesaid rings for an old somr, or
£2 10s. in cold cash—
The time when the majestic police-constables appear all
glorious without in dazzling duck trousers and
helmets like to the snow-capped Andes—
Christmas-Time !
The time when the sun, leaving the sombrous, cold, old
world, comes South to bathe in the Pacific and kiss
the wet and naked limbs of the young men and
maidens—
The time of Surf and other bathing, when the Righteous
shudder, and say “Shocking !" and “Shameful!'’
and go forth to gaze upon the Goodly Show—
The time when the Mayors and Meddlesome Matties of the
Marine Suburbs ponder and deliberate upon the
length and breadth and shape and color and texture
°f gay and giddy pantlets and Combinations—
Yea, even the Neck-to-Knee and the Next-to-Nothing
bathing costumes!
Christmas-Time !
The time of hot afternoons and blistering winds ; and of
burning beaches, blue seas, bronzed boys and blessed
southerlies—
The time of crowded streets and conglomerated trams,
and of congested shops, terrible and bright with
weird gifts and wonderful toys—
The time of the Golliwog, the Jumping Jim-Jam, the
Shivery Spider, and Ursus Eduardus, or the Teddy
Bear, a strange and foolish beast suffering from
chronic rheumatism or rigor mortis —
The time of the Terror of the Toyshops—
The time of the irruption of fearful and amazing Noises
and hideous Nightmares of sound—
The time of the season of Peace and Goodwill and Ger-
man Bands and German Sausage and elderly Prawns
and venerable Crayfish—
The time of mendacious and perspiring Dagoes, waving
dirty hands, with cries of “ Fri’ Feesh, won 1 ” and
“ Two large-a Pla’! ”
The time of the Cheerful Landlady and the Bashful
Boarder—
The time of the small stuffy room, the Kapok Bed, and
the glorious company of the Mosquitoes—
The time of the Genuine Norfolk Howard that walketh
by night, and in the morning is not—though, perad-
venture, if one is, and if he be displayed unto the
Cheerful Landlady’s gaze, she shall say :
“ Oh, my goodness ! What a horrid thing !
What is it } I never saw one in my house before 1
You MUST HAVE BROUGHT IT WITH YOU ! 1 ”
Whereupon the Crushed Boarder fleeth into the night—
or the Nearest Pub 1
Christmas time !
The time when the schools break up, and the trams and
ferry-boats break down, and the burglar breaks in
and T. Bent breaks forth into song—
The time of picnics and pleasure jaunts and school treats,
and large armfuls of lightly-clad Girl—
The time of Two Being Company, of Three Being a
Divorce Case, and of Four Being an Unlawful Assem-
blage or Pleasant Sunday Afternoon—
The time when the streets at night are one vast resplen-
dence filled with happy, careless, laughing, chatter-
ing, friendly, jolly people and much-squashed
Babies—
The time of cheers, fears, leers, seers, beers and remi-
niscences of old years—
The time when Virtue and Vice walk together in amity
and it takes a detective to say Which is Which—
The time when even Politicians cease from reviling, and
the Insects are at rest—all except Bent, who is bein«-
heartily walloped throughout 49 constituencies ! °
The time when the heart enlargeth, the liver hardeneth
the head swelleth, and the Boiled Owl singetli
nightly—
Christmas-time !
N.S.W.,
O. C. CABOT.

From “ Oscar Weight,” of Gulgong
(N.S.W.): —
Mention is made (B. 3/12/’08) of “ some pretty big loads
of wool ” in Western Queensland, one of 12 tons being
drawn by a team of 38 horses. That would be quite a
modest load in these parts. Our record load this season
weighed a few pounds off 18 tons, and contained 110 bales
of Springridge wool, and was hauled to the railway by 14
horses. ’Twould be interesting to know the record load
for the continent.
But The Bulletin reckons that, if those 14 horses
had to drag 18 tons over 150 miles of West Queens-
land bush track, sandhill and creek, they would
be a bit weary in the last lap. Hence big-load
records aren’t of much account, anyway—the kind
of track the load has to be dragged over makes all
the difference.
• • * •
“Q.” : Henry Seaton, a walking-round-
the-world crank, is hoofing it from Brisbane to
Sydney. Seaton, who is 6ft. sin. long, is cir-
cumambulating the globe, he says, for the usual
silly wager. He is accompanied by a pair of
Indian clubs, and gives exhibitions of the
whirly art that has made Tom Burrows and
Jack Griffiths notorious, in order to rake in suffi-
cient cash to buy boots and tucker. It may in-
terest Bulletin correspondents who were re-
cently telling the untruth about long walks to
know that Seaton’s biggest day’s tramp is 56
miles. His average, however, is only 22 miles.
STRONG DISEASE, STRONG REMEDY.
A parson, discussing temperance methods at a Vic-
torian Anglican Synod meeting, the other day, suggested
that, as heavy drinkers are never lolly eaters, drunken-
ness might be cured by persistent consumption of sweets.
GENTLE INEBRIATE: “ M' dear young lady, they
tell me lollish ish cure for my complaint.
Will you pleash gi' me pen' orth o’ pep'mints? ”
A STRAY FROM SYDNEY.
THE SPONGE: “Devil of a place Melbourne. The beastly dust spoils the look of everything .”
“ Werrimee ” : The Halfway Hut stood on
top of a steep hill, a mile off the road, and at the
edge of a dense brigalow scrub. Seeing a buggy
and cart and a lot of poultry about, I reckoned I
was in luck’s way, for these things suggested a
married couple, and married couples on lonely
roads are generally good to travellers. About 49
dogs rushed at me. Three black gins ran out and
threw sticks and the scrubbing brush at them,
and a big fat gin chased them with a broom. The
baby toddled after her. She made a swipe at a
half-starved mongrel—and struck the baby across
its middle, and knocked it sprawling. She
dropped the broom and grabbed the babj up by
its legs. Then she performed a series of pirou-
ettes on a bare patch, and danced baby up
and down till its little bingy felt better. I accosted
the'eldest girl, a fine-made damsel of 16, black as
charcoal, but nat-
tily dressed, and
her glossy hair tied
with a yard and a
half of pink rib-
bon. “Is the boss
at home?” I in-
quired. “He’s
away ridin’,” she
answered. “ He’ll
be home sun-
down.” “Is the
missus at home?”
She looked towards
the big gin. That
lady stepped for-
ward. "‘l’m the
missus. You want
ter see me?” “Oh,
not particularly ! ”
I answered, taken
aback. “ I wished
to know if I could
camp here.” “No!
I don’t think my
’usban’ would like
it,” she replied.
“ You camp in the
scrub. Plenty fire-
wood keep you
war m.” “ I’d
rather be nearer
the water,” I said.
“ Any harm to
camp at the water-
hole?” “Oh, no!
My ’usban’ not
objek you camp
there.” As I was
turning away, a
young blackfellow
eventuated from
the brigalow quar-
ter, and I inquired
if he was the boss.
“Oh, no!” said
my lady of color.
“ Him only com-
mon rouseabout.
He work for my
’usban’.” “What is your husband?” I asked,
“ Him manager. All about dis station,” she said,
indicating half the universe with a sweep of her
arm, “ him manage it.” “ I see. He doesn’t want
another rouseabout, I suppose ? ” “0 tear, no 1
One plenty. Very slack time now.” About sun-
down Her husband called on me on his way home.
He was a full-blooded aboriginal, spotlessly clean,
his big bushy beard well combed, and his English
scholarly. Subsequently I heard him playing a
concertina. I met many like him afterwards in
that part of Queensland. His place, of course,
stfltirm • but the white manager at the
was an out-station , but the white manager at tne
head station—a palatial residence—was married
to an aboriginal woman, and all the sub-stations
were in charge of black married couples.
A RIVER SONG.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Bourke’s white and scattered house-tops fade
Like ghosts disturbed by day—
And we are bound for Adelaide,
Two thousand miles away !
We move between the high, brown banks,
From that old sunset town,
What time the engine strains and clanks
Because of Captain Brown.
The fussing, shallow paddles break
The water like a flail,
And up and down our whitened wake
The muddy ripples trail;
And someone on the lonely pier
Has waved a dumb farewell;
He knows the hates that brought us here,
And, knowing, will not tell.
But he could well this answer give,
If he would choose reply :
“ He comes to live the lives we live,
He comes to see us die ;
He comes to trudge the tracks we tread,
And ride the roads we ride —
And our rebellious star of red
Out West shall be his guide.”
An eagle flies above us now
(A mournful bird is he);
The wild ducks flap before our prow
With weird persistency.
And where the dark cool shadows span
The sun’s broad silver gleams,
A lonely, patient fisherman
For ever smokes and dreams!
But Bourke is very far away,
For Louth is at our prow.
And those we left behind us may
For others giggle now.
And lo ! we pass the red-clay banks.
Far, far from Sunset Town —
The engine strains, and throbs, and clanks
For good old Captain Brown !
n.s.w.

No notice (except of a hostile character) will be taken of
communications insufficiently stamped. Name and
address must appear on MSS. No liability is under-
taken re contributions voluntarily submitted, whether
sent by post or handed in; and no MS. will be
returned unless stamps (of any State) accompany.

on occasion, was a favorite aboriginality with Noah dur-
ing the big rain. The opening sentences of it in its
original form invariably drove Mrs. Noah blushing from
the menagerie, and on to the roof of the ark, where she
crouched, regardless of the damp, till the raconteur was
through.

In the “Robur” Tea Co.’s calendar the
pictures selected for reproduction this vear are taken
from the National Gallery. They have been reproduced
so well that they are worth a better fate than to be
thrown away when the calendar has come to the end of
its usefulness. The Trustees of the Gallery are to be
commended for their good sense in putting no obstacles
in the way of the pictures being made available for the
purpose of copies being taken, so long as they are for
distribution to the people, free of charge. The “ Robur ”
Tea Co. distribute these calendars to their grocer cus-
tomers for free distribution to the public. Ask your
grocer for one of these calendars, as they are worthy of
preservation for their artistic merit. •
