PERSONAL
ITEMS
Apropos Judges and pensions. There are
now four N.B.W. Supreme Court Judges on
pensions of £lB2O a year—M. H. Stephen, William
Owen, Gregory Walker, and W. J. (Water Jug)
Foster. The two latter retired after short service,
on the ground of ill-health. Mr. Foster has now
been in receipt of a pension for 14 years. Chief
Justice Darley will make the fifth pensioner, his
annual dole being £2450, which he takes with him
to spend amongst his ennobled kinsmen, the
XXX Guinnesses (Darley’s grandmother was a
sister of the original XXX brewer). The last
“Chief” who retired on pension, Alfred Stephen
(after 34 years’ service), bitterly complained that
he had only the pension of a puisne Judge, and
that he was unable to live upon it. The old man
had some unmarried
daughters and several
wealthy sons. It goes
without saying that he
did not want a meal,
and that there was no
big grocery bill owing
when he died.
THE VACANT CLOTHES AND BRASS
PLATE.
“Mr. James,” of S. M. Herald, goes on
a six months’ tour of the world. “ Mr. James’ ”
years are somewhere between 70 and 80, and he
proposes to lead a peaceful existence for the
balance of his days.
James Mirams, whom the Victorian boom
of 20 years ago swept out of Victorian politics, is
still a bustling citizen, who occasionally fires some
good Australian into the Age, for which he used
to write financial articles 30 years ago. The
ancient will be 70 on Jan. 2, when he hopes to
reckon up 10 married children and 20 grand-
children, which, by the way, is a better record in
children than in grandchildren. Concerning this
aspect of the case, Mr. Mirams might do worse
than issue a sort of pastoral, or encyclical, or
something else with Plain English in it on .1 an. 2.
John Halloway, of Spotswood (Vic.),
achieved a record of a sort the other day, by cele-
brating his 77th birthday, and his completion of
51 years’ continuous work as engine-driiver for
T. Robinson and Co., agricultural implement-
makers. Fifty-one years of firing, greasing,
oiling, and tightening-up the belting ! It doesn't
sound very cheerful; but the patriarch has at least
the satisfaction of knowing that he has been doing
something more for his country than the whirling
drummer whose business it is to visit a fresh town
every day in order to sell imported tape or
tooth-powder.
Mi’s. May Vivienne, whose ambition is to
write a big illustrated volume about each State—
she has tackled S.A., Westralia and Broken Hill
so far has got her book on “Sunny South Aus-
tralia ” into a second edition. But it will
never be as popular as the first, which
contained a misprint of the kind that book
catalogues describe as “curious.” The lady had
been driven out in a buggy to inspect a vineyard,
and it was a hot day, and the grapes were tempt-
ing, and she broke a commandment. Unfortu-
nately, in telling the story, she got mixed in her
commandments (aided therein by the fact that
different churches number them differently), and
she broke the seventh on that occasion !
That simple error sold a lot of copies of the book.
Thus “ Clarence O’Hagan ” :
S. M. Herald sets up a doleful howl, like a lost pup
suffering from nostalgia, because grumpy Gregory Wade
“ has booked his passage for the West, and has been
suffering from insomnia ” ! The miserable old lady (evi-
dently laboring under the delusion that Wade is Ma’s
Last Hope or Bulwark against Socialism) moans that
‘The Premier is under special stress. Everythinsr
centres upon him.” Exactly—that is the trouble. Wade,
who is almost a second Bent without that beauty’s
“comic” songs, imagines himself to be, and speaks and
acts as if he were, King, Queen; Governor, Parliament,
and Bottle-oh all in one. Not satisfied with helping
persons of the Carruthers type to steal wire-netting,
Wade takes upon himself the variegated jobs of (1)
guardian of public(house) morals, (2) chief cusser of the
Commonwealth, (3) gagger of free speech, (4) hopeless
appealer-at-law (at public expense), and (5) General
Blister on the peace and happiness of N.S.W.
No wonder he suffers from insomnia! A man who
spends his days in going through hopeless briefs against
Australia and his nights in Parliamentary sharp practice,
and any spare time he may have in stridulating about
imaginary “wrongs” and “woes,” must expect to find
that he has murdered sleep. And now that he is to go
West to conspire against the Commonwealth with the
wooden-headed Groper Premier, Newton Moore, S. M.
Herald shrieks that N.S.W. can’t afford to lose him for a
single day ; he is Indispensable, and the Constitution
will be wrecked, and things generally blithered if he goes !
If the brutal truth may be told, while Wade has got
insomnia (for which he is to be commiserated), the public
has got the hump over Wade and his narrow, ignorant,
insolent, bitter “ politics.” Wade can go a lot further
than Perth and few will weep. He may stay in Groper-
dom for the rest of his days if he likes, and sleep 14 hours
out of the 24, and 1,500,0000 f Ma’speople will not repine ;
in fact, about 1,499,999 of them would be dam glad. The
other one (theS. M. Herald) would soon get over it.
“Horse Marine ” writes :
Commander Jack Richardson, who now bosses the Aus-
tralian naval forces in Queensland, vice Colquhoun who
lost the number of his mess in Sydnev during Fleet week,
was one of the most popular men in the forces in Victoria.
Originally an officer in the P. and O. service, he
took a commission from the Victorian Government
when that State imported an infant navy and
torpedo flotilla years ago. He became a torpedo
expert, and was regarded as a valuable officer.
But for some reason not yet explained he was
retrenched in 1902, and, npt being a rich man,
he had to take what was offered him in the
shape of a temporary clerkship in the newly-
formed Federal electoral office. That didn’t
suit him worth a cent, and he didn’t suit it,
but he struggled along, learned type-
writing, studied the Electoral Act,‘and
fretted and fumed under the irritating
confinement and routine of a Govern-
ment office. Then at last he got back
into his beloved torpedo boats, and once
more was able to don the gold lace and
practise “ two steps and overboard ” on
the Cerberus in Hobson’s Bay. Now he is
happy in Bananaland waiting for a flood to
give him a chance of navigating the
Gayundah up into its old resting place
among the roses in the Brisbane Botanical Gardens.
The “ Oldest Inhabitant ” writes :
Of the barristers on the roll of the N.S.W. Supreme
Court, when the present Chief Justice Darley came to
Sydney in June, 1862, four, who never held a brief, are
still living—H. C. Dangar (1854), R. M. Fitzgerald (1860),
J. D. Cox (1862), W. A. Long (1862). Of those who had
been in practice there still remain M. H. Stephen (1860)
a retired Judge ; W. J. Foster(lBsB), alsoa retired Judge ;
G. B. Simpson (1858), still on the Bench ; Wm. Oweri
(1859), a retired Judge ; and J. E. Salomons, K.C.,
retired from practice.
“ Historicus ” : In an old copy of Sydney
Atlas, stored away in the Public Library, Mac-
quarie-street; I came upon a letter in the hand-
writing of the late Henry Parkes. It had prob-
ably been placed there as a bookmark and
forgotten. The letter acknowledges the kindness
of Edward Wise, who had lent Parkes certain
boqks. Edward Wise was the father of B. R.
Wise of to-day. He ranked as leader among the
small band of literary Australians of his time.
Before leaving England he had published a number
of volumes. His compilations on the law of riots and
the law of bankruptcy were highly esteemed. He
became Judge of N.S.W. Supreme Court in 1860,
but died about five years after. Mr. Wise was a
large and judicious book collector and an en-
thusiast for the diffusion of education among the
working classes. He presented a very fine collec-
tion of valuable works to Sydney Public Library.
SENATOR WALKER, OF N.S.W.
“ Harrison O.” complains :
The other day Edward VII. had a sneeze in his nose,
or something of the sort, and the stirring news was seized
on by the Cable Trust and sent out here : “ His Majesty
the King has for the past few days been suffering from a
slight cold, but is now recovering.” He then evidently
inhaled some camphor and put his feet in mustard and
water, for next day we were informed : “ The condition
of the King, who has been suffering from a cold, has so
far improved that his Majesty will to-day proceed from
London to Brighton. An official bulletin issued last
night stated that his Majesty is now much better, and
his journey from Norfolk to London has in no way exerted
a prejudicial effect upon him.” Then Ed. went for a
spin in a motor, and the tremendous happening was
chronicled by cable thus: “His Majesty the King, who is
visiting Sir Edward Sassoon, M.P., yesterday accompanied
his host in a motor ride for three hours.” Then next day,
the 12th, Edward had a perfectly beastly headache, and
the item was stretched out to this : “In view of the re-
ports that have been circulated during the past
few days, with regard to the health of his Majesty
the King, special interest attaches to an announce-
ment made in the Lancet, the well-known medi-
cal journal. The Lancet declares, on the best
authority, that the health of his Majesty gives no cause
for anxiety. He is recovering from an attack of neu-
ralgia.” During the next three days it would appear that
Ted managed to contract the ’flu, for on the 15th this
news was sent across many thousand miles of ocean :
“The influenza cold from which his Majesty the King
has recently been suffering, has now almost disappeared,
and his Majesty hopes to-day to be able to attend the
investiture ceremony, to be held at Buckingham Palace.”
Next day somebody noticed that His Nibs was looking a
bit white about the gills, so he rushed off and told the
Cable Trust people, who promptly enlightened the world
with the tremendous news that “His Majesty the King,
whose influenza cold has now almost disappeared, was
able yesterday to proceed from Brighton to London and
attend the investiture ceremony at Buckingham Palace.
His Majesty, it was observed, looks pale.” Truly, it is a
good thing we have the Cable Trust to inform usof these
stupendous happenings. It all reads like a sort of sausage
serial; I have only read up to the 16th of the month, but
fresh items will no doubt transpire day by day, and will
be continued in our next.
A correspondent, writing to a Sydney
morning paper re a name for the Austra-
lian capital, suggests that it should be called
“Hume,” and says that the Murray River
was originally named “The Hume,” after
the explorer’s father, “who was the first
Commissary-General of Australia.’’ This is
incorrect. The first Commissary-General was
Andrew Miller, and he was succeeded by John
Palmer. Andrew Hume was one of the
superintendents sent out by the British
Government. Governor Phillip despatched
him to Norfolk Island to grow flax. He suc-
ceeded some, and had a couple of “pieces
of cloath ” (sic) made; he may therefore be
described as the first flax-spinner. In 1792
Phillip permitted Hume to become a settler ;
he had a grant of 30 acres at Toongabbie,
where he was appointed Superintendent of
Live Stock, and looked after the delivery
of provisions. He lost his billet through
speculating in cattle. Andrew Hume signed
the address of welcome to Governor Bligh.
Major Johnston, when Acting-Governor,
re-appointed him as Superintendent of Live
Stock. He did not hold the billet long, for
Colonel Foveaux dismissed him “as un-
worthy by indications of dishonesty.”
Hamilton Hume, the explorer, was born
in Parramatta in 1797, and died at Yass in
1873.
By The Bulletin’s “ Oldest Inhabitant”:
Premier Janies Martin, in September, 1868, appointed
F. M. Darley to a seat in the Legislative Council. That
was done a month before the second Martin Ministry—
there were three Martin Ministries—ret red. There were
30 members then in the N.S.W. Uppish House, and of
them Thomas Holt, Henry Moore, Alexander Park, and
John Richardson were appointed on the same day.
Darley is the sole survivor of the 30. The Martin Ministry
that made the appointments has also unanimously passed
away. Of the Robertson Ministry that followed—the first
of three Robertson Ministries—only Julian Emanuel
Salomons, Darley’s temporary predecessor as C.J., sur-
vives. Darley has outlived his contemporaries by reason
of his active life; he never rode if he could walk. Of the
Legislative Assembly of 1868 there still remain J. F.
Burns, Ben Lee, and Samuel Lyons.

A SHORTER WAY.
The difficult problem presented by big estates had not
been overlooked. It must be solved. The interests of
the State demanded it, but the interests of the owners
should not unduly suffer. The scheme he proposed was
a gradual process of dissolving big estates, spread over
years.— Bent in his Budget statement.
Why not something that will act quickly and
settle the mattar?
A VICTORIAN ELECTIONS PRAYER.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
VIC.
Lord of the trackless sea and land,
Put out forthwith Thy mighty hand,
And cast from his exalted place
(That bears the stain of his disgrace)
The fatted bull whose ag6d face
Expresses to our common sense
The force of brazen impudence.
Bring him next week to utter grief.
And grant the groaning State relief.
Lord of the starry firmament.
Deliver us from Thomas Bent!
Disclose him to the Public sight
For what he is. Turn dark to light,
And pin the records of his shame,
Who hears a crooked sounding name,
Upon the breast of his ill-fame ;
That matrons stern, behind their fan,
May whisper, “ Oh ! the bad old man ! ”
And giddy girls may cry, “ My dear,
Just think of him as Premier! ”
Lord, ere the present month be spent,
Deliver us from Thomas Bent!
Ruler of all the things that are,
Hurl Tommy from his motor car,
And from the railway car ornate
Reserved for him by Mister Tait;
Let all his deals in real estate,
And what not, be at last laid bare
To catch the breeze and taint the air,
Until his friends, as well as foes,
Must sniff them with upturned nose,
And cry, disgusted by the scent:
“ Deliver us from Thomas Bent! ”
Prompt every voter on tire roll
To do his duty at the poll.
To help one cause bid all agree—
The Cause of Public Decency-
Ignoring, in this hour of need,
All Party claims and calls of Creed,
And aught which can dissension breed:
This is our prayer at morn and night—
“ Incline our hearts to do what’s right.
Lord of the coming Parliament,
Deliver us from Thomas Bent! ”
O.K.

All who are interested in Australian
national politics should read “ The Commonwealth
Crisis,” by.G. H. Kirmess, the great serial now
running through Thk Lone Hand. Forecasting
a “peaceful” Japanese colonisation of the
Northern Territory, Kirniess traces the political
and war consequences with a masterly hand.
See that you do not miss “The Commonwealth
Crisis.”*

The Pills That Cure
RHEUMATISM
That rheumatism can be cured by the use of Dr.
Williams’ Pink Pills is evidenced by the case of
Mrs.'S. M. Bussell, 21 Woodburn Street, Red-
fern, Sydney. She says:—
“I had an attack of rheumatic fever, and after
it rheumatism set in in both legs. I'd have it for
three or four weeks, then I’d get a spell and it
would come on again. I had two stays at the
Little Bay Hospital. They patched me up but
it always came on again. I’d have to lay up
whenever an attack came on. I couldn’t stand.
The house would have to look after itself. My
legs would be swollen and all inflamed. I couldn’t
bear anyone near me. The shooting, burning
pains were agonising. I tried all sorts of lini-
ments and poultices. I couldn’t move in bed with
it. Sometimes the darting pains would go up in-
to my arms till I was crazed with it, and often I
couldn’t lift my arms to do the slightest thing,
such as my hair or dress myself. I couldn’t close
my eyes at night with the agony. I’d feel as if I
were being torn to pieces and would moan with
the pain After suffering like this for years a
friend told me how good Dr. Williams’ Pink Pills
were for rheumatism, so I thought that I would
try them. The second box eased me a little, and
the third did me a lot of good. After the fourth
box every ache had vanished. Now my health is
very good.”

The Australian Parliament has been formally
prorogued till January 27 , 1909.
Since 1901 Iluperfc Clarke has sold 35,740
acres of the land at.Sunbury (Vic.) which “Big”
Clarke annexed, bit by bit." Altogether the land
sold has realised some £210,000, and 1000 souls
now dwell thereon in place of the faint smattering
of humanity that used to make a living out of the
run. So far. so good ; but without a graduated
R*jd Gx to keep the big estate broken up, other
“ Big” Clarkes will presently accumulate this or
other estates, and jiass them on to their sons, so
that they may collar the unearned increment once
again.
It may be the hot weather, or it may be
something else, but Queensland Ministries always
have had the habit of running into one another.
Ihe Herbert Ministry (Queensland’s No. 1)
merged into the Macalister (No. 2). Then
er bert, whose Ministry had merged into
Macalister’s, formed No. 3. Next Herbert’s
second Ministry merged into another
Macahster Ministry (No. 4). Followed
the Mackenzie Ministry (No. 5), Mac-
kenzie having been Herbert’s Treasur-
er. Lilley followed (No. 6), with
Hodgson out of Mackenzie’s Ministry
(INo. o), Macahster (No. 2 and 4),
and Pring (No. 1 and 3). Palmer
(No. 7 Ministry) had been in

It begins to dawn upon the understand-
of the Victorian public that Gov. Gibson-
Garmichael probably consulted John Madden
before taking upon himself to grant Bent a disso-
lution of the State Parliament. The effusively
loyal owner of the waxed lip-hair is an undis-
guised Tory, and as such he naturally doesn’t
disapprove of the awful Bent. Mioreover Mhr-
ray it is understood, thwarted Madden ’in his
application for 12 months’ leave of absence when
he made it awhile ago. Should Bent get back to
the Premiership through the dissolution that Gov.
Garmichael granted after consulting Madden the
latter may be expected to get his leave of absence
without further delay. Which is not to say that
the somewhat hard-worked gentleman doesn’t
really need a holiday.

since political morality has been at such a low ebb
khe desire to make away with
the Bent-tainted institution by the adoption of
the Unification and Reconstruction policy grows
stronger every day. B
The Maoriland Government is
listening hard to anybody who fean tell
it a tale of how to raise the wind.
Lately a farm-seller and financier
struck something new and gorgeous in
t r e rT ay of ideas - R'S plan is that
the Government should invite English
capitalists, small and otherwise, to
send out to the Public Trustee sums
of not less than £SOO for investment
at 4 per cent, net—the Trustee being
“a corporation sole with a seal of
office and perpetual succession, and
whose integrity is guaranteed by the
State.” Joe Ward wolfed the idea; a
confidence-trick man doesn’t love a
new “pitch” better than Joe Ward
loves anew way of coming at the shy
lender. And presumably the “sums
of not less than £500” which Joe
Ward doesn’t want will be put into
lands and things, which the bright
originator of the scheme probably has
some hopes of selling to the Govern-
ment or any other old party.
Victoria s Attorney-General Davies is
showing a vast possession of worldly wisdom
during the present scuffle. He hasn’t, of course
to go before the electors yet awhile, but he has so
far taken the best of care not to help a hard-
pressed colleague on the platform. He has made
no explanations or excuses, neither has he given
his version, nor has he rounded on his colleagues
or abused the Opposition.' He just lets anyone
else take the blame who wants to, and “lays low
and says miffin’.” In the art of “ layin’ low and
say in miffin’ ’ no one can come near solicitor
Davies, the brother of Matthew.
It is characteristic of the amazing muddle
of Victorian politics that Swinburne stiinds in the
Government interest (Bent being the head of the
Government and pretty nearly the whole Govern-
mens), yet Swinburne says that he won’t take
office in a Ministry of which the awful Bent is
Brenner, and he won’t take office in a Ministry
whereof Bent is even a subordinate member. In
fact, he won’t personally touch Bent with a long
pole. And still he is a Bent candidate urging the
State to return a majority in favor of the Bent
Government. This shows a nice discrimination
on the part of Swinburne.
It is reckoned that the population of the
Commonwealth at the close of this year will he
4,278,000. That means that in eight years of
Federation the population has increased
by 503,000. And 251,000 of the in-
crease, or half the total, has been in
JN.b.W. Apparently the down-
trodden Ma State has made as
much profit out of Federa-
tion as all the other five
States put together.
THE RESURRECTION OF ANTI.
The disruption of the party threatens to place the
balance of power in the hands of the Socialists Every
effort must be made to bring- the Anti-Socialists together
once more. —Argus scream in the interests of Bent.
ARGUS: “ Come out of your box, Anti; you're
wanted! Regarded as a policy you're a poor
thing, but we've got no better.”
“Stony”: Malvern is one of the Mel-
bourne suburbs which helped to put poor, lonely,
high-principled Norman Ba.yles into Pawliament’
The other evening there was an open-air concert
in the Malvern cricket ground—move-on pictures,
brass bands, cosy corners and so forth, with an
any-sort-of-coin collection at the entrance-gate.
One solitary, gorgeous motor-car called to take its
owner to the entertainment, and the name of, that
owner can be guessed in one cry. The elections
are at hand, and the results are doubtful, and a
great man will go even to a dog fight these days,
and be affable to both dogs if they happen to come
from his electorate.
Despite the prodigious babble of Bent and
Co. about their attention to educational require-
ments in Victoria, items like this walk daily
through the papers like a procession of insanitary
ghosts
The present schoolroom measures 24ft. by 16ft., ex-
clusive of a wretched lean-to. There are 65 children on
the roll, with an average attendance of 58. The con-
ditions are most unhealthy, and in hot weather involve
positive cruelty to the children. Over nine months ago
the Department authorised the erection of a new school,
but, up to the present, nothing has been done.
It is always “nothing has been done ’’—unless it
is a job or a tram line. Then almost everything
has been done.
If only half the things alleged aWit Bent’s
land dealings are true, the Commission of In-
quiry which the new Parliament must appoint
will have to say some very severe things, if it
does its duty. Murray, M.L. A., said lately that
Bent had been interested in the Maribyrnong
estate, afterwards traded off to the State; and
that he had been similarly interested in the still-
unused Thornbury brick-land. Watt, M.L.A.,
stated on the platform that the Brighton 25 acres]
which Bent is now trying to sell at a big advance
owing to the State-built tram-line running through
it, are not the only paddocks he owns down that
way, and that the Elwood canal might reasonably
have been expected to improve the value of Bent's
private property. The canal, however, has
proved such a failure that nobody will look at
either the State’s or Bent’s land there, but that
wasn’t what was expected. Bent brags of having
paid off his boom liabilities ; but if Peter has been
levied upon to pay Paul. Peter’s views about the
transaction ought to be worth hearing.
Victorian Premier Bent is reaching out
every tentacle he has for votes. Here is a con-
densed list of his promises :
To railway servants : Improved conditions and accom-
modation.
To farmers: New railways ; more land ; £600,000 of
borrowed money to provide increased irrigation, the cash
to he advanced to the local bodies.
To settlers : Abolition of the loading of Crown lands ;
deferred payments spread over 40 years without interest. ’
To landowners : That their big estates won’t be inter-
fered with for years, if at all; and that there shall be no
land-tax.
To miners : Wages Boards, and loans to mining com-
panies.
To the unemployed : Railwa3’s and a vigorous system
of public works.
To the municipalities : More grants.
To Prospectors, Fossickers, etc. : Amendment of the
Gold Buyers Act (Bent’s own recent Act).
To School Teachers: Improved accommodation and
salaries.
To Parents: Improved educational facilities, extra
scholarships, etc.
To the Seripture-in-Schools crowd : A Bible-in-Sehools
Referendum.
To the States Frights crowd : A determination to fight
for State Frights, and the submission of the Murray
Waters agreementand Victorian-S. A. boundary dispute to
inquiry by a .Royal Commission.
To People in General: No new taxes and reduction of
some old ones.
To the Community at Large : That he will get them a
gallon of beer out of a pint pot.
A wonderful promiser is T. Bent.
In four years Bent had about £3,500,000
more revenue than his predecessors. He reduced
the Vie. unfunded debt by £1,700,000, but in-
creased the funded debt by £1,285,000 ; so that he
actually paid off £415,000 out of the £3,500,000 he
had to play with. With the balance—well, he
bought some land.
The strongest condemnation of the Bent
Government’s policy so far offered has been sup-
plied by Mines Minister McLeod, the only Lower
House Minister who was with Adolphus right
through the chapter. He chortles proudly that
the price of country land in Victoria is 30 per
cent, higher than when the Bent Government
began its boom policy, five years back. That is
to say, Bent and co. have made it even more
difficult for genuine settlers to get on the land
than before they started their hypocritical schemes
of closer settlement.
THE RE-CHRISTENING OF YASS.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
It is early to speak of the matter, but it has been
broached and I now venture to suggest that the capital
be called after the King. It would honor the head of the
Empire and the leading man on this planet to-day. His-
tory will not forget Edward the Peacemaker.
If the plain name of “ Edward ” would not do, but we
should soon become accustomed to it—l suggest that a
word be framed in which it should be the groundwork.
“ Edwardia ” has occurred to me, but that could doubt-
less be improved upon.— F. B. Boyce in the Sydney daily
papers.
And why “ Edwardia,” Mr. Boyce?
It seems so short,
So commonplace.
It were a tort
Against the race
That we should pass
This golden chance
To re-name Yass.
But let’s enhance
The township’s mana by our choice.
“Saxe-Ooburg-Gothaville,” I think
Should meet the case.
’Twill both suggest
The Guelphic race
And swift arrest
The roving eye
Of ev’ry wight
Who journeys by
By day or night;
Be he stone sober, or in drink.
“Odalisque City ” also ; and
“ Poker-town,” “ Race-
Burg,” “ Dry champagne,”
Are all in place.'
The dullest brain
Must comprehend
How apposite
They are. They lend
Unto the site
A regaPsavor, courtly, grand.
What’s that ? These names are too ornate,
And inappro-
priate to such
A paltry show
AsYassis; much
As they appeal
To him who, aye
Loving and leal,
Hankers to pay
Respect to monarch, site and State ?
Possibly so. Then am I led
To urge a style
Meet, loyal, brief;
No mighty pile
Of words that grief
Shall bring to those
In whom a sense
Of humor glows;
No name immense ;
But one whose length,
Width, depth and mass
Bespeaks the strength
Entire of Yass..
Hold tight! My final choice is “Ned.”
N.B.W.
SINGEMOINE.
THE INVERSE RATIO.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
During the last seven years the strength of the military
forces in the Commonwealth has been reduced from
29,069 to 22,631, while the cost of administration has in-
creased from £48,543 to £58,084,- —News Item.
We should not object to paying for defence ; but I confess,
With expenditure increasing and the forces growing less.
One is apt to pause in wonder and bewilderment, my son,
As to what is like to happen if this sort of thing goes on !
If is worth owning it is worth defending
Is an obvious proposition no sane man will cavil at!
’Tis a kind of life assurance, or a form of guarantee,
And to pay the needful premiums we should cheerfully
agree!
Yet ’tis not alone a matter of incurring large expense,
For the first and primal object is an adequate defence !
That, of course, we’ll have to pay for, as I think I said
before,
And there’ll be no sense in grumbling if it costs a great
deal more !
But, supposing each Nine Thousand (of increased ex-
pense) or Ten
Means a corresponding decrease of some Seven Thousand
(men),
In a geometric ratio of concurrent rise and fall
HOW MUCH WILL OUR ARMY COST US WHEN WE HAVE NO MEN
AT ALL?
N.S.W.
W. T. GOODGE.

EST. 1818.
Anti-Sosh throws its alleged principles
out of the window when they come interfering'
with its own job. The other day a British
Medical Association deputation waited on the
N.S.W. Government to point out that £200 a
year was not nearly enough to pay a woman
doctor for inspecting school children, etc. When
it’s a case of employing a seamstress, for instance,
the law of supply and demand mustn’t be inter-
fered with ; Anti-Sosh says the lady should be
employed for what she will accept. But when it’s
a case of employing a woman doctor, the blessed
law of supply and demand has to be hung up, and
a “proper” wage paid—not any mere pittance
that the hardest-up woman doctor may be pre-
pared to accept.

women. But, in these enlightened days, women can
afford tolaug-h at the wisdom of the Prince of Denmark,
even as she can snap her fingers at the sun, the great
enemy of the complexion. With impunity she may golf
and motor, hunt and frolic in the surf, knowing full well
that Valaze, the marvellous skin food, will put to rout
every freckle, dispel sunburn, tan and sallowness, and

vents, as Valaze removes, freckles, tan and sunburn. It
is a positive safeguard for the skin against the action of
the sun’s rays. 2s. and 3s. 6d. All chemists, or direct
from Helena Rubinstein and Co., Valaze Massage In-
stitute, 158 Pitt Street, Sydney, 274 Collins Street,

A WOMAN’S LETTER.
Sydney, December 22, 1908.
My Dear Arini,—
On one of our recent lambent, flame-like days
when the weather might almost be vilified with
the phrase, “a devouring element,” I was slowly
turning over the leaves of an illustrated English
paper. In weather that only locusts and lizards
like, one should not do anything hurriedly ; so I
turned that illustrated paper in gradual fashion,
even as a tortoise might do. Well, as I turned
the pages languidly (with a hat-pin) a beautiful,
slender creature about seven feet high and wear-
ing faultless robes and a tiara rose from the con-
volutions of a billowing train till her proud young
head (waved to the minute with the Marcel wave)
touched the big, black headlines that told her
name and state—and frocks. She had a Cupid-bow
mouth, half the size of one of her big eyes.
Her eyes were very big and appealing.
About her long, round throat, no thicker than
the average fat lady’s wrist, was a double row of
jewels, and yet a third row—a rope of pearls—
hung to her waist. One tiny hand held the rope,
thus bending the arm bare to the shoulder, and
displaying the boneless elbow with its “delicious
curve.” (N.B. —The “delicious curve” of his
heroine’s elbow, or cheek, or chin, is dearer than
his favorite nib to the heart of the male novelist.)
The vision looked to have about 19 years to her
life and 20 inches to her waist, hips and bust.
Bringing a mental lever into play, I got my gaze,
off the vision to read what she was wearing.
“Amethyst satin and velvet, in conjunction with
Honiton. Long silver and amethyst embroideries
formed a plastron ” —not a porous plaster—“ down
the front of the skirt and ”
There was a lot more delicate and desirable
reading, ending with the statement: “ A portrait
of Lady Truscott will be found on our third page.”
Lady Truscott is the new Lady Mayoress of
London, and she was the vision that had held me
cataleptic with admiration. That is, a vision
according to the Arbiter Frockiorum who de-
signed the gown. Abandoning the leisurely
attitude and discarding the hat-pin, 1 turned back
to the third page, using both hands and a foot in
my eagerness to see the portrait of this Aphrodite
in the latest directoire gown and hairdressing. . . .
The dots represent the mental hiatus induced
in me by shock. I’m not going to describe
Lady Truscott, except to remark that she looked
very weighty, much brainer than the dressmaker’s
noodle on which her amethyst gown was
hung, and very tired. She reminds of a Sydney
lady who wanted to come into some charity
tableaux and be the stooping lady in “The Sea
hath its Pearls.” A distinguished German
gentleman was on the committee. “ She vould not
do .or the part,” he commented, “but she iss a
very vordy voman and has zehn—l mean ten—
chiltern.”
All through the hot, slow, sleepy afternoons of
last week—just such afternoons as those through
which Lindsay Gordon’s “Sick Stockrider” rode
small detachments of Sydneysiders trammed,
walked, motored,* drove to the tennis courts
at Double Bay to watch the champion-
ship tennis. The court is low and shut
in by rising ground and high houses, which cir-
cumstances make for intolerable heat. As for the
path from “pay here—a shilling please” to the
grass—the person who authorised its being covered
with cinders and ashes ought to be treated with
marked coldness. Ashes blow into one’s eyes, and
cinders scrunching underfoot set one’s teeth on
edge more grittily than the sour grapes our father’s
ate, or our ancestors consumed, or words to that
effect. The play was very dull, but the view
from the back of the grandstand, which is not
boarded up, was otherwise. It costs only sixpence
to sit in that grandstand, but the exhibition of
fatted calf and the amazing heel and wonderful
hose is worth more.
Mrs. Arthur Macarthur has just completed a
strenuous time in a private hospital. Her friends
aver that she is the pluckiest woman in this city,
for though she knew for weeks beforehand that
she would have to undergo a surgical ordeal she
never even mentioned the matter to them.
The Lassetters, father and ex-military son, re-
turned to Sydney town last week, leaving their
wives behind them. The youngest scion of the
house of Lassetter remains at school in Switzer-
land, and his mother stands by to see he doesn’t
slip on a glacier, or stop an avalanche, or engage
in a financial operation with a local waiter—all
manifest dangers in the land of good scenery and
bad hotels. The Lassetters’ Bathurst place has
been let to a branch of the extensive Wigram-
Allen family ; for the grass-widowers propose to
abide all their time at Redleaf.
The “Eugenie lift” has cropped up in gowning.
Most of us heard the term for the first time when
it was mentioned in connection with the trousseau
of the American girl, Miss Elkins, who was
supposed to be about to marry the Duke
of Abruzzi. The Eugenie lift is a little train
to the front of the skirt. If the ex-Empress
Eugenie (presumably it is she who is honored in
the phrase) had big feet, one could understand the
tail in front; but she smote the floor with very
small supports. So perhaps Miss Elkins has the
fore as well as the aft train merely because she
likes the job of holding them up with an ex-
imperial “lift.”
Mrs. “Ted” Simpson left, with her son, for
Colombo on Saturday. She travels aboard the
P. and O. ship whose crew argues amongst itself
with meat choppers, furnace bars and the like.
And there are slanderers who say that our Sydney
women lack enterprise and daring ! Other adven-
turers by the same craft are Mrs. George Hughes
(she was Miss Madge Suttor), the Tooheys, and
Miss Margaret Anglin. I have not heard whether
these people go armed or simply trust in Provi-
dence.
The Lucas Tooth Bart, is receiving an old-world
“welcome home” from his Bega tenants this
week—in fact, the ceremony may be in progress
even as I write. The tenants are said to have
bought two gross of Pansy Novelettes (the
works panned out at something under Id. each—
a reduction being achieved by the acceptance of a
quantity) with the object of studying just how a
Bart, should be greeted on his return to his
ancestral estates. The roasting of an ox whole
represents no difficulty to that cow-pervaded dis-
trict, and barrels of beer are, in the circumstances,
anticipated to be forthcoming without much diffi-
culty. Where the welcomers fear they will bump
trouble is in the details of the jamboree. The
retainers are unskilled in taking the horses out of
the family chariot and dragging the same with
their own toil-worn but faithful hands. Besides,
there isn’t a chariot at Bega. There is no one on
the estate to take the part of the white-haired
family butler, whose job it is on these occasions
to stand on the front steps permitting tears of
affection and loyalty to leak on to his shirt front.
The tenants’ wives don’t know how to “ bob,” and
they refuse to learn. The older cow-cockies
ascribe this disreputable state of affairs wholly to
Socialism, and possibly they are right.
Under the heading “Fuss and Feathers” a
writer in Sydney Telegraph deprecates the aboli-
tion of ali fuss and the whole of the feathers from
military uniforms, and says that what is wanted
is distinction with cheapness. Pondering upon
this demand and filled with pity for the Military
Board, which is struggling with the new uniform,
your “ Akenehi” has planned a distinctive, cheap
and utilitarian dress for swashbucklers. Norman
Lindsay has sketched it for me. “You must
make the tunic a modified Incrovable, ” I ex-
plained, “ to conform to the prevailing fashion in
women’s coats. Pants with three frills, and sand-
shoes, the cheapest shoes on the market ”
“ With spikes in them,” interrupted Norman.
“Your dream of fair warriors would slip when
running away if you didn’t give him spikes in his
sandshoes.”
I allowed the spikes.
“He ought to have a sunshade,” I argued.
“Please draw him holding up his sunshade and
with his rifle on the opposite shoulder.”
“Will I put a bow on the rifle ?” asked Nor-
man. “ The ribbon could be got for twopence,
and the ‘ distinction ’ would be enhanced.”
I allowed the bow.
“Now as to the important item of the helmet,”
I explained. “ The helmet of my design may be
got in almost every back-yard. When a rooster
has done salutation to the glowing morn (as
FUSS AND FEATHERS.
Shakspeare hath it) for three years it is time he
died. The whole outer part of him, with the
exception of the legs, from beak to tail tip could
be dried and blocked into a helmet. It would
greatly resemble the helmet of the early Roman
soldier. ”
“So it would,” enthused the artist, “and the
man in it would greatly resemble the early
Christian martyr.”
“So all the ‘feathers’ and some of the ‘fuss’
could be retained. A man could catch, kill, and
fashion his own helmet. The Defence Depart-
ment need be under no expense for that. Spurs
will, of course, be worn, one behind each ear, so
when my Dream, as you call him, is dancing with
me he cannot tear my frock.”
And then the artist hied him forth and did it,
and the result of his labors appeareth on this
page.
Saturday was a day of many adieux. On every
step of two steamships’ gangways and saloon
stairs a farewell bouquet was reared aloft above
the head of the madding crowd, and on all sides
was heard the dignified phrase of regret: “ S’awf’ly
sorrj f you’re off.” On the Mongolia, Miss Anglin,
with Premier Wade tagged on to her like a creditor
sticking to a debtor, beamed upon a multitude of
admirers. But the most multitudinous multitude
of farewellers to that vessel were there to bend
the spine to the James Fairfaxes, Mr., Mrs., and
Miss. On the Maheno, bound for Wellington
(M.L.) was a battalion to say “ Aurevoir” to Miss
Ola Humphrey, and to assure her that Sydney
would be but a Valley of Dry Bones in her ab-
sence. As it said this it gave her the leak-
ing bouquet of a size to please a gladiator.
It piled the flowers on her until in her blue frock
and etnerald-green winged hat and ruff she took
on all the subtle hues of a flying fish. The
Kolkers, the Titheradges, and the rest of the
H.-K.-T. co. also went along to Ward’s Own
Country, where it will open on Boxing night.
The Rev. F. H. Campion, the Bush Brother,
sometime of Dubbo (N.S.W.), and before that a
figure at Sydney Government House —he who
reckoned all clerics should be celibates till he met
the right girl—is a Father. Not, be it under-
stood, in the Popish acceptation of the term, but
merely in that of the birth-rate statistician. His
wife presented him with a son at West Grinstead
Rectory, Sussex, England, last month.
Miss Ruby Adams is now studying under
Bouhy, in Paris, and absorbing seven lessons a
week.. He thinks so highly of the quality of her
voice and its standard of cultivation that he will
soon put her into his operatic class to cultivate
her repertoire, which will include “ Traviata,”
“ Romeo and Juliet,” “ Faust,” and other master-
pieces.
Since our swell hotels have raised their tariffs
the star mime is on the look-out for a flat or a fur-
nished cottage. Miss Beatrice Day has a cottage
at Lavender Bay; Julius Knight is seeking a
dream of a humpy of his very own during his
season here: and when Miss Ola Humphrey
returns she intends to acquire the flat habit.
Mr. and Mrs. Tommy Burns were at the last
Anglin night—“ The Thief”—and when they
essayed to quit their motor car at the Australia
after the performance, the police had to intervene
between them and many hundreds of demonstra-
tive admirers. It may be true, as Holy Writ
says, that the Almighty has no joy in the strength
of a man or the legs of ahorse. Well, His crea-
tures made up for His. indifference.
A friend sends me this :
The new Hospice for the Dying was opened at Darling-
hurst last Saturday. It is a charity that is worth more
support than most. We all have to die, and by all
appearances, the process is an unpleasant one. The
Sisters of Charity who conduct the hospice take in all
classes regardless of station, sect, or race. It suffices for
them that the applicant is poor, and so hopelessly ill
that a hospital will have none of him or her. By
their efforts the deathbeds of a good many hundred
unfortunates are rendered as cheerful as may be every
year. Those who reckon there is something heroic in a
band of women devoting themselves for life to the task of
caring for indigent moribunds—-a task that is unrelieved
even by the sporting chance that the hospital nurse
always has before her of her patient recovering—might
do worse than send a guinea along to the new Darling-
hurst establishment.
A woman, writing to an English paper, protests
against—-
the never-varying talk as to the personal appearance of
women. It is really tragic that we should for ever be
dogged with this necessity. Very few are born good-
looking, and they themselves have nothing to do with it.
It lasts but a few years.
And the writer goes on to ask—
Is not character, disposition, or temperament, which
are all more or less within their own control and powers of
training, of infinitely more importance ?
The topic has grown pretty wearisome even here
in Australia, for most that is'written on the theme
is copied into the Commonwealth press. All the
Women’s Pages, Ladies’ Columns and Boudoir
Gossip articles fire off at my sex such heaps of
advice about skin foods, face massage and exer-
cises to reduce waist-measurement. A broken
complexion is worse than the breakage of the
whole Decalogue. The fat lady who cannot wear
the Directoire gown is condoled with more deeply
than if she had lost a beloved failed
to get one at all. She is told how to train down,
and to obliterate all but one chin. Anon the
woman with lines on her neck is reminded that
by those lines her age is made known to other
meows, and is instructed in the art of ironing
herself out smooth. According to the gospel of
Saint Face-Massage and the epistle of the apostle
Skin-Food, there need be no bad figures, no
mottled complexions, no poor hair, no lines on
face or neck. “ Hold your head up and your eyes
will appear larger,” says one voice crying in the
wilderness of cosmetics. “Worry makes wrinkles
under the chin,” wails another of the wives of
Solomon Eagle. “Guard against your mouth
collapsing at the corners,” says a third Cassandra,
as if anyone can defy the laws of nature. No
wonder someone has arisen to protest against
being dogged by the personal appearance bogey.
Lord Sholto Douglas, one of the brothers who
befriended the unhappy Wilde in his hour of
need, has stepped into the limelight again. He
has been arrested for shooting a man in British
Columbia, where he owns a fruit ranch. A wire
from Winnipeg to his brother, the Marquis of
Queensberry, stated last month that the fruit-
rancher, on arriving home from a shooting excur-
sion, found there “an ex-officerof the British Army,
whose name is reported to be Roland, and ordered
him off the premises. On Roland refusing, Lord
Sholto emptied the contents of his shot-gun into
his back, injuring him seriously in the head and
neck.” What the ex-officer of the British
army had done to merit such, to put it
mildly, inhospitable treatment hasn’t yet trans-
pired. The Queensberry family is convinced that
it was something fairly reprehensible. “To
describe what my brother did as a case of
attempted murder,” the Marquis of Queensberry
said to a London Daily Mail reporter, “is mani-
festly absurd. We are all perfectly satisfied that
he had ample justification for what he did.” But
the views of the Queensberry Marquis are notori-
ously lax in regard to the plugging of human beings
with lead. He wrote to the London papers only
a few months ago stating that he would empty his
revolver into the first motorist who threatened the
safety of his children in their walks abroad. The
shooting fruit-raiser married a Miss Loretta
Mooney 13 years ago.
“ Mario ” writes from Brisbane :
Strange and terrible are the things that may Happe
to Jones, and these things are sometimes the reason Why
Smith Deft. Home. One of the Jones tribe lives in this
city. He is practically unknown to society, for he plays
so much Bridge that he hasn’t time for the giddy whirl.
One night lately he met at a friend’s house two charming
ladies, and the party hit it off so well that the last tram
had gone before anyone realised the situation. No cabs
were available, but Jones undertook to walk home with
the ladies provided no vehicle transpired en route. Now
Mrs. Dash was not strong, and her husband got nervous at
her non-arrival, and was just preparing a relief gang when
the belated party arrived. “ Good gracious, my dear,” said
Mr. Dash, “ what kept you—and how did you get home? ”
“Oh! we walked,” replied wifey, “and a Mr.—er—l
forget his name —oh, Mr. Jones saw us home.” “ What
Jones?” asked Mr. Dash. “Really, I don’t know,” was
the reply; “just Jones.” Mr. Dash grunted, and the
matter dropped. Meanwhile, Mrs. Blank and Mr. Jones
proceeded to the abode of the former, where Mr.
Blank, a jovial soul, just in from a club, was
wondering where his bit of muslin had got to.
In she came. “I say, my dear, you’re very late,”
remonstrated Mr. B. “Well, so are you,” retorted the
lady. “So I am,” admitted the husband, “ but who saw
you home?” “A Mr. Jones,” said Mrs. B. icily, and
banged the ice-chest. Blank felt snubbed. A few days
later the two husbands met, and the Jones episode hap-
pened to be mentioned. Now two husbands are darkly
suspicious on three grounds : (1) It seems unlikely that
one Jones would take two ladies home on the one night;
(2) it seems impossible that there could be two Joneses
on one night; (3) it is difficult to believe that there is
any real person called Jones. Thus are the simplest facts
distorted by ill-regulated minds.
Yours Affectionately,
AKENEHI.

MELBOURNE CHATTER.
Melbourne, December 21, 1908.
My Dear Yarrik,—
The Carmichael lady, clad in a wreath of roses,
a smile and a simple dress, came down from the
refrigerating room of Macedon Government
Humpy to wind up the Arts and Crafts show and
add her commendation in the disposition of
diplomas. It’s real right-down aggravating to be
a Governor’s wife under such conditions. Just
when she has got used to a luxurious hammock
and has learnt the hang of the mosquito net she
has to get everything disarranged for the sake of
a one-day’s sojourn in hot and dusty town. Yet
beside the lot of a Governor’s wife put that of
a Governor’s gentleman help—but that must stand
over for another story.
Anyhow, the Carmichal lady and one attendant
knight came and handed out the diplomas.
Treasurer Mrs. Box and Secretary Miss Chomley
sighed with relief and faded off for a necessary
rest. Mrs. Box was so busy getting other people’s
things in order that her own pet sampler had to
go on the walls unfinished. Tall Blamire Young
raked in a prize for modelling, and Mrs. Young,
in a lace scarf of adorable antiquity and some other
drapery, drew a diploma for metal work. Pretty
Ruby Lindsay, in a green linen frock, dimpled
all round the show because her exhibited sketches
were sold right away. The Mac George skating
Adonis marched proudly about with a sister who
had worked a prize portiere. Walter Butler had
made a commendable oak cabinet. He also lent
some ancient Greek lamps that gave one an eerie
feeling just to touch. A Sydney girl, Miss
Lichtner, got the highest place for lace.
It was a very hurried and hustled show (though
a decided financial success), and people came
along at the very last minute with exhibits
tucked under one arm and a shilling admission fee
grasped firmly in the other hand. The Exhibition
was not too well advertised, and had not hoped to
develop into an affair of such importance. It
began as a woman’s work show (a sort of mild
continuation of the Woman’s Work Exhibition),
but was made to include men’s work by force of
appeal. It promises to be a big proceeding next
year, for it will be an annual affair. Fred New-
man let his exquisite show of enamels be swamped
in a trade exhibit of Australian cut jewels; but
the enamelling was solely and only his own art
arid craft.
That second performance of “ The Liars ” by
Upper Crust Amateurs, at the request of the
Dudley Countess, brought a well-frocked and
jewelled crowd to the Princess’s t’other Saturday
afternoon ; and the Distressed Actors’ Fund will
be the better for the day’s takings.
The Yarra banks have been made further ornate
by a new boathouse. The University Rowing
Club has put through 50 years since it was
founded by Prof. Irving, at one time an English
Don at our University. So far, the club has
always used other people’s sheds for its boats, but
Saturday afternoon saw the opening of its own
premises. It is quite a beautiful establishment-
built altogether of jarrah timber. The ground floor
is 70ft. long, and upstairs are bathing and dressing
rooms, with up-to-date lockers in hardwood finish,
and balconies all round, looking on to the river and
over the shrubberies and Alexandra Avenue.
Saturday’s ceremony brought a learned crowd.
Sydney University was represented by Mr.
Purvis, and Adelaide by Prof. Darnley Naylor.
George Fairbairn is president of the Henley-on-
Yarra push, and Gyles Turner boss of the Vic.
Rowing Association, and they and their ladies lad
the frivols. Mrs. Mayoral Burston having cut
the Club ribbons and opened the House,
the crowd fell upon tea and strawberries.
Mr. John Lang, an enthusiast in the Henley busi-
ness, and one of the most energetic battlers for
the boat-house, was there. In his honor the club
made his baby son, Andrew, a life member of the
new club house, and the company toasted the'
young recruit.
There is no doubt the rowing clubs are making
marvels of the one-time ugly Yarra banks.
Henley was gorgeous this year, but there
are still many improvements contemplated.
For one thing, last Henley showed a hopeless dis-
regard for the rule of the river. Little tin-pot
craft and squealy, flower-burdened girl-boats got
hopelessly in the way of the racing, and fluttered
helplessly one way and another amid infuriated
shouts. Big Henley-on-the-Thames has its inex-
orable rule —“big boats to the centre, and small
craft to the sides,” and the jumble-muddle answers
as quickly to command as a pantomime ballet.
Motor launches are allowed only their own parti-
cular roadway—the current from their screws
disturbs the evenness of the “ course.”
Another want is an open-to-the-public chance
to. slay a hot-day thirst. Last Henley, unless one
chanced to be a guest of the houseboats, no one
asked the happy question “ Have you a mouth?”
There was only one small tea-room, and even the
wares, of the ice-cream man on his trolly were
earnestly considered.
The Savage Club has a chance to knock spots off
the other elevated Melbourne clubs by making a
poem of the claim it has pegged out on Yarra’s
shore. All the other clubs have merely coal-
barge-like house-boats ; the Savage has a strip of
earth and a tent. Why not make a permanent
stronghold there, give it a name, and let the club
fix landing steps at the river edge of its own strip ?
To ask mamma’s consent, and with reconciliation
papers to be signed, the clever peace-maker
daughter of our only baronet turned England wards
the other day under the wings of the Russell-
Clarkes. During her recent sojourn, in Australia,
Phyllis.collected material for another novel. She
has also collected a heart—that, of Reggie Power,
son of sporting Robert, and a fine fellow to boot.
Phyllis celebrated her twenty-first birthday at
Rupertswood, and the clans gathered. Whereat
papa Rupert, in the fulness of his heart, said the
girlie might make any request of him and he
would grant it. And she did ! “ Come home to
Park Lane and celebrate my twenty-second birth-
day with mother.” To which the fond parent
dutifully replied “I will.”
The sunburnt cabbies of Melbourne opened their
eyes and blinked the other day when a smart han-
som bowled along Collins-street, driven by a vision
in blue coat and skirt, and a neat hat with a
gossamer twined round it. Cabby guessed all
sorts of things, and guessed wrong; for really it
was only a Gippsland girl’s first attempt at tool-
ing the only kind of vehicle she had never
yet handled. The damsel comes from Yarram,
where the giant gums grow, and no seasoned cabby
could have more skilfully carried out the contract
to drive a friend round the city.
Miss Grace Fitzgerald, one of the daughters of
the late Surgeon Fitzgerald, was knotted on
Monday at Scots’ Church. She took unto herself
Mr. Ted Officer, son of the late Suetonius Officer,
of Kallara Station (N.S.W.). The reception of
the wedding guests was quiet and unceremonious.
The bride wore a soft white walking dress
and big white hat, arid dispensed with the
pomp of attendant maids. The bridegroom relied
upon handsome bachelor Hamilton Russell
to back up his part in the ceremony. Professor
Allen handed the bride over to her groom. The
Grices, the Cliveden Clarkes, the Staughtons, the
Byron Moores, and the Brodribbs and a good
many medical friends made the friendly little
party. Then the wedded ones departed for a
short country moon preceding their departure to
France.
The Collins-street.medical crowd feels quite in-
jured that anyone could impute Brown Girl as a
motive for its New Year holiday. It appears
that the destination of the holiday-seekers is not
the isles where the glossy and lightly-clad maid
hangs her feet over the edge of the coral reef, but
the isles in Bass Straits, which are inhabited
mostly by mutton birds and lighthouse-keepers.
The motive is natural history and fishing, and any
idea of Brown Girl is cast behind them by the
medicoes as if it were the Devil. An expert
fisherman and tackle-mender accompanies the
rovers as a guarantee of good faith and Grundy.
It is strange how likings run in families. The
brother of the doctor who absorbed much lime-
light in the divorce case has just married a cousin
of the leading lady in the same show. Mr. Will
Strong, one of the four sons of the ex-ex-Mayor,
was wedded on Tuesday (15th) to Miss Ethel
Nicholson, a cousin of the handsome Steel family.
The event transpired at St. Thomas’ Church,
Essendon, and the cake-cutting frivol happened at
‘‘Woodlands,” the brewery McCrackens’house.
The affair was military to the last degree. Bride-
groom Willie Strong is decorated with S’African
medals, and his best man, Captain Carstairs, had
even more of the brave bawbees hung on his
manly chest. So the cake was severed by a sword,
and the pomp and circumstance of gold lace
and arched weapons surrounded the ceremonious
knot-tying. Lieutenant Armstrong also helped
groom the star actor, and the bride had
Miss Millie Morrow and Joy M'Cracken to weep
over her and carry her gloves and unnecessary
paraphernalia. Baby Bruntoh, daughter of the
only daughter of the Strongs, carried the bridal
train. Dr. “Bob” Strong was in evidence,
though he had no lines in the play. Mrs. Lady
Mayoral Burston led a battalion of councillors in
full municipal pomp. The Senatorial Bests blazed
above the almost-forgotten Snowdons, who gained
their bauble years ago. The McCrackens lent a
wealthy glitter to the ceremony. The best man,
Captain Carstairs, takes a leading part in a cere-
mony of his own, in Sydney, on the 4th of the
new year. He marries Mils Lee, daughter of
Public Works Lee, of N.S.W.
Two pantomimes dashed onto hot Christmasy
Melbourne on Saturday—Her Majesty’s and the
Royal. The Rupert Clarke-Meynell-Gunn gor-
geousity gave a special view of its frilly final dress
rehearsal, when the principals and their minions
panto-ed all the afternoon and far into the night.
The new Pantomime Boy shows something
specially artistic in legs. She is a Gibson Hirl
type, with an Edna May deliberation and dimple,
and a telling ’Murkan burr to her lines. She also
possesses saucy eyes and a few other odd members,
and a sweet soprano warble that only just gets
there. The panto, is “ Cinderella*” and it has
the unusual merit of keeping to its legend. Pump-
kins turn into an eleetric-iighted coach, and mice
into real horses. . But Cinderella Ruth Lincoln
should have chosen something more hatchety in
face and figure for her two step-sisters. (The
opulent Jennie Opie is one of them.)
Ruth herself makes a charming enough
“Cinders ” in her brown rags, but her glad rags
after the transformation lack the requisite-
glory. They consist mostly of a modest little-
white tulle dress without a train, and badly hung.
It is what day-time women call “the dirty
length”; too short to be held up and yet long
enough to almost touch the ground—an impossible
length for grace in stage exploits. A dress must
be either short or long—short to the knee and
frilly, or long with the magnificence of a stage’
train. Elsie Perrin has some cute dances. That
fair young damsel travels with a brother as.
chaperone. He is an equally fair young man who
rather enjoys the job. You see, his sister is only
one of many pretty girls he sees on the journeyings..
The beauteous Bottomley tenor wears d curled
wig, and looks more feminine than the girliest
girl. Cromwell, as the Baron, and “ Mister Rook,”'
as the grotesque stepmother, get in good solid
work above the average of pantomime rot. The
transformation scenes are like dreams of German
Christmas cards.
A witch and a leggy ballet girl or so slipped
round “front ” during the show, and were politely
shoo-ed off by the management; but at a last year’s.
Williamson rehearsal the whole gaudy lot" of
ballet and panto boys came up to the lounge for
tea at the interval. Some visiting baldheads
shivered in shocked delight at so much, joy,. All
the plump-legged ballet boys, in the glory of their
new tights and toggery, sat round in fascinating
propinquity, whilfe the youth of the occasion,
served out sandwiches and soft drinks.
Though there have been two desperate failures,
in bands since the time of the Besses and their
boom, the irrepressible Taits rebound with
news of something special in the way of brassy
blowers. The something special is being shipped,
for these parts right away.
The Madge Mclntosh people have winged
their way off to Perth. They carry two Wounded
with them, and—steeped in superstition—wonder’
who will be the unlucky third. Atholwood lost
his voice and Beatty followed him on to the sick
list with a serious injury to his throat, the result
of a too realistic struggle in “Jim the Penman.”
Gaston Mervale, who grabbed over-f erven tly at
what Beatty breathes with, had the double part
thrust on him at a few hours’ notice, while
Beckett nervously stepped into villain Mervale’s
shoes for the fraction of a week that remained of
the season.
A benefit to the brilliant and unlucky Athol-
wood was the last of a long series of charity
matindes fired at Melbourne audiences. A good
house answered the appeal at the Theatre Royal.
Little Dr. Maloney—he of the boundless popu-
larity and the numerous hair—commanded a solid
wing of ticket buyers on one side of the circle,
and Dorothy Vane Menzies led her contingent
from the north-east box. Mrs. Tom Hogan
spread her brogue and her wings over a flurry
shoo-ed up by the Scotch energy of Mrs. Alan Mor-
rison, one of our political society dames, who has
taken to amateur theatricals in her middle age.
The amateurs opened the matinee bill, much to
the disgust of Augustus in the gods. Augustus
wanted bellowdrama. They that sit in high
places thundered the delicate dialogue of the
amateur’s “Sunset” into . complete eclipse,
and the beautifully-dressed make-believe act-
resses dissolved into tears. A “dashing
duo” from the Opera House then raised the
spirits of the uppermost, and they joyfully settled
down to consider the thick and gaudy coloring of
the vivid “ Prisoner of Zenda.” Julius Superbus
and his minions lent themselves for this piece.
Madge Mclntosh not only played an act of “ Jim
the Penman,” but swayed ecstatically through a
concertina solo with all the abandon of Horace on
the back wheel of a picnic van. The Thornton co.
put in a one-act comedy, and various eccentrics
and balancing freaks filled gaps in a long pro-
gramme.
Dr. Ridley, a youthful Melbourne medico, has
picked out a pretty Melbourne Hospital nightin-
gale for his life’s matrimonial venture. His nurse-
fiancee is a sister of Dr. McWhae.
The Ross Soden mansion at Toorak nears com*
pletion, and its owner, whose eyes bulge with the
fatness of her wealth, is having a triumphant time
explaining to her neighbors the horrible expense
of each startling new item in the building’s com-
position. For instance, the fire hole in the lady’s
own particular cubby is to framed in beaten
silver and mother of pearl. The decorations
will, however, have a beauty beyond the expens-
iveness beloved of the millionairess’s soul, for the
mighty multitude of carved wood linings and
beaten copper gleams has been put solely in the
clever hands of Mrs. Poster-Blamire Young, a
lady who carves beautiful dreams into wood, and,
albeit, possesses a capable eye for broad, general
effect.
From “Rosna,” who is tired of Adelaide :
The air is full of heat and farewells to the Exes. In
another brief week they’ll be sailing over the raging
brine, hunting up an island called’Trinidad, which, when
found, they will proceed to govern. The past week or
two they’ve been torn hither and thither to attend a bout
of school speech days ; and, being quite as fond of youth
as they are of beauty, they have gone to the lot without an
audible murmur. The frilly and frocky women saved up
their pennies, and bought a small diamond tiara to crown
her Ex.’s kindly brow’, and thus show the Trinidadians just
how we appreciated her. An address, wondrously tooled
and illuminated, went with the gift. A gift isn’t a gift in
South Aus. unless it has an address pinned to it.
Her Ex. has been much interested in Viola Byrne, and
had that kiddy and her mother at the Residence the
other afternoon to Say good-bye to her and hear her re-
cite again. The Way dame was also there, and the babe
was much petted by the company.
Little Clara Kleinschmidt has had her concert; and no
previous young singer ever achieved such a triumph
in this village, that’s certain, For a start, with the luck
that ought to follow a voice like hers, the night was
beauteously and unexpectedly cool; and, though the
affair was arranged in little over a fortnight, and there
w’as none of the private-ticket-selling horror, the Town
Hall was crammed to its tallest chimney pot. All official-
dom was represented: Their Excellencies and a party,
the Chief Justice Ways, and the Mayor and Mayoress
were there. The uncommon-looking girl came on to the
platform in a dainty white frock, and stood perfectly still
while she lifted up her great voice for the crowd’s inspec-
tion. For an inexperienced country maiden her presence
is remarkable. In spite of her name, there is nothing
of the Teuton in her appearance. She is a bru-
nette, with a pair of dark, magnetic eyes. As
for the voice, it is so high you think it's a big soprano ;
also it’s so low that it must be a contralto. But there’s
so much temperament and general promise about it that
you don’t care which it is.
The Appendreena Amateurs had an invitation perform-
ance of Hamilton Aide’s “ Dr. Bill,” at the Walkerville
Hall, the other night, and a well tailored and millinered
audience turned up and had a mirthful time. For once
the hall wasn’t quite packed, but that was probably
because the Kleinschmidt concert had attracted society
in bulk; also the tram-ride to Walkerville just now is
a fearful and wonderful experience. The Appen-
dreena folk are rather champions in their ama-
teur way; and the thing was very well put on,
as usual. Norman Malcolm played the name part,
and the chief honors on the other side of the hedge went
to Muriel Craigie. Kathleen Sutherland’s good looks
were concealed under the wig and other decorations of a
fussy old dame ; while Ethelwyn Robin, as the maid, and
Graham Saunders, as the brainless scion of. Johnnydom,
were responsible fora good, large portion of hilarity.
Their Exes, who haven’t missed a ’Varsity commem.
since they came to Adelaide, had many sorrowful re-
marks made to them at that function last Week ; also more
speeches and compliments were handed to Professor
Bragg. Latter, after 23 years hard work at our ’Varsity,
goes to a British appointment, much to the sorrow of the
undergraduate fraternity. Apart from these excitements
it was a deadly dry affair. The undergraduates, for
whom the commem. is generally supposed to have been
invented, were not admitted apparently because some
ancient fossils on the Council were afraid of being made
fun of. Among ad eundems was a D.Sc. bestowed on
Geoffrey Dulfield, the fearfully distinguished young South
Australian who has just come back from Europe to look
after the Australian stars, which he considers are being
neglected.
An Adelaide scribe, writing from London, reports that
South Australian Frank Bradley, is playing a millionaire
in “ The Marriages of Mayfair,” at Drury Lane, a pretty
large triumph after less than three years on the London
stage. She adds that the Australian called, five times
•on his first manager withoutgetting any satisfaction. Then
he told him that London managers made him tired, and
he might be refusing a second Irving if he only knew it.
Upon" which the manager instantly engaged him at
.£1,965,314 a year or less.
Some much-travelled individual has been telling us that,
when finished, Adelaide’s electric-car system will be the
finest in the world. The only blot on this statement is a
suspicion that it never Will be finished. Now, when
iseveral of the lines are ready and waiting, it is discovered
that there is not enough material for all the cars needed.
Meanwhile the ancient horse-boxes are becoming perfectly
acrobatic. The other day one sat down and flung its
wheels broadcast. It was on a single line, but the follow-
ing cars were quite undaunted- They - simply drove
•out on to the open road and passed the ruins that way.
Your old electric and cable trams can’t do things like
'that—see 1
We are waking up ! Glenelg beach has burst into an
open-air theatre, where Pierrots and Pierrettes will occur
nightly. Also, it is soon to have a maze. And if the
authorities had the sense to stop mobs of racehorses from
using it as a training track, it might be the finest beach
in all Australia—or, anyway, as fine a one as any.
Some items from Brisbane :
Our vice-regal fplk departed on Thursday Toowoomba-
wards, and the local shanty is given over to the ghosts of
other days—the Shades of the real live Marquis that once
shone upon us ; of Miss Kennedy, that served a vice-regal
brew in black bottles ; of Musgrave, the gentle, whose
life it is said was shortened in some mysterious way by a
pair of boots (the details of the story were lost in the
flood), and of Henry Wylie Norman, the charitable, the
man who, when remonstrated with for going among the
strikers, replied, “ Where there are working men, there
are no assassins.”
Her Ex., prior to her flitting, entertained the very
small fry of the Crbche and Kindergarten at a Christmas
Tree on Thursday evening. There was a big cake, and
a tree ablaze with candles and toys, which the rep. of
Edward Bex had spent the morning hanging, sticking
and pasting on. I know that he had a hand in it because
I passed him and his missis in a cab going lunchwards,
and he was sucking his thumb where the hammer
struck it.
Our pretty Mrs. Ruthven has gone down to your city
per Omrah, for a set of new frocks and frills. I wonder if
you will admire her Geo. Du Maurier style.
The death is announced of Mrs. Blair, a sister of Mrs.
De Burgh Persse. The deceased lady died in the Emerald
Isle.
Mrs. Robert Philp and sister Ellie go to the land of
apples after New Year; and Dr. Lucas (he of the pawpaw
ointment that will heal the gash in a galvanised-iron tank
in three applications and grow wool on a top hat in a
night) goes to view the London smoke-stacks in February
next. There he will see his daughter, who is just now
battling with the musical exams, of the Associated Board.
That daughter is said to be doing great things for her
country. Another young native of these parts that has
had the.cable-man on her trail of late is Miss Florette
Prior, niece of Mrs. Campbell Praed. She is announced
as a big success at one of the Brinsmead concerts. When
she left these shores, Florence lifted a voice that rang
full but (now and again) it also rang false. No doubt that
little trouble has yielded to much teaching since then.
Yours affectionately,
JOHANNA.

IN TRE HOME.
A vast amount of human misery is
endured, by thousands of men and
women who are never really well.
Headache, sickness, nervous depres-
sion, dislike of food, sinking sensa-
tions, with distressing dizziness and
weakness, make life a daily misery
to such sufferers. And the cause is
indigestion —a stomach that starves
the body, because it cannot digest
sufficient food to keep it well, and
leaves the mind weak, irritable,
depressed. To such unhappy ones
Mother Seigel’s Syrup brings a mes-
sage of hope, brings the ready help
to health, brings the quiet, strong
sense of confidence which only
health can give—health re-made
and regained, through sound diges-
tion. Take it daily after meals.
Mr. T. Fitzgibbon, x, Hugo Street, Redfern,
NSW writing on March 18th, 1908, says :
“ For years I suffered most severely from
acute indigestion. Nothing did me any good
until I got Mother Seigels Syrup. The
first bottle enabled me to eat and digest,
and soon I was quite cured.

The Inky Way.
Hall Caine, in his autobiographical sketches, deals in-
terestingly with Blackmore, who seemingly was his dear
friend and candid critic. The generation that can t
read Dickens ” or understand how he could have far out-
rivalled all other novelists of his time in popularity, will
be surprised to hear that Blackmore, the author of
“Lorna Doone,” could read nothing but Dickens in his
latter days when his health was shattered. The combina-
tion of lurid sensationalism and comic relief in his narra-
tives was only half the secret of Dickens success. Nor
was the strength of his exaggerated character pictures re-
sponsible for the whole of the other half of his large circula-
tion. Some of the charm was in his literary style, which
to the modern short-story reader is intolerably redun-
dant. Blackmore, although he didn’t come to light, as a
novelist until the year before Dickens death, had
acouired his own literary taste during the era of Dickens
and Thackeray and their disciples, when the best novels
in the language were some of the longest. And of all
those popular writers Dickens wa,s the easiest to read.
Jealous contemporaries called him ‘ gushy, pedants
scoffed at him for his want of culture, many Irishmen
and Scotchmen almost hated him for his utter
Englishness and his London prejudices. But up to the
time when long novels were displaced from current favor
by short novels (because the best of the new writers
lacked the old writers’ capacity for sticking to their job)
the Dickens style pleased the popular taste of English-
men, and the sons of Englishmen all the world over.
Blackmore was English, but he was born and bred out oi
London, and was certainly not a townsman at heart.
“Lorna Doone,” the most remembered of his novels, is
admired to-day by a generation that affects to despise the
great melodramatic humorist, of whom Blackmore, in his
sick old age, said, “ He is the only one I can readln?w.
Here is matter for discussion at the next monthly me
ini? of a Dickens Society.

“Newsboy”: Apropos all this chat about Maurice
Brodzky. X met him shortly before he left Mel^°“*'" e - *
was at the time on the staff (such as it was) of the n
defunct People’s Daily. Brodzky was editing a little
four-page sheet called the Sporting News, alsonow de-
funct. (What a lot of papers are “ now defunct by the
way!) Brokzky had dropped into the People s Daily
office to see if it required an editor. He expressed to pie
his horror at “ dee mismanagement, and dee extrava-
gance ”of the Daily. (Brodzky did speak pidgin Eng-
lish ”as far as the word “the” is concerned, at any rate,
notwithstanding “ L.E.’s ” assertion to the contrary.) He
was sure that if he had charge of the People s
could make it pay. In course of conversation Maurice
informed me that the total cost of Producing
the Sporting News was under £4 per ' v ' eeK '
Remarked Brodzky : “ People think it costs a
great deal to run a newspaper, but they can De
produced for very little if one knows dee ropes. Api opos
“L.E.’s’’remark about his writer friends fund of odd
corner anecdote, and his unconsciousness of its value.
Brodzky informed me that he was doing a column or so
of this class of matter every week in the Sporting News ,
“and do you know,” he added, surpnsedly, dee public
seems to like it, for dee sales have increased immensely
since I began it. I intend to give more space to it in
future.” It' was characteristic of Maurice to cast his
pearls before swine, and to make the leading feature of a
paper supposedly devoted to sport a column of matter
dealing fascinatingly with everything except sport, as i
remarked before, the Sporting News is now defunct; but
whether Maurice killed it by devoting its columns to
translations of the Hebrew prophets instead of informa-
tion about ’orse and stoush and dead birds, will never
be disclosed.

“Scribe”: The late David Syme’s worst mistake was
the attempt to work the Herald with the Age, afternoon
with morning paper, lifting the metal about. The papers
were called the Symese Twins. Jardine Smith wrote for
Syme on the Herald. Syme thought him no good. He
went to the Argus and wielded the most brilliant rapier
there, with his daily onslaughts on the “ Berry Blight,
which expression he is said to have invented. It sounded
good, like the Braddon Blot, but Victoria would give any-
thing now to have the old Berry back, and the State
Premiers are shouting for the return of their Blot. ‘ The
Blot that the builders rejected, the same has become the
head of the corner.” When G. P. Smith left the Age, H.
J. Smith replaced him, with a bludgeony burlesque on
G. P.’s slaughterous style. His work was credited to G.
P. Smith. H. J. Smith, though a heavy swell, was known
as “ the baker,” having followed that trade in Dunolly.
He went to the Argus as head of the staff.
THE LOST POET.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
He was born to stir the spirit of his race to tears and
laughter; .
He was born to be the writer and the singer of his age.
For he had the gift in keeping, and the generations after
Should have held his name, “ The Deathless,” as a glorious
heritage.
His the gift of dream and wonder, his to see with clearer
vision
Through the old pretence and fashion, to the heart of
Hidden Things ;
But his people stood before him, and they barred the
gift’s fruition,
And they told him of the madness that such dreaming
ever brings.
They had words to crush the yearning of the high ambi-
tious spirits,
And they held his spirit downward as it struggled to the
light ;
And they whispered to each other, “ ’Tis the madness he
inherits
From the Father and the Mother ; let us guide his steps
aright.”
So they dragged him slowly downward—he was young and
very lonely,
And he turned him from the starway, and he made their
cares his own.
And then—Ah, shall we blame him, if he sunk a short
while only
To the level of his kindred, for the wrong was theirs
alone.
Then he woke as if from slumber, but the starry path was
And^the 6 spirit world was shrouded, and he sought its
light in vain. , .. , .
And the gift of song went from him, as it came to him,
unbidden;
He had turned to meaner worship, and he might not sing
again.
So he bade farewell for ever to the glowing spire and
steeple,
To the strong blue hills a-shimmer, to the brightness of
the sun,
And he left the youth behind him, that was slain by his
own people,
In a grave rose-piled and hidden, and the poet-life was
done.
Then he wandered forth a rover, and the dark and silent
spaces
Never knew the sound of singing by its magic lay un-
crossed ;
But the wrong went up to Heaven, and the angels bowed
And the stars were veiled and hidden for the poet that
was lost.
N.S.W.
NELLIE A. EVANS.


Mr. WILHELM GANZ, one of the leading musicians in London, called, as did many other musical celebrities,
at our stand at the Franco-British Exhibition, played upon and tested our pianos, and afterwards sent the
following purely voluntary testimonial :
Messrs. Beale & Co. Ltd.
November yth, 1908,
8j Onslow Gardens, S. IV.
Dear Sirs,
It gives me great pleasure in stating that I consider the Grand Piano-
forte, which you exhibited at the Iranco-British Exhibition, a very first-rate
instrument. I much enjoyed playing on the same. The touch is excellent, like-
zuise the tone —I especially admired the wonderful tone of the bass. In fact I
hardly remember a pianoforte with a more resonant sound, and A zistralia may
be very proud of your having produced such a fine instrument.
With every good wish for your success in the production of these pianofortes,
/ remain,
Yours very faithfully,
(Sgd.) WILHELM GANZ.
Inasmuch as Mr. Ganz has played thousands of pianos during his lifetime, and is one of the fore-
most musicians of the day, his opinion can only be regarded as of the highest value.
The Beale Australian Pianos have been awarded the Grand Prix, two gold medals and a silver medal
at the Franco-British Exhibition, 1908.
Exact duplicates of the pianos sent to London may be seen at any of our showrooms.
