HEADACHE.
The First Symptom op Every Form of
Disease.
Sick and nervous headaches are but the symp-
toms of other ailments to which they are merely
sympathetic, such as indigestion, biliousness, con-
stipation or some other irregularity of the system.
Many women habitually suffer from headaches,
which make life a daily purgatory. Indeed, this
complaint may be regarded as peculiarly an affec-
tion of the feminine sex, an ailment common to all
women from girlhood to old age.
‘• After years of suffering with sick headaches,
writes Mrs. Josephson, 14 Parsons Street, Rozelle,
Sydney, “I was induced by a friend to try Dr.
Mrs. Josephson.
(From a photo.)
Morse’s Indian Root
Pills, and although I
was dubious of their
doing me any good I
made up my mind to
give them a trial, and
before I had finished
the first bottle T felt
considerably relieved.
I kept on taking the
Pills occasionally, until
I had taken three
bottles in all, and I
found I had quite over-
come my ailments;
they have made quite a
new woman of me. It
is over twelve months since I took the Pills,
and as I have had no return of the trouble I can
honestly recommend them to anyone suffering as
I was. You can use this in any way you like.”
To overcome headaches it is absolutely neces-
say to get at the cause. Drugs which act on the
heart and reduce the pulsations only have a tem-
porary effect. If you suffer from indigestion,
biliousness, constipation, disorder of the liver and
kidneys, or from any weakness peculiar to your
sex, there is no better remedy than Dr. Morse’s
Indian Root Pills.

SPORTING
NOTIONS
Johnson has left his quarters at Botany and
removed himself and his retinue to Manly by the Sea, and
the spectacle of Johnson streaking’ over sand dune and
seashore is an added charm to that delightful suburb.
Said that Johnson’s trainer is in great grief, hor
three weeks he has been trying “to dry up the big
man’s bingey and toughen it, but owing to the sultry
weather and the vital necessity for liquid the warrior
absolutely refuses to dry up. Johnson’s admirers are
much impressed bv the fact that he caught a hare in 10
nlinutes and laid by the heels a greasy pig in quite a few
seconds. But this writer is not impressed. Catching a
hare is a matter of trickery, rather than of speed or
agility, and writer has seen men who couldn’t run for
their lives catch hares in the open paddock in old Cantei-
bury, in the days of his boyhood. The greasy pig is quite
another proposition, and writer cheerfully confesses his
ignorance of what that indicates. His sole acquaintance
with greasy pig has been confined to the dining table and
to dealings with capitalists.
In fact, the air is full of disquieting rumors re
Johnson’s training. The lying jade who occasionally
tells the truth has spread it that Johnson’s training has
consisted largely of champagne and female society, inter-
lineated with streaks of terrifically hard work. Many
of these rumors are due to the fact that another
negro also named Johnson is swaggering around city
bars and being mistaken for the fighter. Writer
knows personally that the colored champion has done
tremendously hard work, and to the touchy his arms, legs
and shoulders are as hard as vulcanite. His strength is at
any time abnormal, and with the exercise he has been
taking it is now almost phenomenal. He can take three
heavy men, one after another, with their hands clasped
round his neck, and give them the giant swing
without perceptibly increasing the speed of his
heart action. Sparring with Lang, the Australian
champion is a baby in his hands, and could be laid out at
any moment. In the clinches, he has a curious lock-
action, by which the Australians who have sparred with
him have found their armsrendered powerless and in-fight-
ing made an impossibility. Not a boxer in the city but
savs Johnson must win, and win easily. In all sparring
exhibitions he has shown amazing skill in defending his
head and face, and not one of the sparrers could land a
really effective blow in the neighborhood of his jaw. And
thatj briefly, is the case for Johnson, the big, brawny,
good-natured, over-grown boy.
And this is the case against him: Burns
shoulders are Atlantean, and his arm at the biceps is as big 1
as the leg of an ordinary man. That arm tapers down to a
fist that is much smaller than that of the average
man, and the arm is really a wedge of bone and muscle.
The white man has trained hard and carefully, and no
hint of Babylonian orgies wanders around concerning
him. Judging by the exhibitions given by both
men, Burns is faster on his feet, and hits more
quickly and with much more weight behind the
blows. Johnson hits with his arm, Burns with
his whole weight. The difference is tremendous.
Johnson’s sparring partners have, one and all, led for his
head, and he has brought the protection of his nobility to
a fine art. But it is not his head he will have to defend
when he meet Burns, but his midriff. His diaphragm, is
admittedly his weak spot. For weeks his trainer has
been trying to “ dry it up and toughen it,” and it is said
that owing to the inclemency of the weather and the
necessity for liquid refreshment Johnson will not dry up.
But this is mere rumor. 'Vet certain it is that not once
in all the sparring matches writer has seen Johnson en-
graged in has he been called upon to defend his
stomach. And it will be there that the yiant
blows of Tahmmy will land in quick succession on
Boxing Day. Johnson’s extra height of sJin. is
increased by Burns’ peculiar “ American ” crouch till the
only part of his anatomy exposed to Johnson will be the
hinder view of him upside down. Possibly Johnson may
knock him out by hitting him on the tail of the back.
Upper cuts will be Johnson’s most effective blows, ana
the upper cut will probably be met on Burns’ glove. And
all the time Johnson’s diaphragm—which will not dry up
—will be exposed to the quick, savage, jerky blows
behind which Burns will put his whole 13 stone. Writer
examined the Johnson diaphragm critically of late, and
found it “a fair round belly with fat capon lined.
It was soft-looking, and resembled a little heap
of sheeny brown satin. Johnson does not per-
mit callers to make free with that part of his
anatomy. It has dimples in it, and a rude touch might
destroy their symmetry. Burns’midriff, on the other hand,
is not a thing of beauty. It is a straight streak of some
hard substance resembling vulcanite, bleached out some-
what indifferently, with ridges on it, and any caller is
permitted to make free to the extent of whacking it with
his fist and proving it to be composed apparently of
leather backed with lead. In plain English, Burns has
trained himself to a point at which all his vitals are pro-
tected with a thick coating of muscle and hardened tissue,
and Johnson has not.
The Burns method of destruction is scientific.
Having located the weak spot, he hammers away,
directing all his blows for it. In the case of Johnson, the
weak spot is the part of the colored man’s anatomy most
easily reached bv the white champion. Therefore, purely
on observation, the writer tips Burns as the winner of the
contest somewhere between the 9th and 13th round. Ut
course, there is always the random blow to be reckoned
with All fights are gambles. But on the form exhibited
Burns should certainly win, as aforementioned. Barring
the random swipe, Johnson, if he is to win, will have to
show a degree of skill and speed not so far indicated in
any of the exhibitions that he has given. .
The above remarks are based on personal ob-
servation. They are borne out somewhat by rumor. It
is said that although Johnson is quite able to do it, he
has not, so far, covered the £2OOO deposited by Burns
with Mr. Corbett as a side wager. It is also stated
that there is a further sum of £2OOO offering on Burns at
fair odds, the only stipulation being that the money
must be wagered in hundreds ; and it is alleged that
up to time of writing, none of Johnson s admirers
had aonlied for any of it. On the other hand, some of
the Johnson party on Saturday last took £5OOO to £3500.
So all is doubt, confusion, and general cursing.
* * * *
The question of the appointment of a referee
has occasioned much trouble. Nathan would not act
and there wore difficulties in the way of choosing a good
man. On Monday morning the two principals met
at the office of the Mclntosh and discussed the
question. The Mclntosh submitted various names, wind-
ing up with “ Snowy ” Baker and Colonel Byrie. Johnson
absolutely refused Baker on account of youth, and
objected to the gallant Colonel as not having enough
experience. As these two just about exhausted the list,
something like a deadlock has been reached. Under the
articles, the Mclntosh has the power to nominate a referee
regardless of either or both the men’s wishes if they fail
to agree upon one. Under the circumstances the only thing
left to the Mclntosh is to appoint himself. He knows the
game from A to Z, and he is deeply interested in having a
square battle, for long after Burns and Johnson have faded
out he will still have to live in Sydney. The present position
is that he must either force one of them to accept someone
he has objected to, or find a new man. No matter who
is appointed, men who have laid money on the affair will
make efforts to influence him, and as all the rest of the
reputable men have been objected to by one or the other,
the Mclntosh can do nothing else but referee Jthe big
fight himself.
* * * *
“Leonce”: Herewith my summing-up of the
probabilities in next Saturday’s conflict. First, to deal
with the allegations of “ cronk business,” that are invari-
ably bandied about in connection with a championship
fight. In this case, they seem to me not unjust merely,
but insane. The Heavyweight Championship of the
World is such a priceless asset these days that it is pretty
well unpurchasable. The offer of £350 a week that Harry
Bickards has made to whichever man wins on Saturday
is but a fleabite by comparison with the salaries the
London, New York, and Paris halls will pay later. There
is an idea abroad that Johnson’s color will debar him from
getting a scrupulously fair deal. This I can’t believe.
Apart from the fact that the officials selected are men of
repute, it has to be remembered that there are keen
judges outside the ring that have to be satisfied—twenty-
five thousand of them. Also that the Mclntosh is a
wealthy and respected citizen with a host of irons in the
fire apart from stoush-promoting ; one who has all to lose
and nothing to gain by the scandal of a bad decision.
No ; if ever a pug was in for a fair deal Johnson is on the
26th.
The points in Burns’ favor are easily told.
(1) He is at the exactly right age to engage in
a heavy-weght event. Just as the bantam is
at his ‘top from 16 to 20, so the heavy-weight is
most formidable from 26 to 30. Burns is rising 27,
and has probably never been stronger or healthier m his
life than he is to-day. (2) Burns is the fastest heavy-
weight the world has ever known.. He combines immense
power and weight with the quickness and lightness of
foot of a Griffo. (3) He has a punch. (4) He is game.
Johnson is a better boxer than Burns. The way
he has toyed with Australia’s best during the last few
weeks has been as wondrous as it was depressing, bound
critics swear that he is as good as Peter Jackson ever was.
His condition seems just about perfect. Onlookers who
have no sympathy with the black man’s cause own that
if he will but “ fight” Burns he should win. But will he
“ fio-ht”? He is said to hold the terrific kicking jabs of
repertory in high respect. By standing on and
“ boxing ” the little man he may hope to avoid the deadliest
weapon in the Burns armory, and get a decision on points.
But that way lies the possibility of Burns scoring a clean
knock-out. Johnson is nearly two stone heavier than
Burns. He has easily accounted for everyone he has
fought of late years with the exception of Marvin Hart;
and he challenges the decision in the Hart battle.
Against Johnson it may be noted that he is but a month
or two short of 31—a fighter is getting no better after 30.
Also he appears on paper to have but a moderate
“ punch ”by comparison with Burns. A large number of
the Johnson victories are points decisions. Johnson s
e-ameness is understood to be nothin# out of the way.
It is certain, anyhow, that the brand of it that he
possesses is considerably inferior to that owned by Burns.
If I were invited to prophesy on this most baffling event
I should forecast that the white man will knock out
the Ethiopian somewhere in the neighborhood of the 15th
round.
* * * *
As an instance of the way the assault-and-
battery microbe has fastened on Sydney, there were some
15 000 people at the Stadium last week to see Les O Don-
nell and Bob Bryant deal it out to one another. Tommy
Burns seconded' O’Donnell, and Jack Johnson did the
same kind office for Bryant. Both lads were announced
as “under 11.4,” but the difference between them was
that, whereas O’Donnell was but a pound or two below
the middle-weight limit, Bryant weighed little more than
lOst. A gamer battler than the Johnson nominee
never stepped into a ring. For 19 rounds he bored in on
his big opponent, and gave him Sheol about the body.
In the out-fighting O’Donnell scored, and in the 20th and
last round he handed the little man some very sultry
punches about the face. “Snowy” Baker, who refereed,
gave it a draw, and that was doubtless the correct deci-
sion. though a big section of the audience reckoned that
Bryant deserved the verdict on the points he had piled
up during the earlier stages of the fray. Burns and
Johnson livened things up by engaging in a furious argu-
ment before the event of the evening began, and for a
space it looked as though the pair meant to forestall next
Saturday’s flare up. However, it was not to be. The big
and the little giant retired to their respective corners,
and there gave their opinions of each other in the hushed
tone that militates against a breach of the peace.
* * • *
The skv is clearer and the gentle zephyrs more
balmy than usual in Sydney just now’. The threatened
resignation of the McElhone from the secretaryship of the
Australian Cricket Board of Control is a dream of the
past, and Fighting Mac will continue to swing the axe
till the question of allowances to players is settled.
On Friday night, the Board of Control sat with
closed doors and did things. The next Australian XI.
goes to England under the management of the Board,
which will collect_ all the money and retain 5 per
cent, out of the first £6OOO taken, and 12J per cent, from
all subsequent takings. The balance it will divide
equally among the players, and thereby abolish the scan-
dal of players being admitted to the XI. on condition
they play “on terms." As the Board figured it out on
the records of previous elevens, this means about £IBUU
to the Board and £7OO or £BOO to each player and the
manager. Immediately the terms were announced, Mr.
Clem Hill, himself a member of the Board, but absent in
Adelaide, announced that he would not go to England.
Mr Noble also announced his intention of staying
at'home. The prospect of making £7OO or £BOO
is so ruinous to both these gentlemen that they simply
cannot allow their sporting instincts to get them down.
To The Bulletin, which will not be included in the il.
ifnder any circumstances, and therefore may lack that
keen perception born of personal interest, it seems that
the Board has been foolishly generous.
• * * *
The end of the sixth round of Sydney first-
grade cricket on Saturday last saw some tall smiting.
No less than five centuries were smacked up by Clarke
(1411 of North Sydney, Kerim (118), of Redfern, Way (126),
of Redfern also, R. Minnett (109), of North Sydney, and
Thomas (105 n. 0.), of Sydney. In addition to these,
scores of 50 and thereabouts were also fairly plentiful.
A curious feature of theround was that it yielded
not one three-point win, and all matches were lost and
won on the first innings. In its match against Waverley,
Gordon made its big score in the wrong innings, going
out in the first attempt for 102. Waverley notched 233,
and then Gordon went to the wickets and scored 251
for the loss of seven wickets. But it was no
good. Time ran out and Wade’s suburb lost the
<'-ame by 131 runs. Burwood has achieved the reniark-
alile, and has at last won a match by making 16U and
then disposing of Petersham for a miserable 93.
It thus arrested the bun with a margin of 68.
Sydney was again a chopping-block in spite of the
efforts' of Thomas, who unfortunately made his big
score in the second innings. The first ended for
220, but North responded with 339 for seven wickets, and
Sydney hopes went up the spout. Glebe, which the
previous Saturday closed with eight wickets down for
362 smashed up Balmain for 206, and secured a margin
of 156. Central Cumberland walloped Redfern by 257
runs on the first innings ; then the Syrian suburb bad the
mortification on Saturday last of seeing two of its men
pile up 244 between them, and all for nothing. Padding-
ton just managed to down Middle Harbor by 46 runs on
the first innings, the figures being 218 to 172.
The result of the round is to leave Glebe in
possession of the box-seat with 14 competition points,
and Central Cumberland next best with 10 points,
while Waverley and University have eight and six respec-
tively. After that there is a great gulf wherein nine
clubs sit in various degrees of darkness and deficit, rang-
ing from one to 11 points behind nothing.
• * * *
A marvellous cricket coincidence occurred at
Kaiapoi (M.L.) recently. A bowler, named Mason, in a
team from Christchurch, did the hat trick in the first
innings and repeated the performance in the second, the
victims, strangely enough, being' the same three batsmen
who were disposed of in the first innings, and they were
bowled in the same order.
» * * *
The victorious walk-over which Arnst scored
ao-ainst Webb is further evidence that, in rowing, weight
and brute force count for a lot, and that the skilful big 1
man is better than the much more skilful little man.
The weary tales of how Arnst had developed a tendency
to carry his head on one side, and jerk his left elbow out
when walking, or something equally ominous, have all
been discounted as mere writers’ flam. The unfortunate
scribe who has to fill so much space in his paper with
gossip about two men who are going to pull a race, or
fi<rht a scrap, counts for little in the da} 7 of reckoning.
Man for man, Arnst had greater strength of arm,
back and leg than Webb, and he was able to put
sufficient of that extra weight and strength into the
motion of his boat to beat the man who is admittedly the
more graceful and finished sculler of the pair. The race
was according 1 to Webb, decided in the first mile and a
half. Arnst sprang to the front, and Webb, after vainly
spurting till a mile and a half had been covered, realised
he had found his Cannae and was donkey-licked. He did
all that a brave man could do by pulling gamely to a
finish and then owning up that he had been fairly beaten.
Arnst took his victory quietly enough, and announced
that he is in the business for money. Therefore he will
row' where the most money offers.

The final of the 18ft. championship of N.S.W.
on Saturday last resulted in Australian running easily
into first piace, followed by Donnelly, Vision and Eileen
in that order. This gave Australian the right to repre-
sent N. S. Wales at the inter-State carnival at Perth.
Oweenee, w’hich w'as ahead of Australian on points, was
biffed out of Saturday’s race by measurement restric-
tions ; and, in any case, the skiff could not have repre-
sented the State at Perth. In Saturday’s. contest Aus-
tralian was eminently suited by the conditions, and in a
favorable environment Christian Webb simply walked
over everything else.
• • *
The swimming contest at the new municipal
baths in the Sydney Domain, on Saturday, for the 300yds.
championship of the State resulted in the coveted honor
changing owners. Cecil Healy, the previous holder of
the title, was on hand to defend his top-knot, ana swam
like the good one that he is. Blit at 250yds., amid tre-
mendous excitement, Harold Hardwicke suddenly
spurted, wiped out the 2yds. between him and Healy, and
challenged the champion. Healy responded, but Hard-
wicke went away like a porpoise and won out by a good
6vds. in 3min. 37 2-ssec., which was 4 2-ssec. better than
Healy and 5 3-ssec. outside Keiran’s world record for the
distance, put up in Farmer’s baths in the same quarter
on February 25,1905.
The carnival also took on the form of a welcome
to that sterling all-round athlete, “Snowy” Baker, who,
with F. Lane, another well-known natatorial artist, gave
an amazing display of high and spring-board diving. A
new feature brought back from the old world by Baker
was the “swallow dive,” in which he flew down with a
beautiful bird-like motion and entered the water like a
swallow disappearing into a haze of sunlight, ihe
Catherine wheel dive is an extraordinary evolution, in
which three men holding one another hurl themselves in
a solid mass off the tower, and, revolving like the fire-
work the evolution is called after, enter the water more
or less gracefully.
At the same time, Beaurepaire, at St. Kilda,
Melbourne, won the 440yds. swimming championship of
the Bent State in smin. 55 2-ssec. This doesn t compare
with Kieran’s smin. 19sec., made at Lavender Bay (Syd-
nev) on April 1, 1905. But “ Bogey ” won by 20yds. and
the absence of push no doubt accounts for the stripling s
rather slow time.
Talking of swimming brings the mind to surf-
bathinv. Writer spent an hour on a recent Sunday
afternoon on Manly Beach, and while there no less than
three rescues were effected. What struck him was the
sheer insanity of the people who, being obviously unable
to swim, yet go into water that is full of shifting currents.
When rescues were effected the life-saver went out with
the line and fastened a sort of life-belt to the Person
was in difficulties and drifting over Jordan. Why doesn t
each bather wear some sort of lifebelt all the time not
necessarily a large one which will hamper him very much,
but something sufficient to keep him afloat till help can
arrive should he get into difficulties? Of course, anyone
eoing in' at present with a cork breast-plate would be
Duved, but if the Council insisted upon such a thing being
worn it would probably increase the number of surf-
bathers, and reduce the list of those who get drowned
and injured through ignorance, inability to swim, and
the sudden changes of the currents. In fact, writer will
give a faithful promise that, if the Council will insist upon
the cork breast-plate innovation, he will himself shift
down to Manly for the summer and display his own
Apollo-like figure in the breakers at least once a day.
And with his physical beauty and Johnson s stomach
both on view simultaneously, Manly simply must boom.
* * * *
Buried away in Forbes (N.S.W.) is a man who
would be invaluable in another place—plenty of other
places, in fact. He is, or rather was, secretary of a bicycle
club in that western town, and this is how he lashed out at
the club and all concerned with it at the annual meeting:
before handing in his resignation : “There are plenty of
men who attend the annual meeting and participate in
the election of officers, and who are ever ready to take
part in a club race, but who will not pay their subscrip-
tions Among these are committeemen and officers....
For the ensuing year members want to be very particular
about their committee. What is wanted are men who
will work for the good of the club . . . and not shirk their
responsibilities by leaving things in the hands of the
secretary. Now as to the secretary- In choosing your
secretary you want to be very particular. So far as this
club is concerned, the secretary wants to be a man with
plenty of time at his disposal; a man who doesn t object
to being made a convenience of ; and last and not least, an
accomplished ‘ smoodger.’... The ba11,., was financially a
failure, .. . due to the apathy of the members. All the
work, . . . worry, . . . and none of the credit fell to Mr.
and myself. ... At the close of the ball the only club
member, responsible or otherwise, on the premises was
myself.” Then he ran on to deal with finance and rub a
few more enemies in the gutter, and finally handed in his
resignation, and sternly refused to consider a request of
the club that he would allow himself to be appointed for
another term. A good curse like this from a secretary is
very refreshing these degenerate days.

chance to win his “ blue” in English ’Varsity football.

From England comes the information that the
simultaneous visit of two Australian football teamsps

but the desire to play cut-throat was too strong, and
wisdom went overboard.
The very latest lunacy in Maoriland is a
“ Marathon ’’ race. It will be held some time in March
next, at Auckland, and the distance will be just over 13
miles. Now, what’s the sense in this so-called Mara-
thon” business, anyhow? The genuine and only Mara-
thon Race is held every four years, over a distance of
about 27 miles, in connection with the Olympic Games,
and is a great event because it brings a runner into com-
petition with the whole world ; but for every little one-
eved athletic club on the planet to rush round madly
and promote races over short distances of from Six
to 13 miles, and to label them Marathon,
is ludicrous in the extreme. No one of the
slightest importance will be interested, elevated or
amused bv the fearful spectacle of a few broken-
winded and perspiring young men, arrayed in bathing
costumes and blushes, struggling through the streets of
Auckland, in the final stages of a scramble to secure a
silver wreath of great value” (to quote the prospectus
put out bv the organisers of the disturbance). This Mara-
thon ” mania is on a par with the “walk lunacy that
was so prevalent in Australia and Maoriland about five
vears ago, and which was an offshoot of the London
Stock Exchange walk. The difference, however, is that
only trained athletes are to compete in the “ Marathon
whereas anyone could undertake the ‘ walk, the result
that a number of ancient cripples, who should have
been supporting themselves on crutches, were seized by
the pedestrian craze, and nearly died on the road.

The N.S.W. police are still toiling over
two mysteries—the fatal bashing by night of Fie
Kee Yang, who was found oh December 1, a
perjshed Chinaman on the floor of his shop in a
Sydney back street; and the cutting of the throat
of Hannah Kelly, the Armidale servant girl, who
was unaccountably slaughtered in her bed, in
the early morning, a few days later. As
regards Fie Kee Yang the law seems to be up
against the dead wall of the inscrutability of Asia.
There is even a difficulty about locating the de-
ceased’s identity; he had various names, as so
many Chows have, and the witnesses seem also to
have various names apiece, and they alb look
alike, except the corpse, and they all appear to
know really nothing worth mentioning. And as
regards Hannah Kelly there is a vague report
that a man was seen running a few yards or a
mile or two from the scene of the tragedy ; he was
either running away from the hotel or towards it;
and he transpired either a little before the murder
or a little after it, or else at the exact moment of
the episode. It may even turn out upon further
investigation that he was seen running in an adja-
cent town the previous week. The whole story is
sketchy and unfinished to the last degree.
Various people are already to the front
with names for the Federal capital, which is fixed
on paper, and will probably soon be unfixed in
reality. “Edward” and “Ed ward ia *' are two
of the clumsy propositions already to hand. A
name will also be wanted for the Federal port on
•Tervis Bay. The Bulletin suggest Hughes Port,
just to lemind Mr. Hughes’ waterside consti-
tuents that their representative toiled for all he
was worth to get the Federal port established
only 87 miles from Sydney, where it must ulti-
mately intercept all that city’s southern export
trade, instead of at Twofold Bay, 300 miles away,
where it would gather a new trade of its own
from Monaro and East Gippsland.

Interstate Mdil
Steamship Lines
HEIST MAS
having arrived,
with its due k,
turkey, heavy
pudding, dyspep-
sia, and high
temperatures, it becomes
necessary to put in a word for the
Five Companies which drag people
around Australia and drag them back again.
Now, as a matter of fact, though people will sniff
incredulously, the quintet of shipping companies
becomes of imperative necessity to the intending
traveller at the Christmas season, seeing that, if
he wants to go away, he elects, in a number of
cases, for a voyage by sea. That solemn fact is
indisputed. People, in truth, generally save up
during the year for a “trip abroad,” since,
to the bulk of them, this rather common
Christian period of festival is, apart from
its incredible pudding and holly and things,
the annual holiday time. And, accepting the
time in its true and antique sense of “holy day
—enjoying it holily—that is, of course, festively,
but sanely and recreatively, it is not surprising
that the Five Companies pouch from them many
a steamer fare. On board a steamer that is push-
ing along between Melbourne and Sydney, or
between Adelaide and Melbourne, there is indeed
everything that suggests festivity with sanity and
recreation. The heat, which often at Christmas
cliase3 people into few clothes and a passion for
temperance drinks on land, hasn t nearly such a
deadly potency at sea. It’s bite is tempered by a
cool sea wind, and the uncomfortable effects
which it produces of plain man steaming and
vaporising are reduced to a minimum, if not
entirely removed. Even the pudding—the inevit-
able penance for all of those who in Australia
are English before they are sensible—doesn’t
seem the same sort of pudding at sea. On
land, where it is ravaged by fat people at a
temperature of 104°, and is slowly con-
sumed by a mass of simmering and
olefiant lady or gentleman in a state
of acute strangulation, it isn’t a
convincing argument for the
Christmas which every well
brought up person spends
more or less dutifully. But,
at sea, the dark brown food,
which is always a . monu-
ment of mystery and patient
labor, ia a very different
matter. Somehow, its ironclad characteristics and
leaden heaviness have assumed a lightness, a fairy-
like airiness. But it is not a change in substance—it
is the same portentous comestible—it is the change
in its surroundings ! If the heat is inclined to be
clamorous—which is rare—there are electric fans
in the saloon ; there is cold liquid all round ; and
every vestige of cool breeze to be had is floating
through the open ports. Under such conditions,
even the sorrowful and penitential concealment of
a tremendously over-estimated bit of nourishment
changes from a popular evil to a something ap-
proaching delight. Now, could such a contrast
between sea and land be fairer or more trium-
phant ?
Now, if there is anything that the prospective
holiday-maker at Christmas resents it is the
possibility of ceremonial while on holiday—of, in
fact, making himself or herself a sort of rigid
religious service in a lot of pretentious clothes.
The ordinary man and woman—and by them are
meant the modest, sensible people which even
every sporting community can produce at a pinch
—wants to be left very much alone in his or her
clothing arrangements while on holiday. Any-
thing does—that is to say, anything that is sub-
stantial and of superior extent to bathing cos-
tumes, fur waistlets, or glass beads, is not censur-
able, because the spirit of holiday must be liberty
without licence in a lot of things that in other cir-
cumstances are strongly and properly regarded.
And it is just on that highly-important point
of dress that life on a steamship has some appreci-
able advantages over that on land. The dif-
ference between the two is not obvious until you
come to think of it—for, generally speaking, holi-
day attire is generally unconventional and careless
wherever it may be. But the point of difference
between what is de rigueur on a steamship and on
land lies in the fact that no one is ever expected
to be a swell on an inter-State steamship, while,
at, say, any popular land resort, one sometimes is.
It is the difference between option and compara-
tive compulsion, and a very solid difference it is !
Thus, for that reason alone, a trip by one of the
steamers of the Five Companies is to be recom-
mended. They don’t ask a person to be attired as
if for a coronation. They leave it to the passen-
ger’s good sense to dress as he or she thinks fit, so
long as he or she doesn’t incline to questionable
eccentricity or foolishness. On the other hand,
at many a fashionable watering place or inland
resort, a person who is not prepared to be impres-
sive, when he or she ought to be indulging in a
sort of sartorial vagabondage, is rather looked
upon as a shocking person, who is very much out
of place. Yet it must not be assumed that any-
one cannot be a toffy perfection on board one of
the steamers afore-mentioned. He or she can, if he
or she likes. But also he or she can just idle
about in the simplest and plainest things, if he or
she likes. That is one of the virtues of a trip by
sea.
CS?
But by far the largest consideration to the
thoughtful passenger, who contemplates a run
round to one of the capitals, are the recuperative
and health-giving possibilities of the voyage. One
does not understand what air is until one gets to
sea, away from the curious, lively, and almost
tangible mixture that does dutyfor atmosphere in a
big city. In a big city, or even in sparsely-inhabited
spots, the air has got to make room for variously-
assorted effluvia, germs, dirt, grit, bluestone, and
other unsuspected substances; whereas, out at
sea, the germs, effluvia, etc., being essentially
land dwellers, drop out of the huge masses of
breath by their superior weight and get drowned
in the coastal seas before they have any chance
of getting further out, to prove a nuisance
to a passing steamer. As a matter of
fact, such things are not met with out at
sea. The air is so pure that it isn’t noticed.
The passenger, who is idling about the deck in
informal raiment, or reading, or spilling tempestu-
ous gallantries to a nice girl in a nook, doesn’t
think of it—or, if he does, imagines he is breathing
vacuum, and an unflavored one at that. Taking
that point into consideration, and also the facts
that there is only restriction to the distance one
may roam, and no restriction to gorgeous eating
and loafing, and even to flirting—if one is young
and ambitious—the intending holiday-maker can-
not but recognise the superiority of a short sea-
trip over a long land one, for instance. Granted
that the land holiday provides a greater selection
of girl and diversions—yet it is
still inferior to the sea one,
by reason of the sea’s
superior health-giving
benefits. If any per- -
son doesn’t believe
it, let him or her
try a sea-trip by
proceeding, as a
first step, to the
offices of the Five.
Companies for par-
ticulars of Christmas
fares.

OBJECTS.
THE company ia being formed with the object of purchasing from the Fibrous Deposit Company
Proprietary Limited the option it holds of purchasing on or before the 31st January, 1909, for
£17,500, the rights of Paris Nesbit, Alexander John M‘Lachlan, and John Mellis Napier,
under Permits Nos. 446, 447, and 448, from the Commissioner of Crown Lands of the Govern-
ment of South Australia to remove fibrous deposits.
The Permits are for one year, from Ist June, 1908, with right of renewal for twenty years on the
following conditions:—
(a) That annual payment be made to the Government of 2J per cent, royalty on actual value of
fibre raised during year.
(b) That area be reasonably worked to the satisfaction of the Honorable the Commissioner of
Crown Lands.
(c) That not less than two men be continuously employed, or that not less than £lOO be expended
during each year in obtaining or preparing fibre from such area, or in work in connection
therewith.
(d) That in working area, navigation, or the use of the foreshore for other purposes, must not be
interfered with or impeded.
Each Permit is renewable on payment before expiry of each year.of the sum of £25, provided that
all the conditions have been satisfactorily complied with.
The proposed Company is to win, work, and make marketable the fibrous deposit mentioned in
the abovementioned permits.
The reports of Captain James A. Mitchell, 9 Queen-street, Melbourne, master mariner; Mr.
Walter Gaunt, Alfred Woollen Mills, Williamstown; and Messrs. Fyvie and Stewart, 34 Queen-
street, Melbourne, engineers, convey to intending shareholders an idea of the quantity and value of
the deposit of fibre, of the uses to which it can be put, of the estimated cost of plant required, and
the estimated cost of recovery. '
The main facts to be gathered from the said reports are as follows:

13ft., at an average depth of 4ft. lOin. under water.

The fibre will spin and weave in union with wool, and will also take the dye evenly with it, being,
so far as is known, the only vegetable fibre to do this. Further, being practically non-inflammable
(its charring point being 373 deg. F.), it is to that extent a non-conductor, unlike cotton ; also it does
not shrink. It seems, therefore, that its chief and most remunerative demand will arise for textile
purposes, partly due to a combination of above facts ; and the value of 3d. per lb., mentioned in Mr.
Gaunt’s report, does not appear extravagant, as it will favorably compete as to price with the short
staple cottons, which, it is understood, are worth at least 4.(d. per lb. at present, with the additional
advantages of .taking the wool dye and retaining the heat, neither of which attributes cotton possesses.
With regard to woollen shoddy, of which over 113,000,0001 b. were imported into Great Britain in
1907 [vide Dalgety’s Wool Review, September. 1908), it is far superior to the lower grades, and more
than equal in many respects to the higher grades, the former values being from 3d. to 4d. per lb., and
the latter ranging up to 6d. per lb., and at the same time it will form a union with either or both. In
addition to'the further uses to which the fibre may be put, reflected in Mr. Gaunt’s report— viz. , Low
Carpets, Coir Matting, Hessian, Bagging, and probably Woolpacks—it has been demonstrated that
it is useful for stuffing or upholstery purposes, competing with flock and kapok. The market price of
the former is approximately £l6 per ton, and the latter sd. per lb. f.o.b. Java.
From the foregoing it will be seen that the vendor believes that the permits are not only exceeds
ingly valuable, but that the possibilities are almost unlimited. The world’s production of cotton for
1905-6 (the latest date available) was 18,125,000 bales of 5001 b. each. The production in the United
States alone for the year 1906-7 was 13.273,809 bales of 6001 b. each, valued at 640,311,538 dollars, whilst
the imports of kapok into the Commonwealth for 1907 were valued at £84,000. The various materials
that have been manufactured from the fibre in union with wool, as also the material in its various
stages of manufacture with wools of various descriptions, and also by itself, dyed and undyed, may
be seen at the office of the vendor company, 31 Queen-street, Melbourne. The fibre is the result of
the sheddings of the leaf sheaths of a sea-grass, botanically known as Posidonia Australis, found in
Spencer Gulf, South Australia, where the sheddings have been imprisoned by the action of wind
and waves in the sand flats referred to in Captain James A. Mitchell’s report.

Dear Bulletin, —In reference to the Labor can-
didates who were defeated bv Bent’s comic
Ministers, you speak of one (B. 26/11/’08) as be-
longing to “a party free from suspicion of jobbery
and bribery.” As a supporter of Labor principles
since I was that high, I only wish your assertion
were true. While the candidate you refer to is
all you suggest, we, who know the party, are
not so sure about the political company he
keeps—or, rather, is forced into keeping
by the exigencies of party organisation.
As the only party with honest principles, the
Labor party should be the only party with wholly
honest exponents. Unfortunately, it is no use for
the supporters of Labor to blind their eyes to the
obvious shortcomings of some of those who pro-
fess the same views. No doubt it is a splendid
advertisement for a party to be able to announce
that it is the only one whose members and organ-
isers are pure and lily-white, but when the
moral smudginess of some may at any moment be
exposed by a smart Tory secretary, the pretence
becomes a tactical blunder as well as a crime.
The decent men in the Labor movement are
foolish to let it run the risk of discredit by
enduring the presence of these characters. The
Fat Minister of the Crown who is proved guilty
of taking bribes is promptly repudiated by his
party, and the subject is dropped ; but from the
time anything of the sort is proved against a
Labor man, the incident will never be allowed to
be forgotten, and it will be held up as an example
of the consequences of putting “persons of that
class” in positions of authority.—A.B.
This comes from the State of the bloated Western-
District sheep-walk :
I wandered down into the Otway forest (Vic.) last week
with a friend, who was going to look at some blocks
recently thrown open for selection. We went by train
from Oolac to a village called Beech Forest. It is a
narrow-gauge line with fearful twists in it, so that often
the guard in the van can shake hands with the engine-
driver. Fr®m Beech Forest we rode along a corduroy
road for about 14 miles. There are settlers along this
road, and their cream is collected by the Beech Forest
factory cart. After we left the farms we struck some
nerve-trying country. Some of the blocks open for selec-
tion were not bad, but lit was a circus trick to get to
them. In this dreadful country, unless you have a cor-
duroy track made of logs laid along a ridge, there is no
chance of getting a vehicle in or out of the place alive. My
selecting pal was pretty hardy, but he began to waver
when an Out-back selector told him how he got his cream
out. He showed us two cans which were fiat on one side,
and fitted with straps. These were fixed on a strong
horse (the selector insisted on the adjective), which
was led up the precipices. We saw hills that
would, I am sure, beat the strongest horse;
but the resident selector assured us it was won-
derful what a horse (providing he was strong ) could
cart up a hill on his back. The same horse had to bring
the flour, sugar, and tea back, mostly down the hills this
time ; and he had to be a smart horse then, or else the
bag of flour would get on top of him and smother him.
It was all very interesting, but I noticed that my friend
was not nearly so amused at it all as I was. And now I
think I was very easily amused, for it is really tragic ;
and the men who drag the packhorse up and down those
accursed hills are heroes. But when we got back to the
Beech Forest pub that night they told us that about 80
intending selectors had already been out to look at the
blocks. Still, says the Argus, the demand for land is
being adequately* met without any revolutionary prac-
tices. I wish I had the Argus editor packing cream out
in the Otway for a week. His language, anyway, if not
his practice, would be revolutionary.
“K.”: Speaking at Berry (N.S.W.) Minister
Perry is reported to have said :
If the children had to turn out early and milk cows,
where was the harm ?
I have lived for 20 years in a dairying district, and
I am so astonished at Perry’s query that I can
hardly answer him. Does Perry know the physi-
cal strength needed to milk an average cow in
spring-time? Does he know that the milking
of from 15 to 20 of such cows is regarded as hard
and heavy work for a man ? Does he know that
children, both boys and girls, often milk eight to
10, and sometimes more, of such cows ? If Perry
saw a girl of from 8 to 12 working, from daylight,
for three hours with a navvy’s shovel, he would (I
hope) feel indignant. Well, I can assure him (if
he has never tried it) that milking cows just now
is heavy, back-breaking and hand-straining work.
I would like Perry to look at the wrists and hands
of these youngsters after milking; and I invite
him to ask some of them how long it takes them
to get to sleep at night owing to the aching of
their arms. Also, he might inquire how many of
the youngsters have to bathe their hands in hot
(very hot) water in the morning before they can
shut their fingers close enough to grab the cows’
teats once more. Minister Perry wants to know
where the harm is. lam writing from Victoria,
but I know that the same conditions occur very
close to where he was speaking, and, it he is care-
ful to get hold of the youngsters when their heart-
less parents are not within earshot, he will soon
have his ignorance enlightened.
+
The law of N.S.W. has a curious conviction that,
while the captain of a sea-going tug of 100 or more
tons requires sleep like any other human being,
the captain of a sea-going tug of 99£ tons can keep
awake all the time. This conviction leads to
astonishing results. The master of the 100-ton
vessel has a certificated mate, and can legally
leave the vessel in his charge. The master of the
vessel has no certificated mate, and if he
wants to sleep he can only leave his vessel in the
unlawful charge of a deck-hand, and have a
snooze contrary to the letter‘and spirit of the
Act; and if the deck-hand brings the craft to
grief, the law is shocked at the misconduct of the
master in not keeping awake all round the clock,
and hangs up his certificate like the Israelite’s
harp on the willow-tree near Babylon. Treasurer
Waddell stated in the Assembly, the other
night, that the Government had this matter
“ under consideration.” Perhaps if the Govern-
ment was required to keep awake until it arrived
at a decision it would get a better understanding
of the position of the skipper of the 99|-ton vessel
whom the statute expects to remain alert from
January to December.
+
Australia’s Foreign-trade press, which has been
asserting doggedly for the past year that the
British cry for “Tariff Reform” is an election
bogey, has to put up with some severe knocks
these times. The latest is supplied by the British
Board of Trade returns recently to hand. They
show that for the month of July last there was a
decrease in the foreign trade of the United
Kingdom of £12,181,345, compared with July,
1907? Says the Blue Book
The total decrease of exports in July is caused almost
entirely by a decline of £6,106,466 in manufactured
goods. The following are the amounts of the decreases
in the principal classes of manufactured exports:—
Iron and steel .. .. .. .. £1,170,629
Other metals .. .. .. . • 232,036
Machinery .. •• •• •• 328,135
Cottons 2,382,340
Woollens •• 770,270
Other textiles 529,666
Cutlery 127,701
Apparel 166,519
The decline for the first seven months of 1908, as
compared with a similar period of 1907, is given
as £58,503,338.

“10-2-4” on the subject of a Maligned
Point: —
The time has arrived, in my opinion, for the journals
which scarify our Sydney nobility to drop localising 1 the said
nobles at Potts Point, using the name “ Mrs. Potts
Poynt” as a synonym’for ml that is haughty and pluto-
cratic in the female line, and so forth. I live at Potts
Point myself, and I am neither noble nor a plutocrat.
Nor, so far as I can judge, is any one of my neighbors.
Time was when things were otherwise. But it is long
years agone. Fashion and wealth have moved eastwards.
As a result of careful scrutiny I have come to the con-
clusion that Potts Point is mainly inhabited in the
present year of grace by two classes of the community—
(l) lodging-house keepers, (2) lodgers. The lodgers are
mostly clerks. They subdivide naturally into (a) Jews,
(b) Christians—there being no Mohammedan clerks.
They are passionate supporters of Dan Levy, M.L.A., to a
man. They believe that Socialism will cause something
to burst in the near ffcture ; also that Fisher, M.H.R., is
an Anarchist, or something. They fear that he and his
myrmidons have designs on their several and individual
30s. a week. Fisher, Deakin, Watson, Lyne and the other
Anarchists are lashed by them very severely at times in
the matutinal ’bus and trams that ply between the
Point and the marts of commerce. But there is
no social significance in this. The lashers merely
repeat to one another what they read in the morning
paper. I do not know any of them, but they look
to me simple and unassuming fellows. If they are Jews,
and youngish, they bear a strong family resemblance to
the famous Mr. Alexander —he who wears the p.oud
style of “ most beautiful man in Sydney.” Their hair is
shiny and black ; their clothes are also shiny, and very,
very neat; their boots are shiny, their faces are shiny,
everything about them is shiny. They glimmer most
gorgeously in the sunlight. If they are Jews and old
they look as Mr. Alexander will look when cruel, un-
®sthetic old Time has imposed a defacing flapper on his,
at present, lovely brow. If they are Christians they look
—young and old—like ordinary or hedge clerks. No ; there
is no suggestion of the ancient regime about these
modern Potts Pointers. In the still calm of the evening
they may be seen on the balconies of their landladies,
resting after the -day’s toil. They do not hurl them-
selves into evening clothes and embark on a wild round
of hilarious frivolity. They do not play Bridge for un-
heard-of or other stakes. Not at all. They just sit there
in slippered ease, smoking grim-looking cigars, till a
southerly springs up ; then they go inside, and go shortly
afterwards to bed. The females of the locality are the
wives, mothers, sisters, etc., of the men (or gents, as it
is the local convention to term them), and they look it.
As a resident of this maligned portion of Sydney, I am in
hopes that, in Heaven’s good time, if not earlier, the
reproach under which it now labors of harboring none
but offensively intolerant people, with blue blood and
money, may be removed from it. If these folk exist any-
where, the social sleuth must seek them miles further along
the YVatson’s Bay tram-line. “ Potts Point ”as a pseu-
donym for the indigenous Colonna or de Vere is as un-
truthful as an Anti-Sosh oonferencer.

Heart Failure.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
THERE’S a somethin’ in the distance goin’
tramp ! tramp ! tramp 1
Far, far away, yet very clear and loud,
And the damnable persistence of its tramp!
,! tramp! tramp !
Has a flavor of the coffin and the shroud.
A sick bed such as this on which I wait, wait,
wait,
All thro’ the day and the twilight and the dark,
What is it but a prison, where a fellow’s bound to
listen
To the marchin’ time he cannot choose but mark?
It sounds like folks with banners, and a drum,
drum, drum,
Them that talk religion are biddin’ me rejoice ;
They tell me ’twould be manners to be singin’ out
hosanners,
But I haven’t got the spirit or the voice.
They say I’d better turn me unto Gawd, Gawd,
Gawd,
But I’m so mortal tired, and He’s so far away.
I can’t seem to concern me with the things they
try to learn me,
And the feet are marchin’ nearer every day.
They get too jolly nigh me with their beat! beat!
beat!
Near and ever nearer at the turnin’ of the night,
Till the silence gets to try me and there’s ne’er
a body by me,
And I lie there like a baby in a fright.
If they’d only take a spell now from their drum,
drum, drum !
Give a fellow time to breathe, and let his trouble
slip,
By gob,. I’d soon be well now, but they won’t;
and why to Hell now
Should they want to make a fellow lose his grip ?
I don’t half like to bend up, for I’ve always kept
my end up,
Never lay and squealed like this, but took my
bit o’ fun,
And if only I were stronger, well, I’d stick it a bit
longer,
But I’m fear’d I got to own as how I’m done.
I’ve used up all my fuel, and the pressure’s failin’
lower,
The life of me’s a-drainin’ off by drops;
The ma,rchin’s somethin’ cruel, but it’s slower,
slower, slower,
There it goes—goes —goes, —now —it—stops.
'QUEENSLAND.
A.W.
Coaching.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
who have been used to
I macadamised highways can’t readily ac-
commodate themselves to the altered con-
ditions pertaining to the back country, where
theje are only little patches of made roads, mostly-
beside the wayside pubs (where the publicans
don’t want them), no bridges or culverts across
the creeks, and the distance from house to house
ranges from 20 to 50 miles. The bush track winds
like a serpent across the hills and through the
forests of mulga and gidgee, whilst over the soft
sandbeds the tracks are as manifold as the beds
and billabongs of an inland river, showing the
many deviations made by drivers in their desire
to save their horses. The latter, fed mostly on
scrub and saltbush, are not always in the pink of
condition, and the long stages, ranging up to 36
miles, together with the immense loads that are
piled on the vehicles, don’t assist the animals in
keeping their ribs hidden. A team is often made
up of anything handy —little and big, light and
heavy, and it may represent several owners.
Settlers give the coach-driver their half-broken
colts to quieten ; and when a man has a horse
that kicks, bolts, jibs, or displays other peculiari-
ties that the owner objects to, the remedy is a few
trips in the body of the coach team. These casual
horses may also be one-eyed, near-sighted or pur-
blind, but the driver is glad to have their help.
I often heard admiring comments from visitors
on the horses that came spanking into Broken
Hill with heavy loads from north and west.
These were the show horses. They had good
roads to travel on, and had to cut out their stage
in record time to make up for the deficiencies of
relays further out. Mail-time is reckoned by
minutes in Broken Hill ; but when you inquire at
the little places towards the border as to what
time last week’s Bulletin will arrive, the answer
is: “ Some time this afternoon, or to-night, or
to-morrow.” A few points of rain on the track,
though it would not stop a Chinaman from water-
ing his cabbages, may delay the mail 48 hours.
Tibooburra is advised by wire what time the
coach leaves Milparinka, 24 miles distant; yet
the people cannot tell within two or three hours,
in average weather, when it will arrive. “ Heavy
load ” means late arrival, “ light load ” early.
Sitting behind knocked-up horses, on a hot
summer’s day, with dust and flies for an accom-
paniment to the creaking of wheels and the rock-
ing of a crawling coach, is an experience that the
Far-back traveller can look forward to without fear
of disappointment. Sometimes, to avoid being
stuck up, he has to walk over the bad places, and
even dig away the sand and mud with a shovel,
and spoke the wheels. One western line traversed
many heavy sandbeds. On coming to one of
these, the driver would call out, “ Now, then, out
you get! ” and women and all would have to step
out and tramp until the horses were able to trot
again. But the westerners are used to that sort
of thing, and willingly walk and graft a lot of the
way over which they’ve paid anything from 6d.
to 9d. a mile for the luxury of riding.
The commercial traveller is an objectionable
passenger to a coach-driver at such times. His
tin trunks are numerous, and their weight would
knock up a mule team. He is generally a heavy
person, too, and he doesn’t like stepping down
into a quagmire. When it is necessary to open a
gate, or to hook a trace-chain that has come
undone, he takes a tighter grip of his cigar and
holds out his hand for the reins. But the com-
mercial is a good fellow. He has always a fund
of anecdote, and is usually provided with some-
thing in a bottle to refresh the driver after a try-
ing experience. He pays liberally for his ride,
and he rides all the way.
On the 200-mile journey between Tibooburra
and Broken Hill, I and others, after sitting
cramped up, numbed shivering for hours on ]
a winter’s night, have got out and walked hve or
six miles to tlie mail change for a cup of tea, an
have then enjoyed an hour or more s sleep before
the coach turned up. By this I do not Wis
infer that the general run of Out-back coaches a
slow affairs; but over portions of the rougn
tracks it isn’t possible for them to travel at tul
walk, much less at full .trot. Though the casual
passenger grumbles at having to get along >y
his own volition, the wea,ry whip, tied to nis
worn-out team, envies him his freedom, and
wishes that he could walk away likewise, ine
exigencies of his calling require him to be on the
box for 50 hours at a stretch, with only a day or
two and at one end only a night’s rest between
trips. Winter and summer, sunshine and rain,
he works all day and all night, and all next day
and right on through the night again, without a
spell, stopping only at the changes for fresh horses
and refreshments, and refreshments on this route
vary from eight to 18 hours apart.
Where there is a long interval between houses,
the “ change” may be simply a sapling yard and
a bough shed. The groom is mostly a single man,
living in a tent. His only work is to look after a
few horses, which run in the bush, groom them,
and help the driver with the changing on mail
days. The coach may pass only once or twice a
week, up and down, and between whiles he has a
pretty lonely time of it, seeing nobody but an odd
swagman. Where a selector’s hut is handy to the
road the selector acts as groom, and his wife earns
an honest penny by providing refreshments for
driver and passengers.
Besides these stopping places there are dozens
of bush post-offices along the road. These are
simply candle boxes, lolly tins, or kerosene tins,
nailed to trees and gate posts. Hollow trees j
are also used, a small piece being cut out the
trunk, and a tin awning nailed above it. Such
receptacles are usually convenient enough for the
driver to draw up alongside, and drop the mail in,
and take out anything that has been left for him.
They occur in places where no sign of life is
visible, in the heart of the scrub, on the edge of a
plain, and on thickly-timbered hills. At other
places a horseman, or a girl, or a black boy will
be waiting, sometimes with parcels which may be
anything from a cabbage to a box of eggs. At
night time the tired passenger, after sitting and
rocking through hours of monotony, occasionally
brightens up at the sight of a light ahead, and
inquires the name of the place. The driver tells
him the name of the squattage, or whatever may
be adjacent. When they pull up, the passenger
is disappointed to find only a man standing by the
roadside with a lantern, having ridden or walked
down to ascertain the whereabouts of a certain
bullock team, and how it was getting on, while
the place the driver had named is two miles or
more off the road. Swagmen’s fires, too, blaze out
here and there, and sometimes the men have a
message for the coachman, a letter to be posted,
or they want to know something about the track,
where water is to be found, the distance of places,
when shearing starts at Boulka Lake, and if
Bancannia has cut out yet. Maybe they only
want a pipe of tobacco, which, in the swagman’s
opinion, is sufficient cause to hold up the royal
mail.
There are receiving offices here and there—at a
wayside store, hotel, or mining camp. On mail
days people gather from all directions at these
places, the arrival of the coach being the most
important event of their lives. The orders and
messages a driver receives from them are many
and various. One man wants a pound of tobacco
from the store ; another wants a pair of boots or a
pair of pants ; a third hands him a bill with the
amount, and asks him to be good enough to get a
receipt; and a fourth wants his dog registered.
Mrs. Brown passes up a pair of ducks, and would
be obliged if he would give them to Mrs. Smith,
with her compliments, as he passes. Mrs. Public-
house wants him, like a good fellow, to get her a
cook or a housemaid in town. Another lady has
been making jam, and desires several jars of it
distributed among her friends along the track.
Also McPherson wishes him to ask Anderson for
the loan of his bay colt, and to bring it up along-
side his team when he is coming back. These are
a few of the innumerable favors which every
coachdriver on the Out-back roads is expected to
do for nothing. If he forgets one of them,
though it might be only a question concerning
the health of Mrs. Jones’ baby, he will be told
with sarcasm that “ he’s got a head and so has a
pin.”
We had anew experience at Packsaddle Bore,
where the road crosses a wide, sandy creek.
There had been rain in the neighborhood, so the
roads were heavy and creeks running. The up-
coach was met here—bogged in midstream. The
driver and a passenger were wading knee-deep in
water, one at the horses’ heads and one spoking,
while a Chinaman, bound for a border squattage,
handled the ribbons and shouted instructions
from the box. “Pullee gley orsee round more
better,” he cried. “Hit urn the black one; my
wor’, lazy blute. Gee up, ’orsee ! Gortam,
whaffor?” We halted on the bank, and our
leaders were transferred to the bogged team. Our
amiable whip also stepped bare-legged into the
water, and'with their combined efforts the royal
mail was rescued from the bog.
Now came our turn. Paddy, the driver, rushed
them in so as to take the stiff part at a run.
Alas ! the pole-hook pulled out, and the leaders
dashed away with the reins, leaving us stranded
in the middle of the creek. Luckily the damage
was easily repaired, and there being plenty of
horses we got out of our difficulties after about an
hour’s delay.
The coach-driver bears a great deal more re-
sponsibility than he is given credit for. Think of
the many steep and nasty gullies crossed in the
dead of night; the deep gutters, begotten of old-
time wheel-tracks, that run parallel with the road
on the down grades, where the least swerve might
mean a capsize; the twistings through scrub,
where the track swings sharply round a stump or
tree, or round the foot of a rugged ridge with the
steep bank of a creek in juxtaposition, and you
get some idea of what you owe to his steady
hand, his keen eye, and his memory of the road,
as he bowls you on through the long night. Half
your time you can see nothing but a black bank
before you, for through the sand beds, over the
stony plains and the powdery, grassless flats, the
road is invisible. Yet the horses swing on with |
an unfaltering stride that instils within you a ,
sense of security. People whose avocations take
them over many roads know how to appreciate a
good whip ; they know what depends on the hand
that holds the reins.
On very dark nights, when the skies are
clouded, it occasionally happens that the road is
missed. On a barren plain below Wonnarninta,
where the track was hard to discern when starlit,
we got astray one night, and were two hours
searching with coach lamps and matches for the
track. Then there is always the dreaded north-
west dust-storm to be reckoned with. Driving
through one of these is worse than any night, for
at times nothing can be seen, and the dust-blinded
horses strive continually to turn from the blast.

PEARS
Matchless for the Complexion.
Once the Hungerford-Bourke coach w'as blown
over and dragged across the driver, who, however,
escaped without serious injury. During unusu-
ally heavy storms the team is turned tail to the
wind, and a halt is made until the worst has
blown over. Again, trees are blown across the
road, and a way has to be picked slowly round
through the timber.
When it is. mentioned that, besides drapery,
spirits and tobacco, such items as butter, fruit,
bacon, hams, meat, vegetables, Wool-sheets, tents,
tin dishes and boots, are sent through the parcels
post, you can imagine what the non-postal matter
is like. Every available inch of space is occupied,
bags of chaff, buggy wheels, perambulators, and
other bulky light stuff being piled on top to a
height of several feet. Then there are the
passengers, generally a mixed lot. Coming down,
you might have a Chinaman beside you, and a
couple of manacled prisoners, with a policeman on
either side, on the opposite seat. And there is no
room to stretch your legs or lean back; you are
compelled to sit huddled in one position mile
after mile, hour after hour. For this reason most
people prefer the box seat. The inside is cooler on
a hot day, warmer on a cold night, and drier in
wet weather ; but you are cramped, and there is
bound to be some old party alongside who can’t
keep awake, and w'ho persists in making a pillow
of his companion and shoring into his ear, or who
bumps him heavily with every roll of the coach.
It is a genuine pleasure to see the lights of a
town suddenly blaze out from the crest of a distant
hill. In the early morning the place looks asleep,
and during the day it has the dull sameness of
many other towns ; but in the early night, when
lights stream from everywhere, a little town looks
big.
The coach often travels through miles of burning
bush, enveloped in smoke and cinders, and run-
ning a hot race with long lines of flames that are
closing in on the road. On the Glen Wills line
(Vic.), in Feb., 1905, the mail coach and contents
were destroyed, and three horses and a swagman
—who had been picked up two miles back—were
burnt to death. The coach had been traversing a
cutting on the side of a hill, on which a fire was
burning, when the wind sprang up suddenly and
the flames leaped over the road in a broad sheet
and completely enveloped it. The driver, who
was knocked overboard and severely injured,
escaped by crawling- into Lightning Creek.
And floods—the Backblocks flood is an uncertain
and treacherous element. One visitation may be
the cause of forming a hard, level crossing ; whilst
with the next a washaway may occur, and the
unsuspecting driver goes into it, his horses are
drowned, his coach wrecked, and mails and bag-
gage are distributed over miles of country. I saw
one such disaster on the Mount Browne route a
few years ago. Four horses, I think, were
drowned, and the driver only escaped after a very
severe struggle.
Other and worse accidents happen at times, no
matter how careful and steady a driver may be,
as witness the smash on the Tarcoola (S.A.) track
in October, 1902. The horses bolted near Wilgena
station, and overturned the coach. The driver
sustained a fractured collarbone, fractured ribs
and severe contusions, and the one passenger, a
lady, had both legs broken. Both lay helpless by
the roadside until accidentally discovered by a
passing stockman. Probably the worst disaster
in the annals of Backblocks coaching was that
which overtook the Powell’s Greek and Anthony’s
Lagoon (N.T.) mail, when the driver, passengers
and horses perished on a dry stage for want of
water.
On the roads trending west from Bourkeand north
from Broken Hill it often happens in midsummer
that horses drop dead in harness from the excessive
heat, and the driver, if unable to proceed with his
weakened team, rides off to the nearest squattage
or to the next mail change for fresh horses, while
the passengers guard the coach and mails, and
beguile the time the best way they can. While
coming into Tibooburra one night a horse dropped
from exhaustion, and the coach was pulled on to
it before the team could be stopped. The coo-ees
of the driver attracted a dozen of us to the scene,
and after lifting the coach off the prostrate animal,
and disentangling the others from the broken pole,
we took hold fore and aft, and rolled her on to the
post-office. This, however, might be considered a
good finish, when compared with the experience
of a North Queensland mailman, who, when his
horses caved in 40 miles from Winton, carried the
mailbags the rest of the way on his back, the
journey occupying him two days.
These are some of the experiences of coaching
in the tame times of the present. They were
more exhilarating in the pioneering days of Gobb
and Cos., when the intrepid whips had to run the
gauntlet of armed bushrangers, and often submit
to being lined up with their passengers by the
roadside while the coach was being ransacked.
One of the famous whips of those days was
Edward Devine, commonly known as Cabbage-
tree Ned. From 1853 (when he was only -17) to
1862, Ned drove a six-in-hand between Geelong
and Ballarat. During the first two years his
wage was £l6-a week, and his tips from lucky
diggers, for whom he conveyed gold to the banks,
averaged even more than that. The roads at the
time were frequented by Captain Melville, Black
Douglass, and other bushrangers. Ned had many
narrow escapes, and on one occasion his coach
was stuck up, and his passengers robbed of £BOO,
by the notorious Ned Jordan, afterwards hanged
for the murder of Squatter Rutherford. Devine
got his sobriquet from the fact that he usually
sported a wide-brimmed cabbage-tree hat, made
iu Parramatta for the London International Ex-
hibition held in 1851, and subsequently presented
to him. It was a conspicuous part of his dress for
5.0 years.
EDARD S. SORENSON.
Before the Mast.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
IF you ever go to sea, travel in a deck-
cabin and make a friend of the stewardess.
Don’t go before the mast; that’s hurtful.
I know, because I’ve been there. Ever so many
years ago I went to sea in the American ship
Conqueror, Sydney to Hongkong, in ballast.
There was no comfort in it. I had to get up at
such preposterously early hours in the morning
that I blush to think of them even now. Till I
became used to it, sleep was almost impossible,
because of the. constant hubbub and curiosity of
the—er—poultry in the forecastle. When we got
outside the Heads, there was a swell of large .size
and sickening intensity, and the second officer
sent me up to the mizzen royal. To this day, I
don’t know what I was sent for; but I went.
The ship herself was a huge and ancient mistake.
We had to scrub' the floors of the heaving
beast ourselves, because the captain was a
hard and misogynic man, and we carried no house-
maids. I found it utterly impossible to get a cup of
tea abed o’ mornings, and I soon felt-tired. Tired !
I was tired of the food, I was tired of the second
mate’s continuous eloquence, I was tired of being
turned out of the poultry-yard in my thin night-
clothes to trifle with the mizzen-to’-gallan’ sail in
the teeth of a hurricane of ice. When we reached
Hongkong, crawling in amid the thick of a dull
wash of rain, I had had enough. I said I should go
ashore, even if I had to work. I did. I went to
the American consulate, where I was paid off by
a supercilious Japanese half-breed. I learned to
hate the Japanese early, and I rejoice to know
that I was not mistaken in my first impression.
But I got away from the Conqueror. Since which
time, as the advertisements say, I have taken no
other exercise on shipboard.
Of course, there were compensations. There
were, for instance, the deck concerts on velvet
equatorial nights, when the stars shone like
remote suspended pearls, and the whiffs of sul-
phurous odors that stole gently from aft and
trailed away to poison the gulls, told us that Mr,
Powis, our beloved second officer, was murmuring
in his sleep. One of our gallant crew had a voice
that I shall never forget—sort of silvery-tenor cut
low, with passementerie of bronchial wheezes and
an effective insertion of falsetto quags. There
was one song of his that always shook the stars tq
tears. The refrain comes stealing to me now
across the litter of old memories :
Ten tha’sand a year is my fortin,
And er l.vdy your daughter shall be ;
She shall ride in ’er kerridge an’ ’or-ses,
On ther green mossy benks er the Lee !
We would join strenuously in that splendid
chorus, till the Pleiades blinked with envy, and
the delighted second officer awoke and applauded
in a lava-torrent of unprintable objurgations.
Then there was another vocalist of our number,
w'ho let slip the fact, one night when off his guard,
that he had modestly refused a New York engage-
ment and the virgin affections of a great operatic
artiste. How well the scene comes back to mel
The manly figure of the young mariner, his
bronzed face thrown back, a shifting lump in his
left cheek, a bit of spunyarn saving the situation
in the figurehead of his honest but dilapidated
moles; the rugged, earnest, dirty, old familiar
faces of my shipmates; and all about us the
eternal, worried night ! And with all this, the
voice of the singer, strung to a tense and infinite
emotion—
Rahnd, rahnd, rahnd,
Rahnd goes ther wheel !
Tick-tack, tick-tock,
Loife is but er reel !
In joy or in sorrer,
In grief or in pine,
* Rahnd, rahnd, rahnd
Goes ther w’eel uv Toime !
Then there were the yarns, too ; the wonderful
crusted chestnuts and the newer inventions.
Stories of the “hungry Isaac” Webb; of the verit-
able sea-serpent that bit the Albyn’s bosun off her
cathead a day out from Callao ; of the lady, wife
of the Old Man of a certain barque, who loved
Paddy , (our champion liar), and how she walked
on the beach till he went ashore at Rio, and there
sweetly wooed him to her lord’s dishonor in such
language as probably accounted for Paddy’s pre-
mature baldness and general-depravity. “’Struth!
but she wus a wanner,” said Paddy. We be-
lieved it.
Ah me, it’s easy to jest; but when I think
of the Conqueror the torpid blood gets hot
within me, and I hate our cant and hypocrisy
more than Ido most times. The Conqueror was
a good ship, as Yankee windjammers go, and the
food aboard her w'as superior to the best average
of the British mercantile marine. But the public
conscience .that can condone the defects of a
commerce that degrades men to that life of dogs
is a conscience callous and vile. Talk of the
romance of the sea ! The romance of the sailor-
man is generally spelled out in linked abomina-
tions of cruelty and contumely. We send our
missionaries of sorts to convert the Hindu and the
Chinese ; but we have only a stray thought at odd
moments for. the whites condemned to the bitterest
heathendom on our deep-sea sailers. Some day,
perhaps, something may be done, and the profes-
sion that demands the valor and the skill of a.
million lonely men on every sea may be so safe-
guarded with humane conditions as to make the
life afloat the honorable and stimulating thing
that it is in the theory of our tinpot nautical
songs. But, in the meantime, things are vilely
bad.
But it will be understood wdiy I rarely or never
attempt to write nautical verse. I’ve been there.
And there are heaps of scribes that never w'ent to
sea.
FRANK MORTON.
Wreckage.
[FOR THE BULLETIN.]
Auckland, —Captain Gregory, o{ the steamer Wanaka,
which towed the missing ship Loch Lomond from Mel-
bourne to Newcastle, inspected some of the wreckage
found near Cape Maria. He says that the wreckage for-
warded to Auckland belonged to the Locli Lomond.—
News item.
THE wreckage comes with its deathly tale
From the lists of the Outer Sea,
The scornful gage of the knightly gale
’Neath the eyes of humanity.
Never again will she answer hail
Or come back to the sunlit quay !
The wreckage lifts on the racing wave
And drifts to the farthest shore,
A grisly token from out the grave,
It signs to the world : “No more !
She rests right well, the poor cargo slave,
On the pitying ocean’s floor ! ”
The wreckage, sodden and smashed and scarred,
Keepeth sullenly on its way
To where the lonely cape mounts guard
Over waters haunted and grey—
The felon waves that look fierce and hard
’Neath the prison-cold light of day.
The wreckage dips and the wreckage swings
’Midst the foam of the breakers’ wrath,
Whilst, soul-like, a screaming seagull wings
Ever restlessly back and forth,
And the mad South wind in madness flings ,
Giant seas at the hated North.
The upturned boat and the broken spar
waste of the insolent sea—
They travel slow but they travel far,
Whilst the days and the long hours flee,
Till the combers thunder on the bar
With the roar of Eternity.
Shattered and scarred on the wind-swept sands,
Battered and crushed on the reef,
So ends the work of their drowned hands
At the hands of the ocean thief.
Oh ! wise is the man who' tills his lands,
For the Sea hath naught but grief.
N.S.W.
E.O’F.
